11) Eurobonds

Previous Sample Questions- Eurobonds
1- Empirical evidence shows that US corporations realize abnormal gains from issuing Euro-debt. If this is true, why they issue only a small fraction relative to their domestic market, or why don't they raise more debt in the offshore markets? Discuss.
2- Why Eurobond market is limited?

Summary 1- Eurobonds
The Eurobond market provides opportunities for US corporations to raise capital offshore and potentially reduce their cost of capital while increasing shareholder wealth. The clientele hypothesis, as proposed by Kim and Stulz in 1988, states that firms can exploit their comparative advantage in providing securities that are in high demand by certain financial clienteles. This hypothesis suggests that, for a period of time, firms can borrow at lower costs in the Eurobond market compared to the domestic market, taking advantage of lower interest rates and catering to European investors who are willing to pay a premium for debt issued by US firms.
In a fully integrated market, there should be no differential cost of borrowing in different markets. However, imperfections exist in the worldwide markets, creating advantages for the Eurobond market. These advantages include bearer bonds that offer investor anonymity and are not subjected to withholding tax, which attracts investors seeking tax benefits and privacy. Additionally, investing in Eurobonds allows for risk diversification and is accessible only to well-credited companies, adding to the market demand for these securities.
US firms can benefit from raising capital offshore through Eurobond issuance, which can reduce their cost of capital and increase their overall value. When the demand for Eurobonds from foreign investors is high, they compete for these securities and drive up their prices, creating bargains for US firms. However, there are limitations to the Eurobond market. Only firms with a good credit market reputation can access this market successfully, as loose control mechanisms require high credibility. Excessive bond issuance, whether domestically or internationally, can harm a firm's creditworthiness, signaling trouble in the US market or an attempt to avoid US regulations.
The Eurobond market also presents some cost-related challenges. Contracting and enforcement costs are higher in this market compared to issuing domestic bonds. Additionally, issuing costs for Eurobonds are higher by 1-2% compared to domestic bonds. These higher costs, along with the limited accessibility and potential reputation risks, contribute to the Eurobond market's limitations.
It is important to note that the clientele hypothesis should have been called the catering effect, as mentioned by Doukas. Catering to investor demands is a short-term strategy, while the clientele effect is more permanent in nature.
The catering theory of dividends proposes simply that firms pay dividends when investors put a stock price premium on dividend payers. Thus, managers cater to investor demand, which means that firms follow the market trend in terms of whether or not to pay dividends, as Baker and Wurgler (2004) mentioned.

Overall, the Eurobond market offers potential benefits for US firms to raise capital offshore, reduce their cost of capital, and increase shareholder wealth. However, the limitations and considerations surrounding reputation, excessive issuance, and higher costs should be taken into account when deciding on the optimal debt issuance strategy.

Summary 2- Eurobonds

The Eurobond market offers advantages that create higher demand for its securities compared to the domestic bond market. The clientele hypothesis suggests that firms can increase shareholder wealth by catering to the preferences of certain financial clienteles (Kim & Stulz, 1988). This hypothesis implies that, for a certain period, firms can borrow at lower costs in the Eurobond market than in the domestic market, taking advantage of a lower interest rate. European investors act as the financial clientele in this scenario, willing to pay a premium for debt issued by U.S. firms. By issuing Eurobonds, firms can reduce their cost of capital and increase their overall value.
In a fully integrated market, there should be no differential cost of borrowing in different markets. However, imperfections exist in the worldwide markets, leading to advantages of the Eurobond market. These advantages include bearer bonds that provide investor privacy, as they are not registered; exemption from withholding tax, enabling tax evasion; risk diversification for investors; and accessibility only to well-credited companies with a good reputation (Kim & Stulz, 1988).
U.S. firms can benefit from raising capital offshore by issuing Eurobonds. When the demand for Eurobonds from foreign investors is high, these investors compete for the securities, driving up their prices and allowing firms to realize higher gains. However, the Eurobond market is limited in several ways. First, it is only accessible to firms with a good credit market reputation. The loose control in this market means that only firms with a high reputation can successfully issue Eurobonds and attract investors. Additionally, excessive bond issuance, whether domestic or international, has the potential to decrease a firm's creditworthiness. If a firm issues too much debt overseas, it may signal trouble in the
U.S. market or an attempt to avoid U.S. regulations. This could lead to a negative perception by investors and harm the firm's reputation. Moreover, Eurobonds are costlier to issue than domestic bonds, with higher contracting and enforcement costs and issuing costs (Kim & Stulz, 1988).
The cliental hypothesis and catering effect are often used interchangeably, but according to Doukas (1989), the cliental effect is permanent, while catering is short-term in nature. The Eurobond market provides temporary financing opportunities for firms with a reputation for low default risk. However, the paper by Kim and Stulz (1988) does not fully support the clientele hypothesis, as it primarily demonstrates that firms successfully timed a hot market.
To summarize, the Eurobond market offers advantages such as investor anonymity, tax benefits, and risk diversification, creating higher demand for its securities. U.S. firms can benefit by issuing Eurobonds, reducing their cost of capital and increasing value. However, the market is limited to firms with a good credit reputation, and excessive bond issuance can harm a firm's creditworthiness. Eurobonds also entail higher contracting and issuing costs. The cliental hypothesis and catering effect are related concepts, but

the clientele hypothesis primarily focuses on increasing shareholder wealth through catering to investor demands.

Summary 3- Eurobonds

The Eurobond market has advantages that make its securities more desirable than those in the domestic bond market. The clientele hypothesis suggests that firms can increase shareholder wealth by catering to the preferences of certain financial clienteles (Kim & Stulz, 1988). By issuing Eurobonds, firms can borrow at lower costs compared to the domestic market, taking advantage of a lower interest rate. European investors act as the financial clientele, willing to pay a premium for debt issued by U.S. firms. This enables firms to reduce their cost of capital and increase their overall value.
The Eurobond market offers advantages like bearer bonds that provide investor privacy, exemption from withholding tax, risk diversification for investors, and accessibility only to well-credited companies (Kim & Stulz, 1988). U.S. firms benefit from offshore capital raising through Eurobonds when there is high demand from foreign investors. This competition drives up bond prices and allows firms to realize higher gains. However, the market is limited to firms with a good credit reputation, and excessive bond issuance can harm a firm's creditworthiness. Eurobonds also come with higher contracting and issuing costs.
To summarize, the Eurobond market provides advantages such as investor anonymity, tax benefits, and risk diversification, leading to increased demand for its securities. U.S. firms can benefit from issuing Eurobonds, reducing their cost of capital and increasing their value. However, the market is limited to firms with a good credit reputation, and excessive bond issuance can harm creditworthiness. Eurobonds also involve higher contracting and issuing costs. The clienteles hypothesis focuses on increasing shareholder wealth through catering to investor demands (Kim & Stulz, 1988).

1) Endogeneity Problem

It is important to consider endogeneity in empirical studies, particularly when it comes to corporate finance and strategic management. In regression analysis, it occurs when explanatory variables are correlated with error terms, violating a key assumption. When endogeneity is not taken into account, empirical results can be biased and inconsistent, undermining their validity. Firms' decisions to diversify are influenced by a variety of factors, such as firm-specific characteristics and conditions in their industry. Endogeneity can result in biased estimates of the relationship between firm value and diversification in this case.
There are several econometric procedures that can be used to address endogeneity. The first step in determining the endogenous variables is identifying what caused their endogeneity, such as omitted variables, simultaneity bias, and measurement error. It is possible to address omitted variables by including relevant control variables. Simplicity bias can be mitigated through the identification of causal relationships and consideration of underlying mechanisms. Measurement error can be reduced by using more accurate measurements or appropriate proxy variables. Endogeneity can be addressed using instrumental variables (IVs) by finding variables that are correlated with the endogenous variable but not with the error term. A two-stage least squares regression is a common method for dealing with endogeneity, which involves estimating predicted values in the first stage before regressing the outcome variable on the predicted endogenous variable in the second stage.
Depending on the context and available data, alternative methods can also be employed. For self-selection bias, Heckman's correction model is often used, as are panel data methods for dealing with unobserved heterogeneity, and double difference estimators like Difference-in-Differences (DD). Also, avoid using proxies or use them correctly to reduce endogeneity issues.


2) Hedging Currency Risk
Whether corporate managers should hedge currency risk to protect shareholders' wealth is an ongoing debate in finance. Currency hedging advocates argue that companies with high exchange rate exposure, particularly those engaged in international operations, should hedge to mitigate their financial exchange risk (Jorion, 1990). For companies and shareholders, hedging currency risk is considered essential. According to Barkov and Bodnar (1994), previous studies may have produced ambiguous results due to bias in sample size and limited analysis of currency pairs. To provide more accurate results, they propose using lagged models and a broader range of currency pairs.
As a result of currency hedging, systematic risk can also be managed and cash flow can be stabilized during economic recessions (Doukas, 1995). The solution provides protection against economic shocks and mitigates the impact of global economic downturns. As well, currency hedging can increase a company's value by avoiding costly external funding (Allayannis & Weston, 2001). The combination of operational and financial hedging further reduces firms' risk exposures
On the other side of the debate, opponents argue that currency hedging can be costly and may not necessarily increase the firm's cash flow. Based on Modigliani and Miller's Modern Portfolio Theory (1968), investors would diversify away foreign exchange risk on their own and not pay a premium for firms that actively hedge. Managing hedges can result in conflicts of interest between managers and shareholders, as managers may make decisions based on their own interests rather than the interests of shareholders (Jensen & Meckling, 1976). Hedging decisions can be influenced by managers' own wealth and compensation incentives, resulting in agency costs (Smith & Stulz, 1985). Guay and Kothari (2003) find that derivatives usage in firms' risk management strategies is not significant compared to overall firm size and cash flows. While hedging can increase cash flows, it comes with costs such as transaction and translation costs. Companies can hedge financially or operationally, and value is added only if the benefits outweigh the costs.
In summary, corporate managers' decision whether to hedge currency risk depends on many factors, including the firm's exposure to currency risk, international activities, hedging costs and benefits, and alignment of managerial incentives with shareholder interests.


Delta and Vega
A manager with a high Delta, which represents their tendency to be sensitive to price changes, is likely to hedge more because of this exposure. They can reduce the risks associated with these changes and protect their positions by hedging. In contrast, managers with a higher Vega are less likely to hedge because they are more sensitive to changes in asset volatility. Volatility may be more attractive to them because it can possibly increase profits on their options positions.
According to Knopf et al. (2002), managers' stock and option portfolios correlate positively with their hedging activities. In firms where CEOs' portfolios are more sensitive to stock prices (higher Delta), derivatives are increasingly used for risk reduction. Conversely, hedging activities are negatively associated with stock option portfolios of managers whose Vega is higher. The higher the Delta, the higher the incentive to hedge, whereas the higher the Vega, the lower the incentive to hedge.



3) Stock Price Synchronicity

The movement of stock prices together is a common phenomenon in emerging markets. Several factors contribute to this high level of synchronicity. According to Morck et al. (2000), investors rely more on market-wide information rather than firm-specific information because they are aware of correlated market fundamentals, such as market size, country size, and economic diversification. Another factor contributing to synchronicity is poor property rights protection in low-income economies. Insufficient safeguards for private property rights discourage risk arbitrage and create opportunities for noise traders, while political events and rumors can trigger market-wide stock price swings (Morck et al., 2000).
In emerging markets, a lack of regulatory enforcement and low levels of voluntary disclosure also increase synchronicity. Many family or group-owned firms in these markets make it difficult to obtain detailed information, limiting the availability of firm-specific data. Consequently, analysts are forced to rely on stock market information more often, resulting in a higher degree of synchronicity in stock prices (Morck et al., 2000). When market-wide information is high and firm-specific information is low, investors struggle to differentiate between securities, resulting in more synchronized price movements (Piotroski & Roulstone, 2004). Stock prices are also synchronized in emerging markets due to greater analyst coverage. As analysts gather and disseminate information about firms, securities with more analyst coverage tend to incorporate a greater amount of market data (Chan & Hameed, 2006).
Compared to advanced economies, these factors suggest less efficient processing of economic information in emerging markets.



4) Hypothesis Development: CEO Overconfidence/Envy & Foreign M&As

This study examines the relationship between CEO envy and foreign mergers and acquisitions (M&A) through four hypotheses. Using data from the CRSP, Compustat databases, and ExecuComp database, the CEO envy level will be calculated as the difference between the CEO's salary and the median CEO's salary in the same industry.
The first hypothesis suggests that CEO envy influences M&A timing, focusing on later stages of M&A waves. The second hypothesis proposes that high CEO envy is associated with lower management quality, reflected in smaller Tobin's Q. The third hypothesis suggests negative abnormal stock returns for companies engaging in M&A due to CEO envy. The fourth hypothesis explores the mediation of corporate governance in the CEO envy-M&A relationship.
The study employs various methodologies, including difference-in-difference analysis, event study analysis, and propensity matching score technique, to test these hypotheses and offers additional considerations as robustness tests. The research aims to contribute to understanding the impact of CEO overconfidence on international M&A decisions.


5) Hypothesis Development: Brand Value/Recognition and The Firm Value

To test the impact of brand value on firm value, the following methodological procedure can be employed:
Data Collection:
* Obtain secondary data on brand value from consulting companies or brand valuation lists.
* Gather financial data such as market value, book value, and sales data from public sources or databases like Compustat, CRSP, Bloomberg, and SDC.
Hypothesis Development:
* Hypothesis 1: There is an exponential positive relationship between brand value and market-to-book ratio.
The efficient capital market model suggests that firms with stronger brands should have higher market-to-book ratios, indicating greater shareholder value.
Measurement:
* Calculate brand value by using appropriate methods provided by consulting firms or utilizing the brand valuation lists available.
* Compute the market-to-book ratio as the ratio of market value (share price multiplied by the number of shares) to book value.
* Estimate the financial value of the specific brand by dividing brand sales by the total firm's sales.
Analysis:
* Conduct a non-linear regression analysis to test the relationship between brand value and market-to-book ratio.
* Formulate the regression model as follows:
M/B Ratio = a + b(Brand Value)^a + error, where a and b are coefficients to be estimated.
Robustness Checks:
* Use alternative measures of brand value or evaluation to check for consistency of results.
* Conduct the analysis for specific industries or locations to examine if the relationship holds consistently across different contexts.
In summary, the methodology involves collecting brand value data, financial data, and market information. Hypothesis is developed to examine the relationship between brand value and market-to-book ratio.



6) Global Diversification and Industrial Diversification

According to Denis et al. (2002), globalization and industrial diversification can have a significant impact on firm value. They found that globally diversified firms trade at a discount compared to domestic firms operating in the same industries. This finding contradicts previous studies that suggest diversification can create value. For example, Kogut (1990) proposed that multinationalism creates value for the firm by introducing flexibility in the movement of resources. Doukas and Travlos (1988) found that shareholders of firms making acquisitions in new countries experienced positive abnormal returns. Kuppuswamy and Villalonga (2010) focused on industrial diversification during the financial crisis and found that it acted more like an insurance premium rather than a pure loss. Eun et al. (1996) indicated that cross-border takeovers generally result in synergy-creating activities and significant wealth gains for shareholders. Doukas and Lang (2003) demonstrated that geographic diversification increases shareholder value and improves long-term performance.
In Denis's study, methodological deficiencies were identified, such as measuring firm value and defining global diversification. It is possible that these deficiencies contributed to the contrasting results.
Doukas and Kan (2006) criticized the measurement used by Denis et al., arguing that it only considered changes in shareholder value and ignored bondholders' value. Denis et al. used excess values (EV), which measure the firm's actual value relative to its imputed value based on book value debt. Global diversification should be viewed from both perspectives when assessing its impact. Further, the definition of international diversification based on foreign sales may not capture the full extent of diversification, resulting in a misrepresentation of their effects on firm value.
There are methodological weaknesses in previous studies, and some scholars have found that international diversification enhances the value of firms using similar methodologies to Denis et al. The presence of endogeneity, such as poor-performing firms choosing to diversify or acquire low-value targets, should also be considered when interpreting the relationship between diversification and firm value.



7) Multinational Network and the Internalization Hypothesis

The difference between the multinational network hypothesis and the internalization hypothesis lies in their explanations for foreign corporate expansion. The multinational network hypothesis suggests that multinational corporations (MNCs) create value by expanding globally and utilizing their network of operations, while the internalization hypothesis proposed by Caves (1971), focuses on the firm-specific intangible assets that enable markets multinational firms to overcome challenges in foreign markets.
Recent patterns of foreign corporate expansion suggest that the multinational network hypothesis is more consistent with the observed trends. Tax considerations, non-intangible asset-driven expansion, and risk reduction perspectives support the multinational network hypothesis. Companies have relocated or expanded their operations outside their home countries for tax benefits, some firms expand without significant intangible assets, and risk reduction through international diversification is a key factor. The internalization hypothesis does not explicitly address these aspects.
Additionally, the concept of reverse internalization differs from internalization in that a firm does not possess intangible assets prior to expanding into foreign markets, and suggests that firms expand into foreign markets to obtain intangible assets, such as skills and knowledge-based resources, that can then be internalized in their home country (Eun, 1996).
While both hypotheses contribute to understanding foreign corporate expansion, the multinational network hypothesis provides a broader framework that aligns with recent patterns and motivations observed in the business environment.



8) Devaluation of the US Dollar

The decline of the US dollar against the Euro can be attributed to several factors. Firstly, the US trade deficit, resulting from importing more than exporting, reduces foreign investments and diminishes the demand for the US dollar. Additionally, the loose monetary policy of the US Federal Reserve, aimed at stimulating the economy, has led to inflation expectations and a decrease in the value of the dollar. Low interest rates in the US make investments less attractive, causing capital to flow out of the country, further reducing demand for the US dollar. The increasing use of other currencies for international transactions and the rapid growth of emerging economies also contribute to the depreciation of the US dollar. Finally, concerns about the US's deficits, weak recovery, and geopolitical risks have eroded confidence in the currency.
To protect against a potential future decline in the US dollar, companies can take various measures. They can diversify their currency holdings and investments across different currencies to reduce their reliance on the US dollar. Utilizing financial tools such as forward contracts or currency swaps can help hedge against exchange rate fluctuations. Companies can also focus on expanding their exports to take advantage of a weaker US dollar and manage costs effectively to increase operational efficiency. Considering partnerships or acquisitions in countries with stronger currencies can help offset the impact of currency fluctuations. By understanding these factors and implementing appropriate strategies, companies can safeguard their shareholder value from the potential depreciation of the US dollar.



9) Interest Rate Parity Condition

The concept of interest rate parity explains the relationship between interest rates and exchange rates in different countries. Covered interest rate parity states that the difference in interest rates between two countries is equal to the expected change in exchange rates. Empirical research supports this theory, showing that there are no unexploited profit opportunities in covered interest arbitrage. On the other hand, uncovered interest rate parity suggests that the spot exchange rate should adjust to reflect differences in interest rates. Studies have found that exchange rates quickly adjust to new information, supporting the asset-market theory of exchange rates. However, some corporate borrowers deviate from interest rate parity principles in their currency choices.
Research by Frenkel and Levich (1975) concluded that transaction costs, demand-supply elasticities, and timing lags in executing arbitrage account for most apparent profit opportunities in covered interest arbitrage. Doukas and Melhem (1986) found that exchange rates adjust rapidly to new information, but also move randomly in response to information that alters market expectations. McBrady, Mortal, and Schill (2010) observed that corporate borrowers often make opportunistic currency choices, deviating from interest rate parity principles based on factors such as issuer size, location, ratings, and cash flow. In summary, interest rate parity explains the relationship between interest rates and exchange rates. Covered interest rate parity shows no unexploited profit opportunities, while uncovered interest rate parity allows for potential profit opportunities. Empirical studies support interest rate parity but also highlight cases where corporate borrowers deviate from it in their currency choices.

10) Dual Listing in Advanced Capital Markets

Firms cross-list their shares in the US stock market to benefit controlling shareholders through risk premium reduction, access to developed capital markets, enhanced information disclosure, and increased bonding and monitoring mechanisms (Doidge, Karolyi, & Stulz, 2004). The Segment Hypothesis suggests that cross-listing helps firms in segmented markets avoid higher risk premiums and increase firm value (Doukas & Witzer, 2000). The Liquidity Hypothesis posits that cross-listing can improve stock liquidity by accessing more liquid markets (Doidge, Karolyi, & Stulz, 2004).
Factors influencing de-listing from the US stock exchange include lower turnover percentage, lower abnormal returns, higher regulatory costs, and negative abnormal returns in the home country after de-listing (Doidge, Karolyi, & Stulz, 2004). In the Tokyo Stock Exchange, US firms have chosen to de-list their shares due to diminishing market segmentation, recession in the Japanese economy, and changing growth opportunities (Alexander et al., 1988). Dual listing involves costs, and managers may choose not to engage in cross-listing if the benefits do not outweigh these costs (Alexander et al., 1988).
The conventional wisdom aligns with the market segmentation theory, where cross-listing provides advantages such as reduced transaction costs, lower information costs, a broader shareholder base, improved liquidity, and signaling of higher value (Alexander et al., 1988). Other explanations for cross-listing include changing market risk exposures, reduced cost of capital, enhanced investor protection, and improved price informativeness (Doukas & Witzer, 2000; Doidge, Karolyi, & Stulz, 2004; Fernandes & Ferreira, 2008). Ultimately, firms choose to cross-list or de-list based on the balance between the benefits and costs associated with these decisions.



11) Eurobonds

The Eurobond market offers advantages for US corporations to raise capital offshore and potentially reduce their cost of capital. According to the clientele hypothesis proposed by Kim and Stulz (1988), firms can borrow at lower costs in the Eurobond market compared to the domestic market, catering to European investors who are willing to pay a premium for debt issued by US firms. However, limitations exist, including the requirement of a good credit market reputation and the potential negative impact of excessive bond issuance on a firm's creditworthiness. The Eurobond market also involves higher contracting and issuing costs compared to domestic bonds.
In a fully integrated market, there should be no differential cost of borrowing in different markets. However, imperfections in global markets create advantages for the Eurobond market, such as investor anonymity, tax benefits, and risk diversification. US firms can benefit from issuing Eurobonds when there is high demand from foreign investors, driving up prices and allowing firms to realize higher gains. Nonetheless, the limitations and considerations surrounding reputation, excessive issuance, and higher costs should be taken into account when determining the optimal debt issuance strategy.
It is important to note that the clientele hypothesis should have been called the catering effect. Catering to investor demands is a short-term strategy, while the clientele effect is more permanent in nature.
The catering theory of dividends proposes simply that firms pay dividends when investors put a stock price premium on dividend payers. Thus, managers cater to investor demand, which means that firms follow the market trend in terms of whether or not to pay dividends, as Baker and Wurgler (2004) mentioned.
Overall, the Eurobond market offers potential benefits for US firms to raise offshore capital, reduce their cost of capital, and increase shareholder wealth. However, it is important to consider the limitations, including the need for a good credit reputation, the potential impact on creditworthiness, and the higher costs associated with Eurobond issuance.



12) Euro-Credit Market & Contagion Effects

Doukas (1989) examines the efficiency of the Euro-credit market and the occurrence of contagion effects. The study finds that while the Euro-credit pricing process is information efficient, non-country-specific risk factors systematically influence country-specific spreads. This implies that news related to changes in borrowers' creditworthiness has an immediate effect on Euro-spreads, indicating efficient processing of information by lenders. However, individual country spreads in Latin America are influenced not only by the creditworthiness of the borrowing country but also by the creditworthiness of other borrowing countries in the region, highlighting the presence of contagion effects.
The study also emphasizes sovereign risk, which refers to the risk faced by lenders that the borrower government may default on its foreign obligations. The findings suggest that lenders in the Euro-credit market are aware of the risks associated with lending to different countries. They consider factors such as the likelihood of default and adjust their lending rates accordingly. The study also highlights the importance for lenders to diversify their lending across different regions to reduce their exposure to systematic risks. In simple terms, it means that lenders should spread their loans to different countries to avoid putting all their eggs in one basket.
The managerial implication of the study is that international lenders face significant systematic risk when they concentrate their lending exposure in a single geographical region. Spreading lending exposure across different regions can help mitigate this risk.



13) Global CAPM

Stulz (1995) examines the Global CAMP (Capital Asset Pricing Model) as an alternative to the local CAPM for evaluating a company's risk in local markets. The global CAPM views a company as part of the global portfolio of stocks. Global diversification offers benefits such as lower risk premiums, required returns, and cost of capital, leading to higher stock prices and firm value. However, there are costs associated with globalization, including increased sensitivity of firm stock prices to global events, resulting in higher investor risk, risk premiums, and cost of capital, which can offset the benefits of global diversification.
The global CAPM formula, RS = RF + ?(RG-RF), considers the required return (RS), risk-free rate (RF), stock's beta in the local or global market (?), and the global market return (RG). Stulz's findings suggest that the cost of capital for firms in small countries should be estimated using the global CAPM rather than the local CAPM. For example, Nestlé's cost of capital is overstated when using the local CAPM because local investors demand a higher expected return to compensate for the higher risk in their portfolios compared to if they could diversify internationally. Stulz also highlights that the globalization of financial markets increases company values by reducing the cost of capital, ultimately stimulating further economic growth.
Overall, Stulz's research emphasizes the importance of considering the global CAPM and global diversification in evaluating a company's risk and cost of capital. It explores the benefits and costs associated with globalization and its impact on stock prices, firm value, and economic growth. The findings underscore the relevance of estimating the cost of capital using the global CAPM, particularly for firms in small countries, to accurately reflect their risk profiles and support informed investment decisions.



14) Opposite View that US Multinational Firms Trade at a Premium

To test the opposite view that US multinational firms trade at a premium compared to domestic firms, we can employ both short-term and long-term approaches. In the short term, an event study can be conducted by collecting share price data of US firms for a specific period before and after the announcement of their expansion. By analyzing abnormal returns during this timeframe, we can determine if there is a significant decline or abnormal return in stock prices. Control variables and robustness checks should be considered to ensure the reliability of the findings.
In the long term, the multinationality effect can be examined by tracking the share value of US firms over a period of five years after their diversification and comparing it with comparable domestic firms that did not expand internationally. The key is to ensure that the firms being compared are comparable at the time of the internationalization decision. By utilizing the difference-in-difference method, we can assess the growth or decline in share values and determine if US multinational firms trade at a discount compared to their domestic counterparts in the long term.
By conducting these tests and analyzing the results, we can gain insights into whether US multinational firms trade at a premium or a discount compared to comparable domestic firms, providing a better understanding of the impact of multinationality on firm value.



15) Fixed Exchange Rate System VS. Floating Exchange Rate System

The fixed exchange rate system is considered problematic compared to the floating exchange rate system due to several reasons. In a floating exchange rate system, currency values are determined by market forces, providing constant updates based on trade and capital flows. In contrast, the fixed exchange rate system sets a predetermined value for the currency and requires continuous intervention by the central bank to maintain that rate. This intervention can deplete reserves or lead to excessive money printing, potentially causing inflation and economic imbalances. Examples such as Switzerland, which abandoned its pegged exchange rate to the Euro due to concerns about increased monetary supply, and Venezuela, where a fixed exchange rate system has resulted in significant foreign exchange shortages and inflation, highlight the challenges and vulnerabilities of the fixed exchange rate system.
On the other hand, the floating exchange rate system offers flexibility and automatic adjustments based on market dynamics. It allows currencies to freely fluctuate in response to supply and demand, providing a more efficient mechanism for price discovery. By allowing exchange rates to float, countries can avoid the risks associated with trying to hold a fixed peg against market forces, such as depletion of reserves and unsustainable policies. Overall, the floating exchange rate system is considered more favorable as it promotes stability, efficiency, and resilience to external shocks, while the fixed exchange rate system often leads to distortions, imbalances, and vulnerability in the long term.



16) Carry Trade

According to a study by McBrady et al. (2010), carry trade refers to a strategy where an investor sells a currency with a lower interest rate and uses the proceeds to buy a different currency with a higher interest rate. This approach aims to capitalize on the interest rate differential, which can yield significant profits, especially when leverage is employed. Notably, similar behavior is observed in corporate finance, where firms opportunistically borrow in low interest rate currencies and lend in high interest rate currencies.
For a carry trade transaction to occur, the market must be in a state of disequilibrium. Investors can protect themselves from exchange rate fluctuations by either purchasing forward contracts or leaving their positions uncovered. In order for the trade to be profitable, the gains obtained from the strategy should surpass various transaction costs, including bid-ask spreads, brokerage fees, and expenses related to the inelasticity of supply and demand (Frenkel & Lewin, 1975).



17) The US: Too Big to Fail

The United States is seen through the lens of international finance as the world's foremost power for the global economy for several reasons. As the world's primary reserve currency, the US dollar provides significant advantages in international trade and finance. As a result, the US can influence financial markets and economic policies worldwide.
Second, the US financial system serves as the backbone of global finance. It houses some of the largest and most influential financial institutions in the world. As a result of the depth and sophistication of the US financial market, capital flows and resources are allocated efficiently, reinforcing economic power.
US regulations and standards are central to global financial regulation. International financial markets are affected by its policies and regulations. Global interest rates, liquidity conditions, and investor sentiment are influenced by monetary policy decisions by the US Federal Reserve.
Since the US financial system and economy are so vital to global financial stability, it is viewed as a "Too Big to Fail" country. A failure or disruption in the US financial system could be catastrophic for the global economy due to the interconnectedness and importance of US financial institutions. Therefore, policymakers and regulators intend to protect domestic and global economic well-being by preventing the failure of US financial institutions and mitigating systemic risks associated with US financial systems.

CEO envy = the difference between each firm`s CEO compensation and the median CEO compensation in each firm`s industry (i.e., CEO compensation relative to other CEOs in the Fortune 500 in the same industry). 

? large negative difference in total compensation between each CEO and the industry median means a large positive score for CEO envy. Put differently, those CEOs who earn substantially less than their Fortune 500 industry peers would have a high envy score. 

? CEOs often seek to maximize their own personal gain even if it is at the expense of principals. For example, the free cash flow hypothesis states that when there is extra cash on hand, CEOs will engage in mergers and acquisitions in order to increase the size of the firm (Jensen, 1988). 

? This increases their sense of prestige and possibly allows them to command a higher salary. The free cash flow hypothesis has been verified empirically by Doukas (1995). Hence personal CEO characteristics influence the strategic activity of the firm. This is a very established idea in the management literature as well (e.g., Hambrick & Mason, 1984).

? Envy of other CEOs is an important personal characteristic that can influence the direction of the firm. Not only do CEOs have an incentive to merge/acquire other firms in order to command a higher salary, but their envy of other CEOs can contribute to merger or acquisition waves because CEOs want to be paid the same and have the same level of prestige as their peers (Goel & Thakor, 2010).

KEY CONCEPTS:
Interest Rate Parity
Asssumption: Financial markets are perfect. There are no controls, transaction costs, taxes etc.

* For any pair of countries the interest rate differential must equal the forward exchange rate differential (premium/discount), otherwise riskless arbitrage will take place
* 1 + Rj / 1 + Ri = Fij / Sij    
R is the rate of interest, F is the forward rate, S is the spot rate
        Rj - Ri = (Fij - Sij )/Sij
* Interest parity theory: In equilibrium the premium (or discount) on a forward contract for foreign exchange is related to the interest rate differential according to:
* (F-S)/S=  (i-i*)/(1+i*)
* F & S are the forward and spot rate; i is the domestic rate of interest on a class of securities; i * is the foreign rate of interest
* When this formula is satisfied there are no profit opportunities from covered interest arbitrage (Frenkel & Levich, 1978)
Purchasing Power Parity Theory
Asssumptions: 
* Financial markets are perfect. There are no controls, transaction costs, taxes etc.
* Goods markets are prefect. International shipment of goods can take place freely, instantaneously and without cost or tariff
* There is a single consumption good common to everyone
* In perfect markets, identical goods have the same real price everywhere otherwise physical arbitrage will take place
* The variation in exchange rate for two currencies is equal to the inflation differential in the two countries over a period of time
* Tj - Ti = Sijt+1 - Sijt /Sijt
T is the inflation rate

Forward Parity Theory
* The combination of assumptions, that financial markets are perfect and the that the future is known with certainty, implies that the forward exchange rate must be equal to the future (certain) spot exchange rate, otherwise riskless arbitrage would take place
* Sijt+1 = Fijt 

SPECIFICALLY FOR QUESTION 3:	
Covered (ie., Fisher closed) interest rate parity condition
Asssumptions: 
* Financial markets are perfect. There are no controls, transaction costs, taxes etc.
* The future is known with certainty
* There is a single consumption good common to everyone
* The nominal rate in every country will be equal to the real rate of interest plus the expected future inflation rate
* Linear approximation: Rj = rij + Tj
* r is the real rate of interest, T is the inflation rate
* If the assumption of perfect good market is added, PPP combined with the Fisher relation implies that real rates of interest be equal everywhere. Since we know that the inflation differential is exactly offset by the currency appreciation, if real interest rates were different financial arbitrage would take place

For Covered (ie., Fisher closed) interest rate parity condition
It is assumed that no covered interest arbitrage opportunities exist. Arbitrage would arise when the interest rate difference between the home interest rate and the foreign interest rate is not completely offset by the forward premium/discount of the foreign currency in the forward market. Therefore the following relationship should hold: 

S(1+ i) = F(1+ i*)

Where S is the spot rate 
F is the forward exchange rate. 
i is the domestic interest rate
i* is the foreign interest rate.
To derive the forward rate for 6 months:
F = S(1 +i)/(1+ i*)

Suppose the six month i=5%, six month i*=7% and the spot exchange rate is $.01/Yen or 100Y/$. 
F = (.01)(1.05)/(1.07) = $0.009875/Y
Put another way:
If I have $100 to invest in Japan and I exchange it at the spot exchange rate I get 10,000Y. I invest it in a risk free security at 7% and expect to receive 10,700Y at the end of 6 months. To cover my position, I must sell 10,700Y forward.

To prevent covered interest arbitrage, S(l+ i) = F(l+ i*) must hold. This means that the forward exchange rate must be $.009813/Y. Just plug in the spot exchange rate and the interest rates and solve for F). If I sell 10,000Y forward, upon conversion I will get ($.009813)(10,700Y)=$105. This 5% return is the same that I would have received if I invested in the home market. Therefore no covered interest arbitrage opportunity is available because the interest rate differential is exactly offset by the forward premium/discount. If the forward exchange rate was $.009875/Y, upon conversion I would receive $105.66 which would be greater than the return in the home market and therefore I could benefit by engaging in uncovered interest arbitrage.

SPECIFICALLY, FOR QUESTIONS 1,2, & 4
Uncovered (ie., Fisher open) interest rate parity condition
* For two countries, the interest rate minus the foreign interest rate equals the expected % change in the exchange rate. 

International fisher effect (ife) - Also called ?fisher open? or ?uncovered interest parity? Very similar to interest rate parity. Only difference is that the forward exchange rate is replaced with the expected future spot rate. International Fisher Effect (also called Fisher-open) states that the spot exchange rate should change to adjust for differences in interest rates between two countries

So, F (the forward exchange rate) is now equivalent to Expected Future Spot Rate - > EFSP
EFSP = S(1 +i)/(1+ i*)

USING VALUES GIVEN IN Q1,2, & 4:

EFSP = (2.04)(1.045)/(1.0625) = $2.0064/£

KEY CITE(S)
Frenkel & Levich (1975) 
* Previous studies of covered interest arbitrage suggest that the parity condition is not always satisfied and thus implying unexploited profit opportunities.
* Paper provides a procedure for estimating transaction costs in the markets for foreign exchange and for securities.
* Allowance for these costs accounts for most of the apparent profit opportunities
* In addition to transaction costs, demand supply elasticities in the various markets and lags in executing arbitrage can account for all of the apparent profit opportunities
* It is concluded that empirical data are consistent with the interest parity theory and that covered interest arbitrage does not entail unexploited profit opportunities
* The neutral band around the interest parity line for the traditional pair accounts for 87% to 88.3% of the profit opportunities and 99.7% for the external pair thus showing the majority of the profit opportunities are accounted for when taking into consideration transaction costs of these two securities
* They model elasticities and the role of timing and show this accounts for the remainder of the profit opportunities


B.Solnik (1978)
Overview: This paper is a review the traditional theory of international parity.
* Focused on real differences between countries
* Monetary risk is assumed away
* PPPT will not apply
* Exchange risk (real) differs according to citizenship while monetary is global and identical for everyone
* The theory claims that a risk premium should be appended to the forward parity relation so that the forward rate (or interest differential) ought to be a biased predictor of spot rates.
* The exchange risk is a separate and important phenomenon rather than simply a monetary by-product
* Some interpret efficient market theory to mean that forward exchange rates should be unbiased predictors of the forward spot rate but this ignores risk
* The existence of a forward bias has received mixed results

Doukas & Melhem (1986
Overview: This paper examines the impact of new information on the term structure of exchange rates. 
* The response of spot and forward exchange rates to anticipated and unanticipated weekly US and Canadian money growth announcements, and the speed of adjustment of the foreign exchange market to these announcements are investigated
* The results indicate that the unanticipated component of the announced changes in the US money supply has an immediate negative effect on the foreign value of the USD
* This is consistent with the asset market theory of exchange rate determination that postulates that exchange rates immediately adjust to reflect new information
* The response of the forward market appears to be weaker thus supporting the overshooting hypothesis
* Asset-market theory of exchange rate determination: the exchange rate is the relative price of two national assets rather than the price of different national outputs, determined by the demand for the supply of various national currencies, 
* Exchange rates move randomly in response to new information that alters the expectations of the market about conditions that could influence exchange rates





3. International Diversification
* J. Doukas and N. Travlos, The Effect of Corporate Multinationalism on Shareholders= Wealth: Evidence from International Acquisitions, Journal of Finance, December 1988, 5; 1161- 1175
* R. Morck and B. Yeung, Why Investors Value Multi nationality, Journal of Business, 1991,64,2; 165-186.
* J. Doukas, Overinvestment, Tobin`s Q and Gains from foreign Acquisitions, Journal of Banking and Finance, October 1995, 19; 1285-1303.
* J. D. Denis, D.K Denis and K. Yost, "Global Diversification, Industrial Diversification and Firm Value", Journal of Finance, 57, 5,2002, 1951-1979.
* J. Doukas, and O. Kan, "Does Global Diversification Destroy Firm Value", Journal of International Business Studies, 37, 2006, 352-371

1. Denis, Denis and Yost (1979), recently showed that US multinational firms trade at a discount The evidence of this study is in sharp contrast with most of the previous empirical evidence. Discuss (20 points) ? Q2-May 2007 [There are 2 arguments: diversification destroys value and diversification creates value. We need to cite for two competing sides. They have methodological problems.]
2. While US firms have intensified their overseas expansion in recent years, in an attempt to enhance their market share and profitability, Denis, Denis and Yost, have recently shown that US multinational firms trade at a discount. The evidence of this study is in sharp contrast with most of the previous empirical evidence (i.e., Doukas et. al., Morek and Young, among others) that do not show that US multinationals lose shareholder value as a result of expanding abroad. Discuss. (20 points) ?Q5- May 2008
3. Recent Evidence shows that geographically diversified U.S firms trade at discount implying that geographical diversification destroys shareholder value. Discuss under what conditions this can happen.
4. Why do US firms expand in foreign markets? What are the motives behind global diversification?
5. What are the main motivations behind corporate multinationalism? Discuss the main results and differences of the recent evidence on cross-border acquisitions from the investors' perspective-Q5- may 2001 [Motivations can be good or bad ? empire building, etc.]

KEY CITES:
Arguments for ?Diversification destroys firm value?
* Jensen and Meckling 1976 - The reason why global diversification may destroy firm value is a line of agency costs (Jensen and Meckling, 1976). The global diversification driven by the managers self interest will potentially reduce shareholder value.
* Jensen 1986 - free cash flow theory - firm with substantial free cash flows will have a tendency to overinvest by accepting marginal investment projects with negative NPV. Therefore investors will discount the geographical diversification for a firm of low q but substantial free cash flows.
* Denis et al (2002): It appears that global diversified firms are traded at a discount as compared to non-globally diversified firms. The researchers estimate that discount to be between 18% and 32%. Thus, there has been a real cost to global diversification as measured by reductions in firm value. Denis, Denis & Yost,2002 found that global diversification reduces the shareholders value by 18% and industrial diversification reduces the value by 20%. A combined strategy of global & industrial diversification causes a reduction in shareholder value by32%. [Destroy firm value]

Arguments for ?Diversification does not always destroy firm value?

Kogut (1990): proposed that multinationalism creates value to the firm by introducing flexibility in the movement of resources. However there are a few things such as synergy and managerial objectives, need to be considered.
Doukas and Travlos 1988 ? it depends on type of acquisition (in new country, less developed country is value enhancing)
They test the positive multinational network hypothesis that multinationals have advantage of flexibility for moving assets and resources through their multinational network (Kogut 1980). They investigate the impact of international acquisitions on the stock prices of US bidding firms. They find that shareholders of firms making an acquisition in a new country where they have not previously made an acquisition experience positive abnormal returns. Firms who make acquisitions into less developed economies are more likely to see abnormal shareholder returns. '[Support that diversification brings value to the firm. Good for other question.]

Morck and Yeung 1991 -those who can leverage intangible assets do better
Their results show support for the theory of internationalization in that diversification can enable a firm to leverage their intangible assets and it does lead to increased firm value. [Use of intangible assets. Good for other questions]

Doukas 1995 - high Q firms do better
Doukas (1995) shows that foreign acquisition announcements have a significant impact on the stock price of bidder firms for high Q bidders and no effect on low Q bidders (consistent with Jensen`s overinvestment hypothesis). US bidder gains are larger when they expand into new markets and in less developed countries than the US (supports Doukas & Travlos 1988). The worst results are obtained by low Q bidders targeting foreign firms in countries where operations already exist by the bidder.
Doukas uses Jensen's (1986) overinvestment hypothesis as a theoretical foundation for his study. Jensen suggests that low Tobin's Q firms may be overinvested while high Tobin's Q firms may be value-maximizing. Acquisitions by high Q firms have higher CAR's than that by low Q firms. [High Tobin's Q are the firms that create value. The ones with low Tobin's Q, lose value. They are using it as a proxy for management quality]
Tobin's Q = Total Market Value of Firm / Total Asset Value of Firm.

Eun et al 1996 - Eun et al (1996) indicates that cross-border takeovers are generally synergy-creating activities. Second, shareholders of the U.S. targets realized significant wealth gains, regardless of the nationality of acquirers. 
Eun et al (1996) provide evidence that one source of this synergistic gain is reverse-internalization of the R& D capabilities of U.S. target firms. They test the synergistic gain international acquisitions. They present the synergistic gains depend on the motive of the acquiring firm. In general, gains will results when the Inquirer has the skills and motivation to take advantage of various market imperfections, especially the intangible assets. (Denis et al 2002)

Doukas and Lang (2003) show that geographic diversification increases shareholder value and improves long-term performance when firms engage in core-related foreign direct investments (Greenfield). Non-core related FDIs cause short term and long-term losses. 


Doukas and Kan 2006 - Debt level of firm is key - low debt firms do better. 
They argue that the level of risk could be impacting shareholder bondholder value and that global diversification decreases the firm risk. Thus, 
1) They posit that reduced risk as a result of global diversification should negatively impact shareholders and positively impact bondholders. 
2) They find that the loss in shareholder value is directly related to the firm's level of debt. Firms with little debt do not experience the loss in shareholder value that firms with debt do. 
3) This study shows that global diversification does not destroy firm value. Using cross-border acquisition data they find three things:
1. Shareholder value loss is directly related to leverage
2. Near-all equity firms do not trade at a discount
3. The use of book value debt in estimating excess value produces a downward bias in globally diversified firms

Overall, the effect of multinationalism depend on many factors: The amount of 1) tangible assets of bidder and target, 2) country of target (developed or developing), 3) quality of managers (high q low q), 4) previous activities of bidder in target`s country, 5) capital structure of the firm etc.. 
The literature has concrete evidence showing that multinationalism adds to the value of the firm. However, Denis et al argue that multinationalism destroys firm value. This finding contradicts the rest of the literature. Though one must realize that Denis et al did not divide their data set depending on any of these criteria as stated above.   [The key term is the debt]


 Doukas (in class, 2010): [Why did Denis 2002 find different results from others?]
1. Recent research has shown that previous studies were suffering from serious methodological weakness, however the controversy of value enhancing vs. value destroying is still unresolved in the literature. 
2. Bodnar et al (1999) used exactly the same methodology as did Denis et al (2002), however they found opposite results (international diversification is value enhancing). 
3. In Denis et al, value and diversification suffer from endogeneity: Is it low value companies that make a decision to diversify internationality, Or Firms that diversify internationally, buy low value firms, therefore are traded at a discount? 

Motivations behind corporate multinationalism
What are the main motivations behind corporate multinationalism? Discuss the main results and differences of the recent evidence on cross-border acquisitions from the investors' perspective-Q5- may 2001

Jensen 1986 - free cash flow theory:  Managers engage in acquisitions for empire building.

Doukas & Travlos 1988 - Acquiring firms' benefits from international acquisitions are greater when US firms expand into new geographic areas and when the target firms' countries have economies which are less developed than the US economy (supporting the branching tree)

Morek & Yeung (1991): they examine the value of multinationality to investors measured by Tobin`s Q. The positive impact of Intangible assets (advertising and R&D) on Q is enhanced by multinationality and this supports internationalization theory. Multinationality itself has no significant impact on Q. Their results do not support the hypothesis that investors value multinational firms as a means of diversifying their portfolio internationally.

Doukas 1995:  US acquisitions of foreign firms show that bidder abnonnal returns are substantially higher for high Q bidder than low Q bidders Bidder returns are found to be higher when foreign acquisitions take place in low tax-rate jurisdictions. US bidder gains are larger when they expand into new markets and in less developed countries than the US (supports Doukas & Travlos 1988)

Eun et al 1996 - provide evidence that one source of this synergistic gain is reverse-internalization of the R& D capabilities of U.S. target firms. They test the synergistic gain international acquisitions. They present the synergistic gains depend on the motive of the acquiring firm. In general, gains will result when the acquirer has the skills and motivation to take advantage of various market imperfections, especially the intangible assets
Doukas and Kan (2006) - Using cross-border acquisition data they find three things:
o Shareholder value loss is directly related to leverage
o Near-all equity firms do not trade at a discount
o The use of book value debt in estimating excess value produces a downward bias in globally diversified firms
Theory of corporate multinationalism - predicting an increase in the firm's market value from the expansion of its existing multinational network. Foreign investment decisions enhance the expanding firm's ability to benefit from the systematic advantages inherent in a multinational network.





4. Dual Listing in Advanced Capital Markets
? Why do foreign firms list their shares in advanced capital markets such as the NYSE and/or LSE? In addition, why during the last seven years have most foreign firms decided to list their stock shares outside the US capital market? (10 points) 
? Why do foreign firms list their shares in advanced capital markets such as the NYSE and/or LSE? (10 points)
? Explain the reasons foreign stocks are listed in the NYSE.
? Explain why dual listing of foreign stocks in the NYSE can benefit the shareholders of these firms.
? Why should firms list their shares in foreign stock exchanges? Is this beneficial to shareholders? Discuss.
? Why foreign firms from less developed and/or developing economies list their shares in the US and London stock exchanges?

ANSWER
Why firms pursue overseas listings
* The conventional wisdom of the market segmentation theory 
* Alexander et al. (1988): The net benefits to cross-listing firms stem from their decision to overcome barriers to international capital flow due to market segmentation with government controls on foreign exchange and capital market. The access to advanced capital markets reduces negative effects that allow foreign firms to raise funds at lower cost than at home:
* Dual listing allows US investors saving any transaction costs associated with dealing in foreign currency.
* The listing in advanced stock exchanges also results in a reduction of information cost: disclosure requirement by SEC and security analysts? activities following international listing.
* A broader shareholder base helps to enhance investor recognition and reduce the shadow cost of incomplete information, lower bid-ask spread and hence lower cost of capital.
* Liquidity effects: developed capital markets are more efficient and liquid than home markets. Market orders are executed with a greater immediacy and higher trading volume.
* Signaling effects: international listing reflects firm`s ability to meet the high disclosure standards establishing that they are higher value firms.
* [TC, partnership]
* However the prevailing explanations based on investment barriers and segmented markets were inadequate and offered several other possible explanations. 
* Changing Market Risk Exposures and The Cost of Capital hypothesis
* A number of studies argued that shares of a cross-listing firm may experience significant changes in its local and global market risk exposures and its cost of capital.
* Doukas and Witzer (2000) focus on the period surrounding the public announcements rather than the listing day, using a sample of Canadian companies listing in the US, show that:
o Firms operating in mildly segmented capital markets can attain a reduction of risk premium through international stock listings leading to a higher equilibrium market price and a lower expected return.
o Their empirical study also supports the view that international stock listing increases firm shareholder base and reduces cost of capital.
* Bonding hypothesis [why firms pursue cross listing.][Bonding is like bonding somebody to behave properly. Monitoring is the cost to the employer. Bonding is cost to the agents.]
* A number of papers in literature had suggested that:
o Foreign firms are generally controlled by large shareholders and U.S. listing enhances the protection of the investors, reduces the agency costs of controlling shareholders.
o The higher disclosure standard of the US makes it harder for controlling shareholders to expropriate cash flows. Consequently, listing in the U.S. is expensive for controlling shareholders of firms with poor growth opportunities.
o The bonding and monitoring benefit of listing exist even in perfectly integrated capital markets.
* Doidge (2004) in his empirical study supports the bonding hypothesis: 
o Cross-listing in the U.S. improves the protection for minority investors and decreases the private benefits. He also shows that for non-U.S. firms that cross-list on U.S. exchanges have voting premiums lower than non-U.S. 
o Firms that do not cross-list and both the high- and low-voting share classes benefit but the low-voting class benefits more. 
* Doidge, Karolyi and Stulz (2004) again offer empirical support for the bonding hypothesis: 
o Listing in the US exchange reduces the expropriation by controlling shareholders and increases the firm`s ability to take advantage of growth opportunities. 
o The premium is related to future growth opportunities, especially for companies from countries with poorer legal protections.
* A positive link between the information environment and cross-listing hypothesis
* Literature documented that a firm`s commitment to a higher level of disclosure associated with cross-listing can alter the incentives of informed market participants to collect and trade on private information, and thereby influence a firm`s stock price formation process.
* Fernandes and Ferreira (2008) investigate stock price informativeness of cross-listing firms in the U.S. and find that:
o Cross-listing improves price informativeness for developed market firms.
o For firms in emerging markets, however, cross-listing decreases price informativeness. [They get less of a premium]
o Price informativeness increases the most for firms in countries with the greatest investor protection. 
Why during the last seven years most foreign firms have decided to list their stock shares outside the US capital market?
* Doidge, Karolyi and Stulz (2007, 2008) in their empirical studies investigated the hypothesis of whether the passage of the SEC Exchange Act Rule 12h-6 adopted in 2007 which makes it easier for foreign firms to deregister and whether the Sarbanes-Oxley (SOX) Act of Congress in 2002 which has made U.S. listings significantly less attractive to foreign companies has been a main reason to explain why many foreign firms leave U.S. equity markets or list their stock shares outside the US capital market.
* Doidge, Karolyi and Stulz (2007, 2008)`s hypothesis bases on some arguments as follows:
* SOX makes a U.S. listing less advantageous because it imposes severe costs on companies and their managers, especially through the compliance requirements of Section 404.
* Controlling shareholders are willing to tolerate the cost of such better governance only if it allows them to raise capital on better terms to fund their firm`s growth opportunities and they find that the benefit of listing in the U.S outweighs the cost.
* If the costs for the controlling shareholder of a U.S. exchange cross-listing go up, delistings will increase or there will be fewer new listings and the value of firms with listings will fall relative to the value of firms without listings.
* If a foreign firm finds listing was no longer valuable, they would want to deregister and the passage of Rule 12h-6 made deregistration much easier than ever before.
* However Doidge, Karolyi and Stulz (2007, 2008) found no evidences that confirm their hypothesis. Their finding is more consistent with the bonding hypothesis than with the loss of competitiveness hypothesis: 
* The characteristics of listing firms have not significantly changed since the adoption of SOX.
* Firms that deregister have poor growth opportunities, come predominantly from more developed countries, and experienced poor stock return performance over a number of years before deregistration.
* Deregistering firms have a significantly lower cross-listing premium, but this lower ?cross-listing premium? cannot be explained by an adverse impact of SOX.
* There is still a distinct governance benefit for firms that list on the U.S. exchanges and that this benefit has not weakened over time.
* However they also find that in the aftermath of SOX, the non-listed firms have become smaller and are therefore less likely to list on the U.S. exchanges or the Main Market in London.




5. Delisting From Foreign Exchanges
In the recent past several foreign firms have de-listed from the NYSE. On the basis of this observation, propose a testable hypothesis, and design a tight testing procedure that consists of 3-4 stages/steps that allows you to test the merits of your hypothesis /theory).

A. Related Articles:

1 Alexander et al (1988) international Listings and Stock Returns:  Some Empirical Evidence
Summary: This paper investigates stock returns surrounding international listings. They find that international listings are accompanied by a reduction in its expected return provided that markets are partially/fully segmented.

Theory:
1. Partial/full segmentation:  If markets are not fully integrated, the stock prices should react to international listings: Barriers to international portfolio investment can be mitigated with cross-listing, Transactions costs are lower, Information costs are lower. 
2. Previous research has shown that international listing leads to the reduction in the expected return on the security if capital markets are completely/partially segmented

Results: International listing accompanies a reduction in the expected return on the stock and particularly for non-Canadian stocks because: Non-Canadian stock markets are more segmented than Canadian stock markets, and it could be that non-/Canadian stock markets are similarly segments but the Canadian market is more highly correlated with the US market.

2. Doidge, Karolyi & Stulz (2004) Why are foreign firms listed in the US worth more?
Summary: At the end of 1997, foreign companies, with shares cross-listed in the U.S., have Tobin`s Q ratios that were 16% higher than the Q`s of non-cross-listed firms from the same country. The valuation difference is statistically significant and reaches 37% for those companies that list on major US exchanges, even controlling for firm and country characteristics. They suggest that US listing reduces expropriation and increases the firm`s ability to exploit growth opportunities. They show that growth opportunities are more highly valued for firms that choose to cross-list in the US, particularly those from countries with poor investor rights. [Counter argument for the delisting. When reasons don't hold, this applies.]

Theory: 
Agency/Corporate Governance:  Firms will only cross-list in the US if it benefits the controlling shareholders; cross listing limits expropriation and increases the ability of firms to take advantage of growth opportunities: 
* Risk premium reduction
* Access to more developed capital markets
* Information disclosure
* Bonding and monitoring
Results: Firms that cross-list in the US have higher valuations (Q) than non-cross listed firms. Firms that cross-list have a price run-up prior to the listing. 

These two papers are explaining why foreign firms choose to enlist in U.S. stock markets. Therefore, when the benefits of enlisting in U.S. disappears, foreign firms may choose de-list from U.S. stock markets.


B. Hypothesis:

Segment Hypothesis: According to the segment hypothesis, firms in segmented markets face higher risk premium and experience higher cost of capital. Therefore, by cross-listing, they are actually trying to avoid the risk premium and subsequently increase firm value. 

Liquidity Hypothesis: Firms may choose to cross-list to be able to have access to more liquid markets with more investors. These foreign firms expect an increase in stock liquidity.

However, if these benefits of cross-listings disappear, firms may choose to de-list.

H1: Firms, with lower percentage of turnover in NYSE after cross-listing, are more likely to voluntarily de-list.
H2: Firms, with lower realized abnormal returns after cross-listing in NYSE, are more likely to voluntarily de-list.
H3: Firms are more likely to voluntarily de-list if they cannot increase firm value.

Firms may also choose to de-list due to regulatory environment. For example, foreign firms which are listed in NYSE are facing higher level of investor protection and higher costs due to regulation. Related hypothesis can be structured like this:

H4: Firms, facing higher level of regulations relative to their home country are more likely to voluntarily de-list. 

IV: Liquidity Measure: Turnover rate of foreign firms? stocks listed in NYSE.
       Cumulative Abnormal Return: Mean Adjusted Returns
       Firm Value: Tobin`s Q 
       Investor Protection Index 
       SOX Implication ( The act had a profound effect on corporate governance in the US. The Sarbanes-Oxley Act requires public companies to strengthen audit committees, perform internal controls tests, make directors and officers personally liable for the accuracy of financial statements, and strengthen disclosure)

DV: Voluntary de-listing: Available from CRSP
CV: B/M ratio, Market Capitalization, Segmentation of the markets

After de-listing from NYSE, firms may experience decreasing returns in their home country due to signaling power of de-listing. Basically, de-listing will signal that these companies performed poorly in NYSE. Therefore, we can hypothesize this idea like this:

[To measure Segmentation, we can use liberalization (trade, money market). The heritage has this.]

H5: Firms, after de-listing from NYSE, may experience negative abnormal returns at home country. 
Why Several U.S firms de-listed their shares from the Tokyo stock exchange? Is this beneficial to shareholders? Discuss.

1) Many U.S. firms listed in Tokyo stock exchange to establish reputation and open up to new and attractive product markets for future investment and trade. However, when Japan lost this attractiveness, U.S. firms de-listed from Tokyo stock exchange. U.S. firms enjoyed first mover advantage, but after Tokyo stock market became easily accessible they choose to de-list.

2) Capital Market Segmentation is a financial market imperfection, mainly caused by government restrictions, investor perception and institutional practices. Most important results of segmentation are high transaction costs, political risks, foreign exchange risk, lack of transparency, corporate governance and high regulatory barriers. Because of segmentation, U.S. stocks can be inaccessible for Japanese investors due to high transaction costs, information costs, and high government controls (Stulz, 1995 and Alexander et al, 1988). Capturing this demand in Japan can be a reason for U.S. firms to establish presence in Japanese stock markets. However, after the integration of Japanese stock markets into the world markets, Japanese investors can access to U.S. markets on their own. Therefore, the decreased demand for the U.S. stocks in Japan might result with U.S. firms? de-listings.


3) The economic recession in Japan reduced the benefits of enlisting in Tokyo stock exchange.

4) Currency sentiment may be another reason for delisting of U.S. firms. When Japanese economy was booming, many U.S. firms entered Japanese markets to build up wealth, reduce the cost of capital, and create value. However, after Japan started to experience slowing down which is followed by the booming in Chinese economy, currency sentiment changed from Japan to China.

De-listing from Japanese stock market is found to be motivated by value creation incentives rather than managerial incentives.    



6. Currency Hedging/Currency Risk
- Does corporate currency hedging matter from the shareholders perspective? Discuss. (20 points)
- Currency hedging has been reported to be of great concern to many corporate managers. In light of the empirical evidence on the pricing of currency risk at the aggregate stock market and firm-specific level should corporate managers hedge the currency exposure of the firm they manage? Discuss. 
- Firms devote substantial resources to manage currency risk. Is this a sound financial management practice? Discuss 
- Exchange rates have fluctuated dramatically in recent years, should corporate managers hedge currency risk to protect shareholders' wealth or not? Discuss (20 points) 
- Corporate currency hedging has been reported to be of great concern to many corporate managers. In light of the empirical evidence on the .pricing of currency risk at the aggregate stock market and firm-specific level explain whether corporate managers should deal with the currency exposure of the firm? 
- Currency risk hedging is investors', not corporate managers' problem. In reality, however, firms devote substantial resources to manage currency risk. Is this a sound financial management practice? Discuss. 
- If firms hedge against currency risk, using currency derivatives, should investors pay a premium for the equity shares of these firms?

- Allaynis and Weston 2001
- Allaynis et al 2001

- Guay and Kothari 2003
- Jorion 1990
- Jorion 1991
- Knoff et al 2001
