KEY CITES
Modigliani and Miller 1968 - Modern portfolio theory: In a perfect world, investors are not willing to pay a premium for firms with an active hedging policy if foreign exchange risk can be diversified away. As a result more stable earnings should not change the cost of capital nor add value to the firm. Risk management is irrelevant to the firm's shareholders who can do it on their own.
Jorion 1990 - firms that have a high degree of international involvement have higher foreign exchange exposure and hedging might be useful to mitigate financial exchange exposure
Jorion 1991 - found empirical evidence that shareholders do not value the hedging activities of managers. His results show that it does not appear that stock returns are not impacted by a company's level of exchange risk (Double Negative). Thus, hedging does not increase shareholder value. Note: this does not mean that foreign exchange risk is not present, it simply means that is not priced by investors.
Knoff et al 2002 - measured the relationship between managerial stock and option portfolios and the firm's usage of hedging strategies to reduce firm risk. The authors find a positive relationship between managers' stock option portfolios and hedging. Knoff at al. (2002) show that as the sensitivity of the CEO's total portfolio increases, the firm's use of derivatives increases. If the stock return volatility is high, they will be willing to take risks and not hedge. If on the other hand the stock price volatility is high they will not take risks and hedge, since this could lead to the value of their equity declining 
}
Allayannis and Weston 2001 - using Q as a proxy for firm value they find a positive relationship between firm value and the use of foreign currency derivatives. The hedging premium is statistically and economically significant and is on average 4.7% of firm value. They also find some evidence that hedging causes an increase in firm value. This is the first study to link hedging to firm value
Allayannis et al 2001 - they test the impact of operational hedging on exchange rate exposure. They find a significant relationship between financial hedging and exchange rate exposure, but no relationship between operational hedging and exchange rate exposure. They test whether operational and financial hedging has any impact on firm value and find that operational hedging alone does not add firm value. Yet, when operational hedging is used with financial hedging, results indicate that firm value is increased.
Guay and Kothari 2003 - They explore the importance of derivatives in a firm's overall risk management strategy. Specifically, they study the magnitude of risk that firms are trying to minimize through the use of hedging strategies. Their findings indicate that derivative usage is not a major strategy as evidenced by the small magnitude of usage compared to overall firm size and operating cash flows. They quantify the impact of derivatives usage and find that the median firm benefits by cash flow increases. Hedging isn't "free" ? the firm must expend resources (transaction and translation costs) to undertake hedging activity. There are two ways companies can hedge: financial (short term hedges) or Operational (long term hedges). Value is added only if increased return/value is sufficiently large to compensate for cost of hedging.

[Diversify portfolio away, so it is not important to investors.]
Arguments for currency hedging
- Jorion 1990 - firms that have a high degree of international involvement have higher foreign exchange exposure , and hedging might be useful to mitigate financial exchange exposure
- The market is not perfect. Hence, risk management creates shareholders' wealth. In reality, the market conditions are not that perfect and, as a consequence, shareholders do face two categories of risks (systematic risks and unsystematic/idiosyncratic risks). Investors can always eliminate unsystematic risk or idiosyncratic risk by diversifying their assets across different regions and sectors. In contrast systematic risks (for example global recession) cannot be eliminated through diversification procedures. In this case, investors bear systematic risks because they expect to receive a risk premium
- Currency hedging reduces bankruptcy costs and the cost of debt
- In the presence of high underinvestment costs, hedging helps ensure the firm has sufficient internal funds
- In the presence of high currency risk exposure, hedging helps ensure a stable future cash flow when the firm is exposed to high currency risk
- Stabilize cash flows
- High costs of financial distress: transaction costs of financial distress can induce firms to hedge financial price risks since the probability of incurring these costs (financial distress) is reduced by hedging
- High underinvestment costs: hedging helps ensure the firm has sufficient internal funds which enables the firm to avoid unnecessary fluctuations in either investment spending or costly external financing and so increases firm value
- Aids in planning


Arguments against currency hedging
- Hedging is costly.
- Hedging does not increase the cash flow of the firm, rather it reduces the uncertainty of cash flow
- From the managerial perspective, if managers cater to the demand of investors, they will hedge when there is a hedging premium and avoid hedging when there is a hedging discount
- Mangers may have an incentive to hedge at the expense of shareholders wealth - as proposed by agency-theory. Management is generally more risk averse than shareholders and may therefore not focus on the primary goal, which should be to maximize shareholder wealth. Managers ultimately can't outguess the financial market experts, and the long-term expected net present value of hedging is zero.
- Managers are usually better informed about structural imperfections as compared to individual investors, and they can take advantage of the disequilibrium conditions through selective hedging.
- In the presence of transaction costs, mangers have cheaper access to hedging as compared to individual shareholders.
- Hedging activities are positively related to the sensitivity' of a manager's stock and stock option portfolios to stock price 

Unless a firm faces one of the following situations, high cost of financial distress, high underinvestment cost, or high currency risk exposure, the use of currency hedging is questionable.

Even if one of these conditions exists, currency risk hedging may not add value to the firm. If investors do not have any preference regarding currency risk hedging then they will not pay a premium for such an activity. Further, if investors think that hedging risk is diversifiable then they may even discount hedging activities. Since low risk is favored by debt holders, currency risk hedging might transfer wealth from shareholders to debt holders. Hence, unless there is no serious risk, shareholders will not value hedging.

There are different empirical findings in the literature. 
Jorion (1990) found that for certain industries or company portfolios, the exchange rate affects firm value. However, this study has serious flaws. First, the data sample is very small and limited to MNEs. Second, in the first part of the study, Jorion states that he found foreign exchange risk for MNEs but he actually didn?t. Bartov & Bodnar (1994) found that for firms that use currency risk hedging, there are no abnormal returns with contemporaneous changes in the dollar, rather there is a relation with lagged changes in the value of the dollar and firm value. Guay & Kothari's (2003) results suggest that the magnitude of derivative positions held by most firms is small in relation to their entity-level risk exposure; however, firms perceive that the benefits outweigh the costs. There was no underlying theory in this paper; therefore, the results are questionable. Knopf et al (2002) shows that as the sensitivity of the stock option portfolio to stock return volatility increases, the firm hedges less.

Briefly, as past studies point out, if the risk is diversifiable then firms should leave it to investors. Further, operating a financial hedging program is costly which reduces the benefits to hedging, so such activity is unadvisable or has minimal benefits. Moreover, when agency theory is considered, managers may be concerned about other things rather than firm and shareholder benefits. Finally, Doukas (in class argument) argues that hedging does not add value to the firm. Firms prosper by operating efficiencies and creating value. Hedging is for protection only.

Jorion (1991) vs Bartov and Bodnar (1994)
Jorion examines the pricing of exchange rate risk in the US stock market using two factor and multifactor arbitrage pricing. Portfolio diversification by investors takes care of the foreign risk exposure, and hedging is only valued if it covers systematic sources of risk.

Jorion found that the risk premium of foreign exchange risk is never significant. However, this study has major issues. The study assumes fixed risk premiums over the entire sample period. However, in reality this is not the case. Depending on numerous factors, risk premiums change. Further, Jorion studied only the current effect of foreign exchange risk on firm valuation. 
This is where Bartov and Bodnar (1994) fill in. They look at both the contemporaneous effect of currency risk on firm value and the lagged relationship between exchange rate movements and firm value. For firms with significant foreign currency adjustments, they find that abnormal returns are not found with contemporaneous changes in the dollar, however, lagged changes in the value of the dollar and firm value are found. These are foreign currency adjustments reported in financial statements and negatively correlated with corresponding trade-weighted index of the USD

In sum the reason for the disagreement in the literature is because Jorion overlooked the lagged relationship between currency risk and firm value.

[If a firm is heavily exposed to foreign currencies, a firm can benefit. The technique of currency hedging is to buy currency at a specific price.]




7. Internalization Theory/ Multinational Network Hypothesis
Questions 1?4 are similar, question 5 also would fit under INTERNATIONAL DIVERSIFICATION 

1. Describe the differences between internalization theory and multinational network hypothesis. (10 points) 

2, Describe the differences between internalization theory and reverse internalization theory with regards to the foreign investment activity of the firm.

3. Describe the Internalization hypothesis and its predictions.

4. Describe the Multinational Network Hypothesis and its predictions.

5. Why do US firms expand in foreign markets? What are the motives behind global diversification? Describe the internalization hypothesis and its predictions

KEY CONCEPTS and descriptions for the three theories

Internationalization Theory ? Morck & Yeung (1991)
According to internationalization theory, a multinational firm has an advantage due to firm-specific intangible assets that allow it to overcome the adversity of doing business in a foreign location (LOF). An assumption underpinning the theory is that market failures (imperfections) lead to the inability to provide secure channels for governing the flow of intangible assets between firms in different countries. In other words, due to the high transaction costs of contracting for proper governance and use of intangible assets (i.e., intellectual property rights), a firm may be less inclined to engage in franchising or licensing. 

Internationalization theory argues, firms engage in FDI in order to increase firm value by internalizing markets for their intangible assets. If the intangible assets are information based, like production or marketing skills, then they are assumed to behave similar to public goods in that their value increases in direct proportion to the scale of the firm`s markets. 

The consequences or predictions are that firms with intangible assets would tend towards investing directly in foreign countries in order to utilize the assets on a larger scale and to internalize the market system in order to have greater control making sure that the assets are not misappropriated during transactions in foreign markets. Thus only firms that currently possess intangible assets should be expected to benefit from multinationality, i.e., the direct effect is from the intangible assets and the multinationality serves as an accentuating moderator. 

Morck & Yeung (1991) in an examination of the value of multinationality to investors as reflected in q ratios find support for the internationalization theory prediction that intangible assests (advertising and R&D) are necessary to justify FDI. Their results do not support the hypothesis that investors value multinational firms as means of diversifying their portfolios internationally. 

However, there are firms without significant intangible assets successfully engaging in FDI indicating there may be other explanations of why firms expand internationally. 

Reverse internationalization differs from internationalization in that a firm does not possess intangible assets prior to expanding into foreign markets but is motivated to expand/invest in these markets with the express purpose of obtaining intangible assets such as skills, knowledge-based resources, and expertise to be deployed and leveraged not necessarily in the host country but in other locations, such as their home country. (Eun, 1996)
Multinational Network Hypothesis
MNCs can be expected to arbitrage institutional restrictions, capture informational externalities, achieve cost reductions, production economies of scale. Therefore MNCs are expected to create value to the extent that their foreign investments are designed to achieve international operational hedging positions that are valued by investors as well as being able to capture rents and benefits arising from any systematic advantages inherent in the multinational network. 

The announcement of a foreign acquisition may result in abnormal returns on the acquiring firm`s stock provided that the expansion of the firm`s operations to foreign markets tends to accomplish the investors? international-diversification objectives while enhancing the acquiring firm`s ability to benefit from the systematic advantages inherent in a multinational network (branching tree). The imperfect world capital markets theory suggests that because of barriers and information asymmetries, investors are prevented from multinational stock diversification but can use MNEs to accomplish it for them therefore impart a higher value to firms expanding internationally. 

The theory was tested empirically by Doukas & Travlos (1988). Using an event study methodology, they investigated the effect of international acquisitions on stock prices of 301 US bidding firms (1975 ? 1983). The findings supported the Multinational Network Hypothesis, predicting an increase in the firm`s market value from the expansion of its existing multinational network. Specific findings are:

* Shareholders of MNCs not operating in the target firm`s country experience significant positive abnormal returns at the announcement of acquisitions
* Shareholders of US firms expanding internationally for the first time experience insignificant positive abnormal returns
* Shareholders of MNCs operating already in the target firm`s country experience insignificant abnormal returns
* Abnormal returns are larger when firms expand into new industry and geographic markets, especially those less developed than the US
* Findings are consistent with the Multinationa Network Hypothesis, predicting an increase in the firm`s market value from the expansion of its existing multinational network

Question 5. Why do US firms expand in foreign markets? What are the motives behind global diversification? See above discussion on reasons/motives given by the internationalization theory, reverse internationalization theory and Multinational Network Hypothesis. Brief summary given here:

Internationalization: FDI occurs because a firm seeks to increase its value by internalizing markets for its intangible assets.
	
Reverse internationalization: to obtain intangible assets to be utilized in the home country

Multinational Network Hypothesis: to generate abnormal returns due to investors increasing their valuation of the firm`s equity because of the firm being able to achieve operational advantages thorugh a network of global operations. 

Besides the reasons supported by the internationalization theory, reverse internationalization theory and Multinational Network Hypothesis, other reasons firms may wish to expand internationally include:

Managerial objectives: Managers act in a self interested manner to increase the size and complexity of firms (agency problems), which may lead to higher compensation as both these factors are associated with higher compensation. Also, internationalization diversifies the firm and lowers firm-specific employment risk for the manager (Jensen & Meckling, 1976; Denis, Denis & Yost, 2002)

Tax avoidance and low-cost inputs: multinationality raises the value of the firm because it permits tax avoidance and access to low-cost inputs (Doukas 1995)

Lower bondholder risk ? the contingent claims hypothesis: the global diversification discount increases with leverage indicating that firms with diversified operations across markets are subject to lower risk (Doukas & Kan, 2006)

KEY CITES
Doukas & Travlos (1988) 
* This study investigates the effect of international acquisitions on stock prices of US bidders
* Shareholders of MNCs not operating in the target firm`s country experience significant positive abnormal returns at the announcement of acquisitions
* Shareholders of US firms expanding internationally for the first time experience insignificant positive abnormal returns
* Shareholders of MNCs operating already in the target firm`s country experience insignificant abnormal returns
* Abnormal returns are larger when firms expand into new industry and geographic markets, especially those less developed than the US
* For the entire sample, unanticipated announcements of international acquisitions are on average associated with normal returns (but this is a heterogeneous group)
* The 99 acquisition announcements made by US MNEs not operating in the target firms? country are associated with a positive abnormal return of .31 percent
* There are no positive abnormal returns for MNCs already operating in the target firms? country (the branch of the tree already exists there)
* Acquiring firms? benefits from international acquisitions are greater when US firms expand into new geographic areas and when the target firms? countries have economies which are less developed than the US economy (supporting the branching tree)

Morck & Yeung (1991)
* The positive impact of advertising and R&D on Q is enhanced by multinationality and this supports internationalization theory
* Multinationality itself has no significant impact on Q
* The positive impact of advertising and R&D on Q is enhanced by multinationality and this supports internationalization theory
* Multinationality itself has no significant impact on Q
* There is weak evidence that multinationality in the absence of R&D or advertising-related intangibles may reduce firm value and this is consistent with the possibility that managers? decisions may be more difficult to monitor in large complex MNEs and that shareholders may be neglected; but the tax effects diversification may be mitigating this so there is no clear answer

Doukas (1995) 
* US acquisitions of foreign firms show that bidder abnormal returns are substantially higher for high Q bidder than low Q bidders
* Bidder returns are significantly and inversely related to free cash flow for low Q bidders but not for high Q bidders
* Financial markets reward more the initial than subsequent foreign investments of value maximizing MNEs.
* Bidder returns are found to be higher when foreign acquisitions take place in low tax-rate jurisdictions
* The 1986 Tax Reform Act exerted a negative effect on bidder returns
* US bidder gains are larger when they expand into new markets and in less developed countries than the US (similar to Doukas & Travlos 1988)
* Low Q bidders have a negative association between bidder returns and free cash flows and this supports the overinvestment theory
* Low Q firms with too much cash invest in poorly performing (negative NPV) projects; free cash flows are positively associated with agency costs
* There is a negative correlation between the dollar exchange rate and the level of FDI

Denis, Denis & Yost (2002)
* 44,288 firm years between 1984 and 1997 - increased in the extent of global diversification over time, and is not just a substitution of global diversification for industrial diversification
* They also find that global diversification results in average valuation discounts of approximately the same magnitude as those for industrial diversification
* global diversification results in 18% shareholder loss
* Findings support that view that the costs of global diversification outweigh the benefits - Analysis of the changes in excess value associated with changes in diversification reveals that increases in global diversification reduces excess value, while reductions in global diversification increase excess value

Doukas & Kan (2006)
Use sample of cross-border acquisitions and the contingent claims framework ) In finance, a contingent claim is a derivative whose future payoff depends on the value of another ?underlying? asset, or more generally, that is dependent on the realization of some uncertain future event ( which posits that the risk-reduction effects of global diversification should decrease the value of shareholder equity (call option) and increase bondholder value
* Shareholder value loss is directly related to leverage
* Financial leverage is the use of debt to buy more assets. Leverage is employed to increase the return on equity. However, an excessive amount of financial leverage increases the risk of failure, since it becomes more difficult to repay debt

* Near-all equity firms do not trade at a discount
* The use of book value debt in estimating excess value produces a downward bias in globally diversified firms
* Increased foreign involvement increases bondholder value it while decreases shareholder value, supporting the contingent claims framework ? thus global diversification does not destroy firm value


8. Euro-Bonds
* Y.C. Kim and R. Stulz, The Eurobond Market and Corporate Financial Policy: A Test of the Clientele Hypothesis, Journal of Financial Economics, 1988, 22; 189-205.
* J. Doukas, Syndicated Euro-Credit Sovereign Risk Assessments, Market Efficiency and Contagion Effects, Journal of International Business Studies, Summer 1989; 255-267.

1. Empirical evidence shows that US corporations realize abnormal gains from issuing Euro-debt. If this is true, why don't they raise more debt in the offshore markets? Discuss.-Q3-may 2004 and 2001 
2. Can U.S firms benefit by raising capital from offshore markets (i.e: issuing Eurobonds?) If they do, why they issue a small fraction relative to the domestic debt?

KEY CITES:
Kim and Stulz (1988)- found positive abnormal returns on euro bond issues, but did not find wreck support for the clientele affect. The authors found on average positive abnormal returns post the 183 Eurobond issues over the period of 1975-1985 as compared to zero or negative reactions to domestic bond issues reported by earlier research.

The clientele hypothesis states that firms can increase shareholder wealth by exploiting their comparative advantage in providing securities that are high in demand by certain financial clientele. This hypothesis implies that for periods of time some firms can borrow at lower costs in the Eurobond market than in the domestic market. [Take advantage of a lower interest rate] [Europeans are the financial clientele][The hypothesis is about increasing shareholder wealth. The US investors are willing to pay a premium to issue debt]

In a fully integrated market, no differential cost of borrowing should exist in different markets, but some imperfections take place between US and European markets. There are two main advantages or imperfections of the Eurobond market which creates higher demand for its securities:
1. Tax benefits - are tax free:
2. Investor anonymity (unknown) [People are more willing to pay more for the anonymity]

Therefore, U.S firms can benefit by raising capital offshore (reducing cost of capital and increasing value). In other words, bargains survive because when demand for the Eurobonds form foreign investors is high, foreign investors compete for them and hence bid up their prices

Why Eurobond market is limited:
1. Accessible only to firms that have a good credit market reputation. Because of the loose control, only the firms that have high reputation can be able to issue successfully and attract investors in those markets.
2. Excessive bond issuance is no matter domestically or internationally, will have the potential to decrease the creditworthiness. Therefore, US firms cannot issue unlimited euro debt because if they do so they are signaling to the rest of the market that they have trouble in the US market. 
If firms keep issuing overseas, investors might think that they are trying to avoid US regulations. More precisely, higher percentage of debt from Eurobonds may signal the inability of firm to meet disclosure performance and reporting requirements in the US. No doubt it will destroy the firm's reputation or even close the door for them to borrow overseas. 
3. Euro-bonds are costlier than issuing domestic bonds 

Dr.  Doukas mentioned that Clientele is a permanent effect; Catering is short term in nature. In fact, the clientele hypothesis should have been called Catering effect. 

The catering theory of dividends proposes simply that firms pay dividends when investors put a stock price premium on dividend payers. Thus, managers cater to investor demand, which means that firms follow the market trend in terms of whether or not to pay dividends, Baker and Wurgler (2004).
9. Endogeneity
Evaluating the impact of a corporate decision (i.e., investment, capitals structure change, among others) requires testing for endogeneity of that decision. Explain why it is important to do so and what econometric procedures would you adopt? (5 points). 10point-2010

ANSWER
* Perhaps the most important and persistent issue confronting empirical studies in corporate finance, strategic management, ect? is endogeneity, which can be shortly defined as a correlation between the explanatory variables and the error term in a regression. [This breaks one of the assumptions of regression. The error terms cannot be correlated. This can be a reverse causation problem.]
* The corporate decision depends on the presence of firm-specific characteristics and industry conditions that lead firms to generate more value from one strategy than others. The strategy choice is, therefore, endogenous and self selected, Shaver (1988). 
* For ex. the endogeneity of a firm's diversify decision in evaluating the effect of diversification on firm value, see Campa and Kedia (2002); Doukas and Kan (2006).
* Evaluating the impact of strategy choice variables on the outcome, for ex. firm value, requires taking into account the endogeneity of the corporate decision. If not doing so, Endogeneity leads to biased and inconsistent parameters estimated and the empirical test is invalid.
* To set up an econometric procedures that appropriately addresses the problem of endogeneity, one have to make sure that all of the following steps are completely done: [Determine which variables are endogenous and why]
* First, be sure to make clear which variable(s) among others, are endogenous and why they are endogenous. In addition to the endogeneity problem of strategy choice or self-selection bias, the following causes of endogeneity should be taken into account and controlled:
* Omitted variables refer to those that should be included in the group of explanatory variables to predict the dependent variable, but are not. If the omitted variable is correlated with any of the explanatory variables, then the composite error term is correlated with the explanatory variables, resulting in endogeneity problem. [Include control variables]
o For ex. the effect of a credit rating on a firm`s leverage ratio, firms with credit ratings tend to be larger on average and many studies have shown a link between leverage and firm size -> Endogeneity problem of omitting variable.
* Simultaneity bias arises when we can reasonably argue either that xi causes y or that y causes xi. Simultaneity also occurs where one variable has an ambiguous effect on another. 
o For ex., firms with high investment opportunities that can lead to an underinvestment problem have more incentive to hedge because hedging can alleviate it. On the other hand, lower investment opportunities can lead to problem of larger free cash flow, which create more incentive to hedge. -> Endogeneity problem of simultaneity bias.
* Measurement Error in both dependent and independent variables if it is measured imperfectly, it becomes part of the regression error. This in turn impacts on coefficient estimates. Studies which rely greatly on proxies have more problems of Measurement Error and hence endogeneity. For ex. Leverage = market value of debt (not book value of debt) /market value of firm, Invesment opportunity = marginal q (not tobin`s q). 
* The second step is the finding of instrumental variable, z. [This is after we find out which variable is endogenous. We would develop an instrumental variable. Find a variable that is correlated with the independent variable and not with the DV. Then we run a regression to determine the predicted variable and plug it back into the original equation. The instrumental variable cannot explain the dependent variable.]
* The IV is a variable that satisfies two conditions:
o The instrument must be correlated with the endogenous variable. 
o The instrument variable is uncorrelated with outcome variable or Cov(z, u) = 0.
* Finally, the most common approach of two-stage least squares-2SLS (3SLS) which supposed to deal with IV and endogeneity can be broken down into two parts:
* Estimate the predicted values, ?xk by regressing the endogenous variable xk on all of the exogenous, controls and instruments variables.
*  Replace the endogenous variable with its predicted values from the first stage ?xk and regress the outcome variable y on all of the control variables and ?xk.
* Alternative methods are to use Heckman`s (1979) correction model to control for self-selection bias induce on account of corporate decision; Panel Data Methods with Fixed-firm effect; Double Difference Estimator: Difference-in-Differences (DD); and finally avoiding Proxies or using Proxies correctly. [To deal with endogenetiy problem]
* Hausman`s specification test can be used to test hypotheses in terms of bias or inconsistency of an estimator. Hausman`s m-statistic can be used to determine if it is necessary to use an instrumental variables method rather than a more efficient OLS estimation. [Determine which method is the best]


10. Stock Market Deficiency
Recent empirical evidence shows that stock trading in less developed and emerging markets is characterized by high levels of synchronicity. Define stock price synchronicity and explain why such a phenomenon exists. (May 2008)

Synchronicity is the experience of two or more events which are causally unrelated occurring together in a meaningful manner. Therefore, ?Stock Price Synchronicity? refers to the tendency of the stock prices to move in the same direction. 

Possible explanations:
1. Firms in low-income countries might have more correlated fundamentals, and this correlation might make their stock prices move more synchronously. [Information is behind this. We don't trust the firms, and we trust the market]
2. Low-income economies often provide poor and uncertain protection of private property rights. Political events and rumors in such countries could cause market-wide stock price swings.
3. In countries that provide poorer protection for public investors from corporate insiders, problems such as intercorporate income shifting could make firm specific information less useful to risk arbitrageurs, and therefore impede the capitalization of firm specific information into stock prices. This effect would reduce firm specific stock price variation, again increasing stock return synchronicity.
4. Presence of greater analyst coverage increases stock price synchronicity because security analysts collect and disseminate information about firms. Securities which are covered by more analysts incorporate greater (lesser) market-wide (firm-specific) information. The idea is when the market-wide information is high and firm-specific information is low for a particular security, investors cannot differentiate between the securities, so the prices of these securities will be more likely to move in the same direction.


Related Articles:

1.Morck, Yeung & Wu, (2000) The Information Content of Stock Markets: Why Do Emerging markets Have Synchronous Stock Price Movements? 
Summary: Stock prices move together more in poor economies than in rich economies. This finding is not due to market size and is only partially explained by higher fundamentals correlation in low income economies. However, measures of property right do explain this difference. The systematic component of returns variation is large in emerging markets, and appears unrelated to fundamentals co-movement, consistent with noise trader risk. Among developed economies, higher firm specific returns variation is associated with stronger public investor property rights. They propose that strong property rights promote informed arbitrage, which capitalizes detailed firm specific information.

Theory: This builds upon Roll`s article that the extent to which stocks move together depends on the relative amounts of firm=level and market-level information capitalized in the stock price


Results:
1. They show that in less-developed countries stock move together yet in developed nations like the US and Denmark stock do not move together as much. This confirms that stock returns are more synchronous in emerging countries.
o Synchronous stock markets are not a result from structural characteristics of economies such as market size, fundamentals volatility, country size, economic diversification or the co-movement of firm-level fundamentals.
o Less respect for property rights by governments is associated with more market-wide stock price variation and therefore also with more synchronous stock price movements
* Since these are not correlated with fundamentals they conjecture that poor property rights protection might discourage risk arbitrage and create noise traders
* However, since they are controlling for fundamentals volatility imperfectly, they cannot rule out other explanations.
o They show that in developed economies, providing public shareholders with stronger legal protections against corporate insiders is associated with greater firm-specific returns variation and so with lower synchronicity
o Results suggest that stock markets in emerging economies may be less useful as processors of economic information than stock markets in advanced economies
* The function of an efficient stock market is to process information and thereby guide capital towards its best capital use.

2. Chan and Hameed , Stock Price Synchronicity and Analyst Coverage in Emerging Markets (2006)
Summary: This paper examines the relationship between the stock price synchronicity and analyst activity in emerging markets. They find that securities which are covered by more analysts incorporate greater market-wide information. Using the coefficient of determination statistic of the market model as a measure of synchronicity of stock price movement, they find that greater analyst coverage increases stock price synchronicity. 
analyst coverage. Having one or more analysts actively tracking and publishing opinions on a company and its stock. Some investors believe that a company with analyst coverage benefits from more investment activity than a company without analyst coverage.


Theory: This study builds upon Morck et al (2000) that stock prices move together more in emerging economies than in developed markets which suggests that less firm-specific information is produced in emerging markets.

The lack of firm-specific information in emerging markets is attributed to:
1. Little enforcement of regulation,
2. A low degree of voluntary information disclosure, 
3. Many families in emerging markets are group/family owned and it is difficult to obtain information.

Results: The number of analysts following a stock increases with stock synchronicity

3. Piotroski & Roulstone (2004), The influence of analysts, institutional investors, and insiders on the incorporation of market, industry, and firm-specific information into stock prices 
Summary: This paper investigates the extent to which the trading and trade-generating activities of three informed market participants, financial analysts, institutional investors and insiders, influence the relative amount of firm-specific, industry-level and market-level information impounded in stock prices as measured by stock price synchronicity.

Theory: This is the third article we have covered that sprouts from Morck`s (2000) article that discussed stock price synchronicity. This paper extends the theory by looking at: The firm-specific information interpretation of stock return synchronicity and the type of information contributed by the trading and trade-generating activities of insiders, institutions, and analysts

Results: 
1. They find that stock return synchronicity is positively associated with analyst forecasting activities, which is consistent with analysts increasing the amount of industry-level information in prices through intra-industry information transfers.
2. In contrast, stock return synchronicity is inversely related to insider trades consistent with these transactions conveying firm-specific information.
3. Supplemental tests show that insider and institutional trading accelerate the incorporation of the firm-specific component of future earnings news into prices alone, which analyst forecasting activity accelerates both the industry and firm-specific component of future earnings news.

Comprehensive Exam-2017
1. Empirical studies invariably control for endogeneity. Explain the nature of endogeneity problem with a specific example and describe the econometric procedures that you would use to mitigate its possible effects. What econometric method/procedures are available to address endogeneity?
9.3 Empirical studies invariably control for endogeneity.  Explain the nature of endogeneity problem with a specific example and describe the econometric procedures that you would use to mitigate its possible effects. 

Endogenous: having an internal cause or origin
    Perhaps the most important and persistent issue confronting empirical studies in corporate finance, strategic management, ect? is endogeneity. The corporate decision depends on the presence of firm-specific characteristics and industry conditions that lead firms to generate more value from one strategy than others. The strategy choice is, therefore, endogenous and self selected, Shaver (1988). 
    For ex. the endogeneity of a firm's diversify decision in evaluating the effect of diversification on firm value, see Campa and Kedia (2002); Doukas and Kan (2006).
    Evaluating the impact of strategy choice variables on the outcome, for ex. firm value, requires taking into account the endogeneity of the corporate decision. Endogeneity can be defined as a correlation between the explanatory variables and the error term u in a regression. One major assumption of OLS regression is that the xi values are uncorrelated with the error terms u. Note that this is impossible to confirm because we only have estimates of the error terms and if correlation exists, then these estimates will be incorrect. If not doing so, Endogeneity leads to biased and inconsistent parameters estimated and the empirical test is invalid.
    In addition to the endogeneity problem of strategy choice or self-selection bias, the following causes of endogeneity should be considered and controlled:
    * Omitted variables refer to those that should be included in the group of explanatory variables to predict the dependent variable, but are not. If the omitted variable is correlated with any of the explanatory variables, then the composite error term is correlated with the explanatory variables, resulting in endogeneity problem.
    For example, firm size is a common determinant in CEO compensation studies. If larger firms are more difficult to manage, therefore require more skilled managers, then firm size is endogenous because managerial ability, which is unobservable, is in the error term and is correlated with an included regressor, firm size. The bias in the estimated firm size coefficient will likely be positive, assuming that the partial correlation between ability and compensation is positive, and that the partial correlation between ability and firm size is also positive. Thus the true effect of firm size is overestimated. 
    * Simultaneity bias arises when we can reasonably argue either that xi causes y or that y causes xi. Simultaneity also occurs where one variable has an ambiguous effect on another. 
    For example, in a regression of market-to-book on an index of antitakeover provisions, the usual result is a negative coefficient on the index. However, this result does not imply that the presence of antitakeover provisions leads to a loss in firm value. It is also possible that managers of low-value firms adopt antitakeover provisions in order to entrench themselves. 
    *  Measurement Error in both dependent and independent variables if it is measured imperfectly, it becomes part of the regression error. This in turn impacts on coefficient estimates. Studies which rely greatly on proxies have more problems of Measurement Error and hence endogeneity. For ex. Leverage = market value of debt (not book value of debt) /market value of firm, Invesment opportunity = marginal q (not tobin`s q).
    Reeb, et.al. (2012) provide remedies. The standard remedy for endogeneity is finding an instrument for the endogenous regressor, xi and do a two-stage least squares (2SLS).
    The IV is a variable that satisfies two conditions (relevance and exclusion conditions):
    * The instrument must be correlated with the endogenous variable. 
    * The instrument variable is uncorrelated with outcome variable or Cov(z, u) = 0.
    * Find a variable that is correlated with the independent variable and not with the DV.
    Finally, the most common approach of two-stage least squares-2SLS (3SLS) which supposed to deal with IV and endogeneity can be broken down into two parts:
    * Estimate the predicted values, ?xk by regressing the endogenous variable xk on all of the exogenous, controls and instruments variables.
    *  Replace the endogenous variable with its predicted values from the first stage ?xk and regress the outcome variable y on all of the control variables and ?xk.
    
    Alternative methods are to use Heckman`s (1979) correction model to control for self-selection bias induce on account of corporate decision; Panel Data Methods with Fixed-firm effect; Double Difference Estimator: Difference-in-Differences (DD); and finally avoiding Proxies or using Proxies correctly.


2. Should corporate managers hedge currency risk to protect shareholders? wealth or not? Explain.

Why some corporate managers have a tendency to hedge more than others? Discuss. 
a. There is a debate in finance academic about whether the manager should hedge the currency risk or not. I personally support the manager to use currency derivatives to hedge the currency risk. My reasons are as follows:
1. Based on Jorion (1990), companies, especially those ones involve into international activities, have higher exposure with the currency risk. The results also shows that exchange-rate exposure is positively related to the foreign sales level. So, hedge the currency risk is needed. (High level of exposure with currency risk and its impact on sale level)
2. Bartov and Bodnar (1994), in response to the ambiguous finding in the literature about the benefit of currency hedging for the companies, argue that the reason why previous studies cannot find a clear relationship is because: 1) sample biased (only consider MNE who are already operationally hedged and usually use financial hedge); 2) only use US dollars against 17 currencies (should use some other 4 or 5 currencies); and 3) should consider the time lag for invested to digest the information. They found that the lagged model worked and while exchange rate risk exists, internationalization is still benefit over risk. (previous studies that argue currency hedging is not beneficial were problematic because of three reasons)
3. Doukas (1989) points out that investors are facing two kinds of risks: systematic risk and nonsystematic risk. The nonsystematic risk can be fixed by diversifying in different sectors and regions. However, the systematic risk (such as a worldwide economic recession) cannot be diversified using that way. Currency hedging is one solution. 
4. In the context of market imperfections, hedging activities could add value to a firm since it can avoid costly external financing if the distribution of future incomes become smoother or less volatile as a result of exposure hedging (Guay & Kothari, 2003).
5. Allayannis and Weston (2001) find that the companies using currency hedging actually increase the firms? value. Thus, managers might be more inclined to hedge to increased firm value. Later, Allayannis (2001) points out that operational hedging alone cannot diversified the risk the firms are facing. Only combing the operational hedging and the financial hedging the firms can lower the risk.
6. Currency hedging, on the other hand, is costly. However, similar as the industry diversification which is discussed by Kuppuswamy and Villalonga (2011), currency hedging can be treated as risk assurance which paid by the companies in response to some economic shocks.
7. Based on last reason, using currency hedging can lower the companies? default risk, which will decrease the cost of debt. Also, with currency derivative in the portfolio, the companies do not need more money in hands which aim to deal with risk. This will decrease the underinvestment problem.
To sum up, with the globalization tendency and the integration of the capital markets of the world, there is a need to use currency hedging. However, Gury and Kothari (2003) find that the values that currency derivatives provide are statistically significant but not economic significant. Managers should notice this problem.

An underinvestment problem is an agency problem between shareholders and debt holders where a leveraged company foregoes valuable investment opportunities because debt holders would capture a portion of the benefits of the project, leaving insufficient returns to shareholders.

b. The reason why managers tend to hedge more than others is because that the managers hold portfolios of the companies? stocks and options, and the managers also have human capital input.
  On one hand, based on Jensen and Meckling (1976), there is a conflict between agents and principles (agency costs) within companies. Managers may change the firm`s decision not for the interests of shareholders but for the interests of themselves, especially when this decision is related to their own wealth or reputation. 
  On the other hand, based on the managerial incentives theory, keep the companies safe could secure the managers? jobs, offer them high reputations and salaries. Smith and Stulz (1985) point out that the managers will induce the companies using more hedging is the managers? wealth is 1) positively related to the companies? stock price, or 2) negatively related to the stock return volatility. The first relationship is simply due to the total stock value holding by the managers is directly related to the stock price. The second relationship is because the value of options held by the managers have convex relationship with the companies? stock return volatility. That means the more volatilities the stock returns, the more wealth the managers will have. 
    


3. 1) Why managers with high Delta are more likely to hedge, while with high Vega are less likely to hedge?
    Delta is the measurement of an option`s sensitivity to changes in the price of the underlying asset. Vega is the measurement of an option`s sensitivity to change in the volatility of the underlying asset. 
    By assumptions/model settings, higher stock return volatility (risk) increases the value of the options held by the CEO (because option is designed to fight against volatility). Thus, he/she has the incentive to engage in risky projects and does not want to hedge. In other words, higher Vega means more risk-seeking behavior and lower incentive to hedge. On the other hand, an increase in stock price associated with an increase in CEO`s total portfolio (stock + options+others). As a consequence, his/her wealth is tied to the company`s operation. He/She has the incentive to diversify. Thus, higher delta means more risk-averse and higher incentive to hedge.
    Consistent with the managerial incentives theory (Smith and Stulz, 1985), managers will induce the companies using more hedging is the managers? wealth is 1) positively related to the companies? stock price, or negatively related to the stock return volatility. 
    Knopf et al. (2002) use vega and delta to gauge CEOs? risk sensitivities. Consistent with the theoretical predictions of Smith and Stulz (1985), they find that hedging activities are positively related to the sensitivity of a manager`s stock and stock option portfolio to stock price, and that hedging activities are negatively related to the sensitivity of the manager`s stock option portfolio to stock return volatility; however, this latter relationship is not statistically significant. They conclude that these sensitivities provide a more powerful test of managerial incentives than the proxies used in earlier studies. 



Should corporate managers hedge currency risk to protect shareholders? wealth or not? Explain.

Why some corporate managers have a tendency to hedge more than others? Discuss

Exchange rates have fluctuated dramatically in recent years, should corporate managers hedge currency risk to protect shareholders' wealth or not? Discuss (20 points) 

If firms hedge against currency risk, using currency derivatives, should investors pay a premium for the equity shares of these firms?
Arguments for currency hedging
    - Increased exposure (Jorion, 1990; 1991)
a. Firms with high degree of international involvement have higher foreign exchange exposure , & hedging can be used to mitigate financial exchange exposure
b. Hedging helps ensure a stable future cash flow when the firm is exposed to high currency risk ? stabilize cash flows
    - Systematic & unsystematic risk (Doukas, 1989) 
a. In reality market conditions are not that perfect and, as such shareholders face 2 categories of risks (systematic risks & unsystematic risks)
b. Investors can eliminate unsystematic risk (I.E. Sovereign risk) by diversifying their assets across different regions and sectors. 
c. In contrast systematic risks (I.E. global recession) cannot be eliminated through diversification. 
    - Financial distress & bankruptcy cost
a. Reduces bankruptcy costs & cost of debt
b. The probability of financial price risk due to financial distress is reduced by hedging
    - High underinvestment costs
a. Helps ensure the firm has sufficient internal funds which enable the firm to avoid unnecessary fluctuations in either investment spending or costly external financing and so increases firm value
    - Aids in planning
Arguments against currency hedging
    - Hedging is costly (Guay & Kothari, 2003) 
    - Hedging does not increase the cash flow of the firm 
a. rather it reduces the uncertainty of cash flow
    - Managerial incentives (Knopf, et al., 2002) 
a. Agency theory: managers may have an incentive to hedge at the expense of shareholders wealth. 
b. Management is generally more risk averse than shareholders and may not focus on the primary goal of shareholder wealth. 
c. Hedging activities are positively related to the sensitivity' of a manager's stock and stock option portfolios to stock price 
    - Investor valuation (Guay & Kothari, 2003) 
a. If investors do not have any preference regarding currency risk hedging, they will not pay a premium for such an activity (Jorion, 1991)
b. If the risk hedged can be diversified away, they will not value the hedging activity
c. Since low risk is favored by debt holders, the currency risk hedging might transfer wealth from shareholders to debt holders 
Hence, unless there is no serious risk, shareholders will not value hedging.
There are different empirical findings in the literature
1. Jorion (1990)  firms that have a high degree of international involvement have higher foreign exchange exposure and hedging might be useful to mitigate financial exchange exposure
2. Jorion (1991) exposure of U.S. common stock to residual exchange rate risk is not systematically related to expected returns. Therefore U.S. investors do not price exchange risk
a. Arbitrage pricing theory: if the economy is described by a small number of pervasive factors, then these factors may be priced in the sense that investors will be willing to pay a premium to avoid these sources of risk.
i. hedging policies can affect the cost of capital if the exchange rate is one of those factors
b. Modern portfolio theory: investors are not willing to pay a premium for firms with active hedging policies if foreign exchange risk can be diversified away
    
    Jorion studied only the current effect of foreign exchange risk on firm valuation & overlooked the lagged effects of currency risk on firm value
3. Bartov & Bodnar (1994) provide suggestions for firms that use currency risk hedging, that there are no abnormal returns with contemporaneous changes (but lagged changes) in the dollar and firm value. 
4. Guay & Kothari (2003) the magnitude of derivative positions held by most firms is small in relation to their entity-level risk exposure; however, firms perceive that the benefits outweigh the costs. There was no underlying theory in this paper; therefore, the results are questionable. 
a. There are two ways companies can hedge: financial (short term hedges) or Operational (long term hedges). 
b. Hedging is costly: Value is added only if increased return/value is sufficiently large to compensate for cost of hedging.
    c. Corporate risk-management theory:  Market imperfections --> volatility costly --> Hedging can:
i. Reduce the volatility of taxable income &lower expected taxes 
ii. Reduce risk premium required by managers in their compensation when managers are risk averse and under-diversified
iii. Reduces the costs of financial distress
5. Knopf et al (2002) shows that as the sensitivity of the stock option portfolio to stock return volatility increases, the firm hedges less.
a. Agency theory: managers are risk-averse because they have investment in stock and human capital in the firm ? they mitigate the risk of their stock options
b. Managerial Incentive theory of Hedging: managers will induce the firm to hedge more as the sensitivity of the managers? wealth to stock price increases, and will hedge less as the sensitivity of managers? wealth to stock volatility increases

Briefly, as past studies point out, if the risk is diversifiable then firms should leave it to investors. Further, operating a financial hedging program is costly which reduces the benefits to hedging, so such activity is unadvisable or has minimal benefits. Moreover, when agency theory is considered, managers may be concerned about other things rather than firm and shareholder benefits. Finally, Doukas (in class argument) argues that hedging does not add value to the firm. Firms prosper by operating efficiencies and creating value. Hedging is for protection only.

2) Stock price synchronicity and explain why such phenomenon is more prevalent in less developed countries.

Recent empirical evidence shows that stock trading in emerging markets is characterized by high levels of synchronicity. Define stock price synchronicity and explain why such a phenomenon exists.

    Stock price synchronicity refers to the tendency of stock price to move in the same direction. Stock return synchronicity represents the extent to which firm-level returns are explained by industry and market returns. A measure that is commonly used to analyze stock price synchronicity is the R square statistic from the market model. A higher R square from the market model indicates a high degree of stock price synchronicity.
According to Merck et.al. (2000), stock price move together more in poor economies than in rich economies. They think that weak property right protection discourage informed risk arbitrage in low-income countries and therefore prevent firm-specific information from being incorporated into stock price.
There are several possible explanations about this phenomenon:
 (1) Correlated market fundamental; 
    Firms in low-income countries might have more correlated market fundamentals, and this correlation might make their stock prices move more synchronicity.
 (2) Poor property rights protection; 
    Low-income economies often provide poor and uncertain protections of private property rights, which deters risk arbitrage and create noise traders. Political events and rumors in such countries could cause market-wide stock price swings. Problems such intercorporate income shifting by corporate insiders could make firm specific information less useful to risk arbitrageurs, and therefore impede the capitalization of firm specific information into stock prices. This will reduce firm specific price variation, again increasing stock return synchronicity.
 (3) Insufficient enforcement of regulation; (lack of information disclosure)
 Low-income economies have a low level of voluntary information disclosure. Many families in emerging markets are group/family owned and it is difficult to obtain information from such firms.
 (4) Analysts coverage (Chan and Hameed, 2006)
 Presence of greater analyst coverage increases stock price synchronicity because security analysts collect and disseminate information about firms. In emerging markets, financial analysts process more market information than firm specific information. Thus, securities which are covered by more analyst incorporate greater (lesser) market-wide (firm-specific) information. The idea is when the market-wide information is high and firm-specific information is low for a particular security, investors cannot differentiate between the securities, so the prices of these securities will be more likely to move in the same direction.
 
 Morck, Yeung & Wu, (2000)
o Results suggest that stock markets in emerging economies may be less useful as processors of economic information than stock markets in advanced economies
* The function of an efficient stock market is to process information and thereby guide capital towards its best capital use.
 



Synchronicity is the experience of two or more events which are causally unrelated occurring together in a meaningful manner. 
Stock Price Synchronicity refers to the tendency of the stock prices to move in the same direction. 
Possible explanations:
1. Correlated market fundamentals:
a. I.E.: market size, country size, economic diversification 
b. Firms in low-income countries might have more correlated fundamentals, and this correlation might make their stock prices move more synchronously.
i. Information behind this: we don't trust the firms, and we trust the market
2. Poor property rights protection:
a. Low-income economies often provide poor protection of private property rights, which deters risk arbitrage & create noise traders
b. Political events and rumors in such countries could cause market-wide stock price swings.
c. Problems such as intercorporate income shifting by corporate insiders could make firm specific information less useful to risk arbitrageurs, and therefore impede the capitalization of firm specific information into stock prices. 
i. This will reduce firm specific stock price variation, again increasing stock return synchronicity.
3. Insufficient enforcement of regulation, a low degree of voluntary information disclosure, & prevalence of relation based private family/group owned firms (difficult to obtain information from such firms due to the lack of networks). 
4. Analysts coverage:
a. Security analysts collect and disseminate information about firms. 
b. Securities which are covered by more analysts incorporate greater (lesser) market-wide (firm-specific) information. 
c. When the market-wide information is high and firm-specific information is low for a particular security, investors cannot differentiate between the securities, so the prices of these securities will be more likely to move in the same direction.
d. Results: The number of analysts following a stock increases with stock synchronicity


Stock Synchronicity
Synchronicity refers to the tendency for the stocks to move in the same direction. According to Morck et al (2000) stock prices move together more in poor economies than in rich economies. They reason this to the level of protection of shareholder rights.
Morck et al show that in countries that provide poorer protection for public investors from corporate insiders firm specific information is less useful to risk arbitrageurs and this impedes the capitalization of firm specific information into stock prices and this would reduce firm specific stock price variation and increase stock return synchronicity. Further, they show that in developed economies, providing public shareholders with strong legal protection against corporate insider is associated with greater firm specific returns variation and so with lower synchronicity.


8.3 Stock Market Deficiency

Recent empirical evidence shows that stock trading in less developed and emerging markets is characterized by high levels of synchronicity. Define stock price synchronicity and explain why such a phenomenon exists. (May 2008)

Synchronicity is the experience of two or more events which are causally unrelated occurring together in a meaningful manner. Therefore, ?Stock Price Synchronicity? refers to the tendency of the stock prices to move in the same direction. 

1.Morck, Yeung & Wu, (2000) The Information Content of Stock Markets: Why Do Emerging markets Have Synchronous Stock Price Movements? 
Summary: Stock prices move together more in poor economies than in rich economies. This finding is not due to market size and is only partially explained by higher fundamentals correlation in low income economies. However, measures of property right do explain this difference. The systematic component of returns variation is large in emerging markets, and appears unrelated to fundamentals co-movement, consistent with noise trader risk. Among developed economies, higher firm specific returns variation is associated with stronger public investor property rights. They propose that strong property rights promote informed arbitrage, which capitalizes detailed firm specific information.

Theory: This builds upon Roll`s article that the extent to which stocks move together depends on the relative amounts of firm=level and market-level information capitalized in the stock price

Results:
3. They show that in less-developed countries stock move together yet in developed nations like the US and Denmark stock do not move together as much. This confirms that stock returns are more synchronous in emerging countries.
o Synchronous stock markets are not a result from structural characteristics of economies such as market size, fundamentals volatility, country size, economic diversification or the co-movement of firm-level fundamentals.

    4. Chan and Hameed , Stock Price Synchronicity and Analyst Coverage in Emerging Markets (2006)
Summary: 

Results: The number of analysts following a stock increases with stock synchronicity

3. Piotroski & Roulstone (2004), The influence of analysts, institutional investors, and insiders on the incorporation of market, industry, and firm-specific information into stock prices 
Summary: This paper investigates the extent to which the trading and trade-generating activities of three informed market participants, financial analysts, institutional investors and insiders, influence the relative amount of firm-specific, industry-level and market-level information impounded in stock prices as measured by stock price synchronicity.


Results: 

1. They find that stock return synchronicity is positively associated with analyst forecasting activities, which is consistent with analysts increasing the amount of industry-level information in prices through intra-industry information transfers.
4. In contrast, stock return synchronicity is inversely related to insider trades consistent with these transactions conveying firm-specific information.
5. Supplemental tests show that insider and institutional trading accelerate the incorporation of the firm-specific component of future earnings news into prices alone, which analyst forecasting activity accelerates both the industry and firm-specific component of future earnings news.






4: Hypothesis development questions: the link between CEO overconfidence and foreign mergers and acquisitions.
   There are four hypothesis I want to test about CEO envy and international expansion. 
First, international M&A actives always show as waves. The reason why companies engage into this activities earlier should due to international opportunities or growth potential. On the other hand, the reason why companies engage into this activities later in the wave should more likely caused by CEO envy.
    H1: International M&A activities caused by CEO envy should be in the later stage of an M&A wave.
To test this hypotheses, I first will collect the list of companies involved into the recent two merge waves. Then, based on the time line, the first quarter companies will be grouped as earlier movers, and the last quarter companies will be grouped as later movers. The data could be collected from CRSP database and Compustat database. CEO envy will be calculated as the difference between the CEO`s salary and the median CEO`s salary in the same industry based on previous research. Using difference-in-difference methodology to figure out whether the CEO envy level of the later mover group is significant higher than the earlier mover group, controlling for industry, firm size, ROE, D/E ratio. (debt to equity ratio)
    
    Second, based on Doukas (1995), investors will down value the company`s activities caused by overinvestment. Firms which are well managed (with high Tobin`s Q) have a higher potential to go international diversification than firms are not well managed (with low Tobin`s Q). The later ones go international may be caused by CEO envy. On the other hand, well managed firms will have a positive abnormal returns with the international M&A announcements, and poorly managed firms will have a negative abnormal reruns with the international M&A announcements. 
    H2: Companies engaging in international M&A activities caused by high CEO envy should have smaller Tobin`s Q.
    H3: Companies engaging in international M&A activities caused by high CEO envy should have negative abnormal stock returns with the announcements of international M&A.
    H2 will be tested by grouping companies engaged into international M&A activities into two groups?high CEO envy group and low CEO envy group. Using the difference-in-difference methodology to test whether there is a significant differences between those two groups? Tobin`s Q, controlling for all the firm level characteristics.
    H3 will be tested using event study analysis. The same as H2, I first group companies engaged into international M&A activities into two groups?high CEO envy group and low CEO envy group. Using Bloomberg data, I can collect the announcement dates of those companies? international M&A activities. Collect the stock prices around the announcement dates and calculate the cumulative abnormal return (CARs).
    Last, CEO envy cannot directly cause the bad decisions. If higher CEO envy really causes the companies to follow others and engage into the international M&A activities, this relationship should be mediated by the companies? corporate governance level. 
    H4: Companies with high CEO envy will be more likely engage into international M&A activities if they have low level of corporate governance.
    To test this hypotheses, I will first collect the lists of companies with high CEO envy from the above test, and using propensity matching score technique to find the matching companies who has the similar CEO envy and other firm characteristics but not involve in to the international M&A activities. Then collect the data of the high CEO envy group and the matching groups. The data can be collected from ExcuCom, and the data will contain number of board members, CEO turnover, CEO tenure, percentage outsider directors, CEO duality, G-index, and CEO age. Those data measure the companies? corporate governance from different perspectives. Still, using difference-in-difference technique to test whether companies with the high CEO envy engage into international M&A activities are those ones have poor corporate governance. 
Last, two more considerations can be used as robustness tests:
    First, CEO envy, based on previous research, is calculated as the difference between the CEO`s salary and the median CEO`s salary in the same industry. However, this measurement is questionable because CEO should envy more about the similar CEO`s salary rather than the median CEO`s salary in the industry. On possible way to deal with this is to rank all CEO`s salary in the industry, and the CEO envy should be the salary difference between this company`s CEO and the next higher company`s CEO.
    Second, based on Cyert and March (1963), most of the time the companies? decision is made by the top management team (TMT) rather than the CEO him/herself. TMT average envy can be used to measure the possibility of a company engaging into international M&A activities.



5: Hypothesis development questions: the link between brand value/recognition and the firm value.

    First of all, there are two aspects of brand value: the level of the brand value and the change of the brand value level. In my opinion, both of them should be tested.
    There are several secondary data in marketing area which can be used to measure the brand value. For example, rate of internationalization (ROI) is one of them. On the other hand, the number of subsidiaries can also be used. However, the brand value is more perceived by the customers. Then, besides using the secondary data, I want to develop a questionnaire to collect primary data. The basic design of the questionnaire could be that showing a lists of companies (in order to get rid of the industry effect and make the questionnaire simple, I may focus on one or two industries only), and ask customers to answer their familiarity with the company using 1 to 10 scales (10 is very familiar). To collect the data of the brand value level change and deal with endogeneity problem, this questionnaire will be distributed every month for more than one years. Second data can be collected from CRSP database. The ROI can be collected form Bloomberg.
The first test considers the basic firm characteristics. The hypotheses is that: firms with higher brand value (growth) should be those one with large firm size, better performance, and higher growth potential.
    Simple OLS regression. Using brand value (t-1) as dependent variable, and firm size, ROE, Tobin`s Q, are independent variables (t), country of origin will be used as a dummy variable. 
The second test considers the brand value effect on stock price. The hypotheses is that: firms with higher brand value (growth) will have higher stock returns.
    Simple OLS regression. Using brand value (t-1) as dependent variable, and stock price return (t) as independent variable, controlling for firm size, ROE, Tobin`s Q, and D/E ratio, country of origin will be used as a dummy variable.  
    Simple OLS regression. Using level change of the brand value (t-1) as dependent variable, and stock price return (t) as independent variable, controlling for firm size, ROE, Tobin`s Q, and D/E ratio, country of origin will be used as a dummy variable.  
    The third test considers that the higher brand value could increase the firm value by bringing more confidence for the investors, which will decrease the risk of the firm. The risk level can be measure as the stock volatility. The hypotheses is that: firms with higher brand value (growth) will show less volatilities than those with lower brand value (change).

The higher brand value/recognition could be a strong positive signal for the consumers to evaluate the quality of firm product, then increase the firm value. Controlling the firm size, firm industry, participator age, gender and education, simple OLS is used to test the above empirically.

ANOVA method could be used, and we even can test the interaction of brand value and brand recognition to firm value.
