    One limitation of the set 2 hypothesis is suitable for some small company, for the big and high value brand company, this hypothesis may a little vague.
    Robust check: Several robust check will be used to in this research:
1. We can use different way to measure brand value/evaluation to check if we can get the consistent results.
2. Or, we can pick up a partial industry or location to check if we can get the consistent results.
    


6: Describe the Denis et al (2002) findings and methodological deficiencies of this study? Why this research findings is contrast with several previous empirical studies.
    a) Denis et al. (2002) examine how global diversification and industrial diversification impact firm value. They find that, on average, globally diversified firms trade at a discount relative to a portfolio of single-segment, domestic firms operating in the same industries. Moreover, multivariate analysis reveals that, after controlling for other factors that potentially affect excess value, global diversification is associated with discount that is similar in magnitude to that associated with industrial diversification. The results show that firms using both industrial and global diversification will cause a decrease of firm value about 32%. Industrial diversification will cause a firm value decrease at 20% and global diversification will cause a value decrease of 18%. The discount associated with global diversification remains relatively constant over time and is robust to alternative estimation techniques that control for the endogeneity of the diversification decision, alternative measures of value, and alternative benchmarks. In one word, they find that increase in global diversification are associated with significant reductions in excess value, while reduction in global diversification are associated with increases in excess value. They thus conclude that diversification actually hurt the company. 
    Several previous empirical studies have the opposite results (argument for ?diversification does not always destroy firm value:
    Kogut (1990) proposed that multinationalism creates value to the firm by introducing flexibility in the movement of resources.
    Doukas and Travlos (1988) find that shareholders of firms making an acquisition in a new country where they have not previously made an acquisition experience positive abnormal returns. Firms who make acquisitions into less developed economies are more likely to see abnormal shareholder returns. 
    Douks (1995) find that foreign acquisition announcements have a significant impact on stock price of bidder firms for high Q bidders and no effect on low Q bidders (consistent with Jensen`s overinvestment hypothesis). Consistent with Doukas and Travlos (1988), He finds that U.S. bidder gains are larger when they expand into new markets and in less developed countries than the U.S. Therefore, high Tobin`s Q are the firm create value.
    Kuppuswamy and Villalonga (2010) focus on the industrial diversification during the financial crisis, and they find that this value decrease is more like insurance premium rather that a pure loss. This study find that the value of diversified firms relative to single-segment firms significant increase during the 2007-2009 financial crisis, and this mainly caused by two channels that the financial crisis can trigger the diversification change: 1) smart money effect, which focuses on the internal capital reallocation. It means that the capital within the diversified firm can move from the losing sectors to the benefits or safer sectors so that the firm will suffer less during the financial crisis. 2) More money effect, which focus on the low default risk of the diversified firms. In that case, the financial crisis increased the value of corporate diversification through better access to credit markets than single-segment firms had.
    Eun, et.al. (1996) indicates that cross-border takeovers are generally synergy-creating activities. Shareholders of the U.S. targets realized significant wealth gains, regardless of the nationality of acquirer.
    Doukas and Lang (2003) show that geographic diversification increases shareholder value and improves long-term performance when firms engage in core-related foreign direct investments (Greenfield).
    In addition, Doukas and Kan (2006) examine the sources behind the global diversification shareholder value loss in a contingent claims framework. This study postulates that the risk-reduction effects of global diversification should decrease the value of shareholder equity (call option), whereas they should increase bondholder value. Their findings shows that shareholder value loss is directly related to leverage. Consequently, near-all equity globally diversified firms should not experience a shareholder value loss. This is consistent with the contingent claims view predicting that global diversification has a positive impact on bondholders? wealth while it has a negative influence on shareholder value (i.e., global diversification discount). Overall, their results reveal that global diversification does not destroy firm value. 
    Doukas and Kan (2006) claim that the measurement used by Denis et. al. is wrong. Denis et al. (2002) follow Berger and Ofeg and use excess values (EV) to measure the firm value (Excess value is measured as the log of the ratio of the firm's actual value to its imputed value). This measurement calculates the debt value of the firm based on the book value which produce a downward bias in globally diversified firms because they only measures change in shareholder value, NOT firm value.. Doukas and Kan (2006) also argue that Denis et al. (2002)`s conclusion ignore the bondholders? perspective. Finally, Denis et al. (2002) consider a companies as international diversified if there is a foreign sale. This measure is also problematic. 
    Overall, the effect of multinationalism depend on many factors: (1) The amount of tangible assets of bidder and target; (2) country of target (developed or developing); (3) quality of managers (high q or low q); 4) previous activities of bidder in target`s country; (5) capital structure of the firm etc.
    The literature has concrete evidence showing that multinationalism adds to the value of the firm.
    b) Why this research findings is contrast with several previous empirical studies.
    (1) Recent research has shown that previous studies were suffering from serious methodological weakness, however the controversy of value enhancing vs. value destroying is still unresolved in the literature. 
    (2) Scholars used the exact same methodology as Denis et al.(2002) did, however they found international diversification enhances firm value
    (3) It should be noted when negative relationship is found, does the result suffer from endogeneity problem: already poor performing firms making decision to diversify, or firms that diversify buy low value firms could have caused the diversification discount as well. 
* Even though Denis et al., claimed that their result is not due to poor firms trying to diversify & thus there seem to be a negative casual relationship established. 
* They did mention, however, the discount might be due to the inefficient reallocation of resource and management due to increased complexity of a global firm
* Other reasons like managerial incentives (agency cost) might be considered 
    Three main points if we want to test the opposite view:
    1) Following Doukas and Kan (2006), we should consider both parts of the firms?shareholders? value and bondholders? value, and the value transfer from shareholders to bondholders.
    2) The bondholders? value should combine the debt value and the change of the leverage (such as the decrease of cost of debt or higher debt limitation).
    3) Following Kuppuswamy and Villalonga (2011), we can use event study to test whether those diversified firms actually safer than the non-diversified firms.
    


Motivations for diversification:
    1. Free cash flow theory (Jensen, 1986)
    a. Firms with substantial free cash flows will have a tendency to engage in acquisition for empire building 
    2. Managerial incentives theory: (Denis et al., 2002)
    a. Managerial personal incentives (better pay, more-diversified portfolio, & prestige)
    3. Internalizing value added resources across national boundaries (Morck & Yeung, 2002)
    4. Increasing firm flexibility to respond to changes in the tax codes, prices, and institutions 
    a. I.E. if tax increase in one country, an MNE that diversifies to different countries can shift focus to another lower tax country
    5. Firms can diversify with lower costs than individuals --> providing investors portfolio diversification --> willing to pay premium
Diversification destroys firm value: 
    1. Agency theory (Jensen & Meckling, 1976)
    a. The global diversification driven by managers? self-interest will potentially reduce shareholder value ? increased agency cost
    b. Weak support that diversification without intangibles reduces firm value due to self-centered managers (Morck & Yeung, 2002) 
    2. Free cash flow theory (Jensen, 1986)
    a. Firms with substantial free cash flows will have a tendency to overinvest by accepting marginal investment projects that have negative NPV
    3. Overinvestment theory (Doukas, 1995)
    a. Investors will discount diversification through overinvestment 
    b. Poor managed firms (over-investors with a low Q) have higher cash flows & lower stock returns in response to acquisition announcements
    c. Well managed firms (value-maximizing firms with a high Q) have higher stock returns in response to acquisition announcements 
ii. Because managers feel their compensation, power, prestige and job satisfaction are enhanced by managing a growing company. 
iii. However, these objectives are of little interest to investors (spending cash instead of making a dividends to investors)? divergence of interest cause agency cost and lowers the return 
    6. Multinational network hypothesis (Doukas & Travlos, 1998)
    a. Investment in a foreign market where acquirer have already existing operation is associated with negative abnormal return after acquisition announcement 
    b. Low Q (over-investors) gain even lower return when investing in a country with prior operations (Doukas, 1995) 
    7. Contingent claim hypothesis (Denis et al., 2002)
    a. Empirically found global & industrial diversification reduces value by 32%
    b. It appears that globally diversified firms are traded at discount as compared to single industry domestic firms
    c. There`s a cost associated with global & industry diversification measured by reduction of shareholder value
Diversification does NOT destroy firm value
    1. Internalization theory (Buckley & Casson, 1976) 
    a. Diversification can enable a firm to leverage their intangible assets to increased firm performance
    b. Those who can leverage intangible assets do better (Morck & Yeung, 2002)
    2. Multinational network hypothesis (Doukas & Travlos, 1988) 
    a. Investment in a foreign market where acquirer does not already have existing operations is associated with positive return 
    b. Firms diversify anywhere internationally for the 1st time experience positive return 
    c. Firms benefit more from diversification when expanding to emerging market or a new industry 
    3. Over-investment theory (Doukas, 1995)
    a. Value maximizers (well managed high Q firms) have higher stock return after foreign acquisition announcement
    b. Value maximizers have higher return when acquisition is in emerging market without already existing operations 
    c. Acquirer has higher return when target operates in low tax regions 
    4. Contingent claims hypothesis (Doukas & Kan, 2006)
    a. Global diversification discount increases with leverage 
    i. Loss in shareholder value is directly related to levels of firm debt
    ii. Firms with lower levels of debt experience less loss in shareholder value than firms with higher levels of debt
    iii. Near-all equity firms do not trade at a discount
    b. Level of risk can impact shareholder & bondholder value
    i. Reduced risk as a result of global diversification should negatively impact shareholder value & positively impact bondholder value
    c. The use of book value debt in estimating excess value produces a downward bias in globally diversified firms 
    d. Incorporating the bondholder perspective, global diversification does not destroy firm value
Overall, the effect of multinationalism depends on many factors: 
    1. Amount of intangible assets (Morck & Yeung, 1991) of bidder and target
    2. Country of target (Doukas & Travlos, 1988; Doukas, 1995) (developed or developing)
    3. Quality of managers (Doukas, 1995) (high q low q)
    4. Previous activities (Doukas & Travlos, 1988; Doukas, 1995) of bidder in target`s country
    5. Capital structure (Doukas & Kan, 2006) of the firm etc. 
The literature has concrete evidence showing that multinationalism adds to the value of the firm. However, Denis et al 2002 argue that multinationalism destroys firm value. This finding contradicts the rest of the literature. Though one must realize that Denis et al did not divide their data set depending on any of these criteria as stated above (Especially the debt)
Why did Denis 2002 find different results from others?
    4. Recent research has shown that previous studies were suffering from serious methodological weakness, however the controversy of value enhancing vs. value destroying is still unresolved in the literature. 
    5. Scholars used the exact same methodology as Denis et al., 2002 did, & however they found international diversification enhances firm value
    6. It should be noted when negative relationship is found, does the result suffer from endogeneity problem: already poor performing firms making decision to diversify, or firms that diversify buy low value firms could have caused the diversification discount as well. 
    a. Even though Denis et al., claimed that their result is not due to poor firms trying to diversify & thus there seem to be a negative causal relationship established. 
    b. They did mention, however, the discount might be due to the inefficient reallocation of resource and management due to increased complexity of a global firm
    c. Other reasons like managerial incentives (agency cost) might be considered 


7: The difference between multinational network and the internalization hypothesis and discuss which of the two hypothesis is more consistent with the patterns of foreign corporate expansion that has taken place in recent years.
    The multinational network hypothesis predict an increase in the firm`s market value from the expansion of its existing multinational network. Firms generate abnormal returns due to investors increasing their valuation of the firm`s equity because of the firm being able to achieve operational advantages through a network of global operations. The imperfect world capital markets theory suggests that because of barriers and information asymmetries, investors are prevented from multinational stock diversification but can use MNEs to accomplish it for them therefore impart a higher value to firms expanding internationally. Therefore, the expansion of the firm`s operations to foreign markets tends to accomplish the investors? international-diversification objectives while enhancing the acquiring firm`s ability to benefit from the systematic advantages inherent in a multinational network. The main benefits the firm can get through building the multinational network are:
* arbitrage institutional restrictions
* capture informational externalities
* achieve cost reductions
* production economies of scale 
* Investor will value the companies? diversification
Doukas and Travlo (1988) find empirical evidence parallel with this hypothesis. In general, this study provides evidence that multinational activities (i.e. FDI, international M&As) enhanced the firm value

According to internalization hypothesis (Caves, 1971), a multinational firm has an advantage due to firm-specific intangible assets that allow it to overcome the adversity of doing business in a foreign location. An assumption underpinning the theory is that market failures (imperfections) lead to the inability to provide secure channels for governing the flow of intangible assets between firms in different countries. 
Firms with intangible assets tend to internalize the market system in order to have greater control making sure that the assets are not misappropriated during transactions in foreign markets. The motive of firms to invest internationally is that they already own intangible assets and want to take advantage of it internationally.
    Based on this argument, only firms that currently possess intangible assets should be expected to benefit from multinationality. Morck and Yeung (2002) find that the positive impact of advertising and R&D on q is enhanced by multinationality and this supports internalization theory.

The multinational network hypothesis, in my opinion, is more consistent with the patterns of foreign corporate expansion that has taken place in recent years. For example, many companies move their headquarters outside US mainly due to the taxation reason, rather than possessing intangible assets. Also, Eun et al. (1996) point out that there is a reverse internalization hypotheses. Reverse internationalization differs from internalization in that a firm does not possess intangible assets prior to expanding into foreign markets, but it is motivated to expand/invest in these markets with the express purpose of obtaining intangible assets such as skills, knowledge-based resources, and expertise that can internalize in their home country. (Eun, 1996)
    Last, the internalization hypothesis totally ignore the risk of being an international player. In another words, the hypothesis cannot explain how to reduce firms? risk by going international. The multinational network hypothesis clearly explain the risk reducing perspective. 
    

Internalization Theory Morck & Yeung (2002)
1. According to internalization theory, a multinational firm has an advantage due to firm-specific intangible assets that allow it to overcome the adversity of doing business in a foreign location. 
2. An assumption underpinning the theory is that market failures (imperfections) lead to the inability to provide secure channels for governing the flow of intangible assets between firms in different countries. 
a. In other words, due to the high transaction costs of contracting for proper governance and use of intangible assets (i.e., intellectual property rights), a firm may be less inclined to engage in franchising or licensing. 
b. Firms with intangible assets tend to internalize the market system in order to have greater control making sure that the assets are not misappropriated during transactions in foreign markets.
3. Internationalization theory argues, firms engage in FDI in order to increase firm value by internalizing markets for their intangible assets. 
a. If the intangible assets are information based, like production or marketing skills, then they are assumed to behave similar to public goods in that their value increases as the internationalization scale of the firm increases.                                                                                                                                                          
4. Thus only firms that currently possess intangible assets should be expected to benefit from multinationality, i.e., the direct effect is from the intangible assets and the multinationality serves as an accentuating moderator. 
a. Morck & Yeung (2002) found support for the internationalization theory prediction that intangible assets (advertising and R&D) is critical to the value of multinationality to investors as reflected in Q ratios. 
Reverse internalization 
1. to obtain intangible assets to be utilized in the home country
2. It differs from internationalization in that a firm does not possess intangible assets prior to expanding into foreign markets but is motivated to expand/invest in these markets with the purpose of obtaining intangible assets such as skills, knowledge, and expertise to be deployed and leveraged not necessarily in the host country but in other locations, such as their home country. 
Multinational Network Hypothesis (Doukas & Travlos, 1988) 
1. To generate abnormal returns due to investors increasing their valuation of the firm`s equity because of the firm being able to achieve operational advantages through a network of global operations.
2. By diversifying globally, MNEs can:
a. arbitrage institutional restrictions
b. capture informational externalities
c. achieve cost reductions
d. production economies of scale 
3. Therefore MNE`s foreign investments can create value to the firm by achieving international operational hedging positions that are valued by investors as well as being able to capture rents and benefits arising from any systematic advantages inherent in the multinational network. 
4. The announcement of a foreign acquisition may result in abnormal returns on the acquiring firm`s stock provided that that the expansion of the firm`s operations to foreign markets tends to accomplish the investors? international-diversification objectives while enhancing the acquiring firm`s ability to benefit from the systematic advantages inherent in a multinational network (branching tree). 
5. The imperfect world capital markets theory suggests that because of barriers and information asymmetries, investors are prevented from multinational stock diversification but can use MNEs to accomplish it for them therefore impart a higher value to firms expanding internationally. 
Other reasons for internationalization:
1. Managerial objectives/agency problem: (Jensen & Meckling, 1976; Morck & Yeung, 2002; Denis, et al, 2002)
a. Managers act in a self-interested manner to increase the size and complexity of firms, which may lead to higher compensation as both these factors are associated with higher compensation. 
2. Tax avoidance and low-cost inputs: (Doukas 1995)
a. multinationalism raises the value of the firm because it permits tax avoidance and access to low-cost inputs 
3. Lower bondholder risk: the contingent claims hypothesis (Doukas & Kan, 2006)
a. the global diversification discount increases with leverage indicating that firms with diversified operations across markets are subject to lower risk 

Internalization Theory ? Morck & Yeung (1991)

the direct effect is from the intangible assets and the multinationality serves as an accentuating moderator. 

Morck & Yeung (1991) in an examination of the value of multinationality to investors as reflected in q ratios find support for the internationalization theory prediction that intangible assests (advertising and R&D) are necessary to justify FDI. Their results do not support the hypothesis that investors value multinational firms as means of diversifying their portfolios internationally. 

However, there are firms without significant intangible assets successfully engaging in FDI indicating there may be other explanations of why firms expand internationally. 


Multinational Network Hypothesis
MNCs can be expected to arbitrage institutional restrictions, capture informational externalities, achieve cost reductions, production economies of scale. Therefore MNCs are expected to create value to the extent that their foreign investments are designed to achieve international operational hedging positions that are valued by investors as well as being able to capture rents and benefits arising from any systematic advantages inherent in the multinational network. 

The announcement of a foreign acquisition may result in abnormal returns on the acquiring firm`s stock provided that that the expansion of the firm`s operations to foreign markets tends to accomplish the investors? international-diversification objectives while enhancing the acquiring firm`s ability to benefit from the systematic advantages inherent in a multinational network (branching tree). The imperfect world capital markets theory suggests that because of barriers and information asymmetries, investors are prevented from multinational stock diversification but can use MNEs to accomplish it for them therefore impart a higher value to firms expanding internationally. 

The theory was tested empirically by Doukas & Travlos (1988). Using an event study methodology, they investigated the effect of international acquisitions on stock prices of 301 US bidding firms (1975 ? 1983). The findings supported the Multinational Network Hypothesis, predicting an increase in the firm`s market value from the expansion of its existing multinational network. Specific findings are:

* Shareholders of MNCs not operating in the target firm`s country experience significant positive abnormal returns at the announcement of acquisitions
* Shareholders of US firms expanding internationally for the first time experience insignificant positive abnormal returns
* Shareholders of MNCs operating already in the target firm`s country experience insignificant abnormal returns
* Abnormal returns are larger when firms expand into new industry and geographic markets, especially those less developed than the US
* Findings are consistent with the Multinational Network Hypothesis, predicting an increase in the firm`s market value from the expansion of its existing multinational network



8. Devaluation of the Us Dollar
What are the main reasons behind the recent decline of the US dollar against the Euro? What you recommend US firms should be doing to protect shareholder value from a future depreciation of the dollar?

The recent US dollar decline against the Euro can be attributed to four main reasons:
Reasons: Demand of $ is low & supply of the $ is high --> depreciation 
    (1) America is running a huge and growing uncontrolled deficit. Current account deficit is the balance of trade in goods and services plus rent, interest, profits and dividends, and transfer payments. The trade deficit is just the net balance between imports and exports of goods. Deficit spending is taking in less than you're spending, so less foreign investments in the U.S. means more U.S. capital outflows.
    Optimists argue that foreigners will keep financing the deficit because American assets offer high returns and a haven from risk. In fact, private investors have already turned away from dollar assets: the returns on investments in America have recently been lower than in Europe or Japan. It seems that the foreign-exchange market is already positioning itself according to the difficulties that the United States will face in maintaining the current level of governmental expenditure and consumption of international tradable goods. Since the foreign exchange market perceives the future difficulty of US dollar to survive the increasingly widening current account deficit, demand for US dollar declines, resulting in the depreciation of the US dollar.
    (2) Fed monetary policy is extremely loose. According to the monetary model, while the loose monetary policy aims to trigger consumption, it also increases prices and creates inflationary expectations. A direct increase in price leads to immediate deprecation and the deprecation is magnified by expectations of future inflation. [Flood market with dollars which keeps the interest rates low.]
    (3) Low interest rates contribute to the decline of the dollar, too, our low interest rates make the US investments less attractive. Since the decline of interest rates triggers capital outflows, which leads to the dollar depreciation. When inflation hits the US investments that will slam the investments.
    (4) More and more international transactions are denominated and carried out in Euros and emerging countries, which negatively affects the demand for dollars, resulting in a decline of dollars. Since it is impossible to run a muscular foreign policy if the world starts to lose confidence in your currency, the US government should take actions to avoid the dollar crisis.

From a peak in early 2002, the inflation adjusted dollar exchange rate, for the most part, steadily depreciated. The weakening of the dollar is likely to be influenced by several factors. The following factors point to near-term depreciation of the dollar:
* Low interest rates and slow economic growth in the United States, particularly in comparison to emerging economies, likely lowers the relative expected rate of return on dollar assets.
* International holdings of dollar assets is high and prudent portfolio management could lead to diversification toward other currencies.
* A substantial trade deficit in goods continues to exert downward pressure on the dollar.
* If concerns about euro area sovereign debt problems abate, this will likely reduce recent safe-haven-motivated inflows for dollar assets.
* A growing inflation problem could induce China to slow accumulation of dollar reserves and let its currency rise relative to the dollar.
* Quantitative easing by the Federal Reserve (Fed) and fiscal stimulus passed by the Congress have had downward pressure on the dollar.
A weakening of the U.S. dollar was unavoidable given the United States? record current account deficit. The United States has had a growing trade deficit in goods transactions financed trade deficit in goods and services by sales of assets to foreigners. In the past, many of those inflows have been private investments of various kinds, but as investors around the world question whether United States government debt, and its dollars, are really worth the paper, it is increasingly difficult for us to finance our deficit with the outside world. A weakening of the demand for dollar-denominated assets by private investors tends to depreciate the dollar. A weaker demand for dollar assets is also a likely consequence of a decrease in the net inflow of foreign capital to the U.S. economy. To finance our current-account deficit, investors need to think they are buying inexpensive assets from us. Everything points to a cheaper dollar.
A depreciating currency could affect several aspects of U.S. economic performance. Possible effects include increased net exports, decreased international purchasing power, rising commodity prices, and upward pressure on interest rates; if the trend is sustained, the United states may also experience a reduction of external debt, possible undermining of the dollar`s reserve currency status, and an elevated risk of a dollar crisis. While holding some promising aspects, a falling dollar cannot alone offset inadequate U.S. and global aggregate private-sector demands. A weakening dollar, if accompanied by continuing weak U.S. consumption, soft business fixed investment, and lower interest rates, would not represent a way out low economic growth. Indeed, if accompanied by a turning away by countries from U.S. assets and loans to U.S. businesses and to the U.S. government, a falling dollar could provide the catalyst for a global recession and global deflation. Americans have long spent far more than they earn. To make ends meet, the United States must import capital. But why would foreigners with savings want to buy American bonds, stocks, property, or factories for a potentially small return when any gain they make could be easily wiped out by a big currency loss?
Unfortunately there is a definite possibility that the dollar could soon decline further or faster. The U.S. dollar is very vulnerable to further weakness on a number of counts: 1) a high, and rising, current account deficit; 2) a high, and rising, federal budget deficit, of uncertain size; 3) a weak recovery, fragile growth and stagnation; 4) low, and uncertain, returns on investments in U.S. assets, especially given downside risk to the U.S. currency; and 5) ongoing geopolitical risks and the threat of terrorism. 
The dollar has benefited from the global flight from risky assets, as well as the unwinding of bets made with borrowed dollars. That has come as a surprise to many who expected that increased government spending and a collapsing U.S economy would cripple the dollar. In the longer term, the dollar`s health remains dependent upon foreigners? appetite for U.S. assets, which will decline as the economy falters and the government continues to inject additional liquidity. Dollar weakness, plentiful liquidity and policy reflation will be persistent themes in the future. Massive fiscal and monetary stimulus has weakened the dollar, whose current resurgence stem mainly from the European debt crisis. Once that crisis rears its head in the debt-burdened United States, the dollar's weakness as a currency will be evident to all, and its role as the world's reserve currency will be in jeopardy. The prospect of substantial further depreciation of the dollar could erode the dollar`s ability to provide ?safe heaven? and important reserve currency function of being a reliable store of value. Foreign central banks may see the erosion of this function as a growing disincentive for using the dollar as their principal reserve currency. Another potential threat is any perceived unsustainability of the U.S. long-term debt problem that may eventually result in a downgrading of the U.S. sovereign-risk rating.
From the global perspective, the dollar`s exchange rate largely reflects fundamental economic forces, particularly those that influence the demand for and supply of assets on international financial markets.  Currently, an examination of those forces highlights a large and potentially destabilizing imbalance in the global economy: in the United States persistent large trade deficits and the accumulation of foreign debt, and in the rest of the world large trade surpluses, weak domestic demand, and the accumulation of dollar denominated assets.  Most economists would argue that this is a condition that carries more than a negligible risk of generating financial instability and eventual global economic crisis.
Moreover, one of the consequences of the Fed`s actions is to push down the value of the US dollar, thus worsening the competitive position of other major countries in international markets. And this may incur reactions from the major players in the world economy. China has two concerns about the fall in the value of the American dollar. It tends to push up the value of the yuan, which impacts on Chinese export markets, and reduces the value of the more than $1.2 trillion of US treasury bonds that Beijing holds. The Bank of Japan to intervene in financial markets with its own version of quantitative easing as another sign of global tensions.
To achieve an orderly correction of these imbalances that assures more stable exchange rates and leaves all the involved economies on sounder macroeconomic footing, structural reforms remain the key to further growth.

    5. Low interest rates contribute to the decline of the dollar, too, since the decline of interest rates triggers capital outflows, which leads to the dollar depreciation.
    


There are a few main reasons why the USD depreciates against the Euro. The Fed has an easy monetary policy in order to boost the economy when it suffers from recession. While the loose monetary policy aims to trigger consumption, it also increases prices and creates inflationary expectations. In 2002, the inflation rate was 1.59% but in 2008 it was 3.85%. Low interest rates will make US assets less attractive. This will cause the USD to depreciate. There are more and more international transactions denominated and carried out in Euros which means there is less demand for the USD resulting in a decline of the value of dollar

9. Describe the uncovered (i.e., Fisher open) interest rate parity condition and use it to derive the expected future spot rate, 12 months from now if the spot exchange rate is $2.04/£ and the interest rates in US and UK are Rus=4.50% and Ruk =6.25%. 
Describe the covered (i.e., Fisher closed) interest rate parity condition and use it to derive the forward rate, say, for six months.

Interest Rate Parity (Covered fisher closed):
Assumption: 
* Financial markets are perfect. There are no controls, transaction costs, taxes etc.
* Future is known with certainty
* There`s a single consumption good common to everyone
Parity condition:
* States that the difference in interest rates between two countries is equal to the expected change in exchange rates between the countries? currencies. 
* Nominal rate in a country = real rate of interest + expected future inflation rate
* If this parity does exist, there is no opportunity to arbitrage
* Arbitrage exist when interest rate difference between home & foreign country is not completely offset by the forward premium/discount of foreign currency in foreign market 
Interest parity theory: In equilibrium the premium (or discount) on a forward contract for foreign exchange is related to the interest rate differential according to:
(F-S)/S=  (i-i*)/(1+i*)
S(1+i)=F(1+i^*)

Where F & S are the forward and spot rate; i is the domestic rate of interest on a class of securities; i* is the foreign rate of interest
    When this formula is satisfied there are no profit opportunities from covered interest arbitrage (Frenkel & Levich, 1975)
	
International fisher effect (Uncovered fisher open)
* Similar to interest rate parity
* Only difference is that the forward exchange rate is replaced with the expected future spot rate 
International fisher effect (fisher open):
* States that the spot exchange rate should change to adjust for differences in interest rates between 2 countries 
* So, F (the forward exchange rate) is now equivalent to Expected Future Spot Rate 
    S(1+ i) = Expected Future Spot Rate (1+ i*)

Key Cites: 
Frenkel & Levich, 1975: 
    Previous studies of covered interest arbitrage suggest that the parity condition is not always satisfied and thus implying unexploited profit opportunities.
    Paper provides a procedure for estimating transaction costs in the markets for foreign exchange and for securities.
    Allowance for these costs accounts for most profit opportunities
    Empirical data is consistent with the interest parity theory and that covered interest arbitrage does not entail unexploited profit opportunities
    The neutral band around the interest parity line for the traditional pair accounts for most of the profit opportunities 
    Elasticities and the role of timing (lags in executing arbitrage) account for the remainder of the profit opportunities
    
    1. Doukas & Melhem (1986): new info on exchange rates
    * The response of spot and forward exchange rates to anticipated and unanticipated weekly US and Canadian money growth announcements, and the speed of adjustment of the foreign exchange market to these announcements 
    * Asset-market theory of exchange rate: 
    o The exchange rate is the relative price of two national assets rather than the price of different national outputs, determined by the demand for the supply of various national currencies
    * The unanticipated component of the announced changes in the US money supply has an immediate negative effect on the foreign value of the USD
    o This is consistent with the asset market theory of exchange rate that exchange rates immediately adjust to reflect new information
    o The response of the forward market appears to be weaker thus supporting the overshooting hypothesis
    * Exchange rates move randomly in response to new information that alters the expectations of the market about conditions that could influence exchange rates


    * MR McBrady, S Mortal, MJ Schill: Do firms believe in interest rate parity?
    Review of Finance, 2010

The author provides evidence that corporate borrowers make opportunistic currency choices since they denominate the currency of their bonds in a manner different from the generally accepted belief in either covered or uncovered interest rate parity. 

The evidence in this paper is inconsistent with the model of firm borrowing behavior where currency choice is independent of the magnitude of foreign currency borrowing rates. Using firm-level tests, the authors identify a number of characteristics of firms that engage in opportunistic behavior and observe that large issuers located in developed markets with investment grade ratings and low cash flow characterize those firms that are responsive to covered borrowing rate differences across currencies. Corporate responsiveness to uncovered borrowing rate differences appears more general. 
-international corporate bond offerings: We construct a sample of international corporate bond offerings from the Thomson Financial SDC Platinum Global New Issues dataset over the period from 1993 to 2005.
-BORROWING COST MEASURES: Interest rate parity asserts that ex-ante borrowing costs are similar across currencies such that borrowers have no incentive to seek borrowing cost advantages by selecting a low yield currency.

       Although the gains firms achieve through opportunistic currency denomination are economically significant, the yield differential tends to systematically decline after issuance. This finding suggests that opportunistic issuance by corporations may be a primary mechanism for driving covered interest yields toward parity.

       For the first large broad sample of international, foreign-currency corporate bonds, they find evidence that managers may choose across a menu of potential currencies to identify borrowing bargains based on differences in uncovered or covered interest yields. The systematic currency denomination decisions of corporate bond issuers are inconsistent with the belief that covered and uncovered interest parity holds at long horizons. The results appear reasonable in the cross section as those firms with the incentives or less barriers to opportunism tend to be those firms with more sensitivity to prevailing covered and uncovered rates. They find that the tendency for corporations to be opportunistic with prevailing uncovered yields appears to be general across the sample, although small, regulated firms with non-investment grade debt appear to be less sensitive. 
       The responsiveness to covered yields, however, is isolated to firms from developed markets that are large, have low cash flow, and investment-grade bond ratings. The gains that firms achieve are economically significant but consistent with well-functioning markets. Bond yields and exchange rates systematically move toward parity following periods of relatively high issuance. This result highlights the interesting notion that international corporate bond issuers, in fact, may effectively be the marginal traders who enforce interest rate parity at long horizons.

10.2. Delisting From Foreign Exchanges
In the recent past several foreign firms have de-listed from the NYSE. On the basis of this observation, propose a testable hypothesis, and design a tight testing procedure that consists of 3-4 stages/steps that allows you to test the merits of your hypothesis /theory.
Theory
Agency/Corporate Governance:  Firms will only cross-list in the US if it benefits the controlling shareholders; cross listing limits expropriation and increases the ability of firms to take advantage of growth opportunities: 
* Risk premium reduction
* Access to more developed capital markets
* Information disclosure
* Bonding and monitoring
Segment Hypothesis: firms in segmented markets face higher risk premium and experience higher cost of capital. Therefore, by cross-listing, they avoid the risk premium and subsequently increase firm value. 
Liquidity Hypothesis: Firms may choose to cross-list to be able to have access to more liquid markets with more investors. These foreign firms expect an increase in stock liquidity.
However, if these benefits of cross-listings disappear, firms may choose to de-list.
Hypothesis:
    H1: Firms, with lower % of turnover in NYSE after cross-listing, are more likely to voluntarily de-list.
    H1: Firms, with lower abnormal returns after cross-listing in NYSE, are more likely to voluntarily de-list.
    H2: Firms are more likely to voluntarily de-list if they cannot increase firm value.
    H3: Firms, facing higher level of regulations relative to their home country are more likely to voluntarily de-list. 
    If foreign firms listed in NYSE are facing higher level of investor protection and higher costs due to regulation
    H5: Firms, after de-listing from NYSE, may experience negative abnormal returns at home country. 
    Decrease in returns in their home country because de-listing signals information that these companies didn?t perform well in the NYSE
Measurements:
IV: Liquidity Measure: Turnover rate of foreign firms? stocks listed in NYSE; Cumulative Abnormal Return: Mean Adjusted Returns; Firm Value: Tobin`s Q, Investor Protection Index; SOX Implication: legal expense from 10k
DV: Voluntary de-listing: CRSP
CV: B/M ratio, Market Capitalization, Segmentation of the markets
Segmentation= liberalization (trade, money market)

10.3 Why Several U.S firms de-listed their shares from the Tokyo stock exchange? Is this beneficial to shareholders? Discuss.
    There are a few reasons why firms delist their shares from the Tokyo exchange. Firstly, as Alexander et al. (1988) argue shareholders benefit from dual listing when the financial markets are segmented. In other words, if US stocks are inaccessible for the Japanese due to higher transaction cost then it is plausible to do dual listing. However, when the segmentation diminishes then the necessity of dual listing is questionable. There are further reasons why firms delist their shares. Recession in the Japanese economy reduced the attractiveness of dual listing in Japan. Moreover, China`s fast growth made American firms to switch locations. In conclusion, there is a cost involved with dual listing. Basically, if benefits aren?t worth costs, then managers will not engage in cross-listing.

    Building on Alexander et al (1988) one may argue that globalization and cross listing will be negatively related. In other words, when the markets are segmented foreign firms tend to make their stocks available in the US. However, since by the help of the Internet, one can buy sell stocks from any country. Thus foreign firms do not need to engage in cross listing. This is a very strong assumption, however. Firms from foreign countries may still cross-list for prestige or to envy others. Still, in my opinion, there is room for research. One of the reasons why managers cross-list is to raise more capital and/or to reduce cost of capital. Hence, this gives managers more tolerant rooms to make investment decision given disposal cheap capital, such as to invest in lower rate of return projects.   In other words, cheaper cost of capital access may harm shareholder`s value. 
ANSWER
Why firms pursue overseas listings
* The conventional wisdom of the market segmentation theory 
* Alexander et al. (1988): The net benefits to cross-listing firms stem from their decision to overcome barriers to international capital flow due to market segmentation with government controls on foreign exchange and capital market. The access to advanced capital markets reduces negative effects that allow foreign firms to raise funds at lower cost than at home:
* Dual listing allows US investors saving any transaction costs associated with dealing in foreign currency.
* The listing in advanced stock exchanges also results in a reduction of information cost: disclosure requirement by SEC and security analysts? activities following international listing.
* A broader shareholder base helps to enhance investor recognition and reduce the shadow cost of incomplete information, lower bid-ask spread and hence lower cost of capital.
* Liquidity effects: developed capital markets are more efficient and liquid than home markets. Market orders are executed with a greater immediacy and higher trading volume.
* Signaling effects: international listing reflects firm`s ability to meet the high disclosure standards establishing that they are higher value firms.
* [TC, partnership]
* However the prevailing explanations based on investment barriers and segmented markets were inadequate and offered several other possible explanations. 
* Changing Market Risk Exposures and The Cost of Capital hypothesis
* A number of studies argued that shares of a cross-listing firm may experience significant changes in its local and global market risk exposures and its cost of capital.
* Doukas and Witzer (2000) focus on the period surrounding the public announcements rather than the listing day, using a sample of Canadian companies listing in the US, show that:
o Firms operating in mildly segmented capital markets can attain a reduction of risk premium through international stock listings leading to a higher equilibrium market price and a lower expected return.
o Their empirical study also supports the view that international stock listing increases firm shareholder base and reduces cost of capital.
    * Bonding hypothesis [why firms pursue cross listing.][Bonding is like bonding somebody to behave properly. Monitoring is the cost to the employer. Bonding is cost to the agents.]
* A number of papers in literature had suggested that:
o Foreign firms are generally controlled by large shareholders and U.S. listing enhances the protection of the investors, reduces the agency costs of controlling shareholders.
o The higher disclosure standard of the US makes it harder for controlling shareholders to expropriate cash flows. Consequently, listing in the U.S. is expensive for controlling shareholders of firms with poor growth opportunities.
o The bonding and monitoring benefit of listing exist even in perfectly integrated capital markets.
    * Doidge (2004) in his empirical study supports the bonding hypothesis: 
o Cross-listing in the U.S. improves the protection for minority investors and decreases the private benefits. He also shows that for non-U.S. firms that cross-list on U.S. exchanges have voting premiums lower than non-U.S. 
o Firms that do not cross-list and both the high- and low-voting share classes benefit but the low-voting class benefits more. 
    * Doidge, Karolyi and Stulz (2004) again offer empirical support for the bonding hypothesis: 
o Listing in the US exchange reduces the expropriation by controlling shareholders and increases the firm`s ability to take advantage of growth opportunities. 
o The premium is related to future growth opportunities, especially for companies from countries with poorer legal protections.
* A positive link between the information environment and cross-listing hypothesis
* Literature documented that a firm`s commitment to a higher level of disclosure associated with cross-listing can alter the incentives of informed market participants to collect and trade on private information, and thereby influence a firm`s stock price formation process.
* Fernandes and Ferreira (2008) investigate stock price informativeness of cross-listing firms in the U.S. and find that:
o Cross-listing improves price informativeness for developed market firms.
o For firms in emerging markets, however, cross-listing decreases price informativeness. [They get less of a premium]
o Price informativeness increases the most for firms in countries with the greatest investor protection. 

C. Related Articles:
    
    2 Alexander et al (1988) international Listings and Stock Returns:  Some Empirical Evidence
Summary: This paper investigates stock returns surrounding international listings. They find that international listings are accompanied by a reduction in its expected return provided that markets are partially/fully segmented.

Theory:
1. Partial/full segmentation:  If markets are not fully integrated, the stock prices should react to international listings: Barriers to international portfolio investment can be mitigated with cross-listing, Transactions costs are lower, Information costs are lower. 
2. Previous research has shown that international listing leads to the reduction in the expected return on the security if capital markets are completely/partially segmented

Results: International listing accompanies a reduction in the expected return on the stock and particularly for non-Canadian stocks because: Non-Canadian stock markets are more segmented than Canadian stock markets, and it could be that non-/Canadian stock markets are similarly segments but the Canadian market is more highly correlated with the US market.

3. Doidge, Karolyi & Stulz (2004) Why are foreign firms listed in the US worth more?
Summary: At the end of 1997, foreign companies, with shares cross-listed in the U.S., have Tobin`s Q ratios that were 16% higher than the Q`s of non-cross-listed firms from the same country. The valuation difference is statistically significant and reaches 37% for those companies that list on major US exchanges, even controlling for firm and country characteristics. They suggest that US listing reduces expropriation and increases the firm`s ability to exploit growth opportunities. They show that growth opportunities are more highly valued for firms that choose to cross-list in the US, particularly those from countries with poor investor rights. [Counter argument for the delisting. When reasons don't hold, this applies.]

Theory: 
Agency/Corporate Governance:  Firms will only cross-list in the US if it benefits the controlling shareholders; cross listing limits expropriation and increases the ability of firms to take advantage of growth opportunities: 
* Risk premium reduction
* Access to more developed capital markets
* Information disclosure
* Bonding and monitoring
Results: Firms that cross-list in the US have higher valuations (Q) than non-cross listed firms. Firms that cross-list have a price run-up prior to the listing. 

These two papers are explaining why foreign firms choose to enlist in U.S. stock markets. Therefore, when the benefits of enlisting in U.S. disappears, foreign firms may choose de-list from U.S. stock markets.

D. Hypothesis:

Segment Hypothesis: According to the segment hypothesis, firms in segmented markets face higher risk premium and experience higher cost of capital. Therefore, by cross-listing, they are actually trying to avoid the risk premium and subsequently increase firm value. 

Liquidity Hypothesis: Firms may choose to cross-list to be able to have access to more liquid markets with more investors. These foreign firms expect an increase in stock liquidity.
However, if these benefits of cross-listings disappear, firms may choose to de-list.
   H1: Firms, with lower percentage of turnover in NYSE after cross-listing, are more likely to voluntarily de-list.
   H2: Firms, with lower realized abnormal returns after cross-listing in NYSE, are more likely to voluntarily de-list.
H3: Firms are more likely to voluntarily de-list if they cannot increase firm value.
Firms may also choose to de-list due to regulatory environment. For example, foreign firms which are listed in NYSE are facing higher level of investor protection and higher costs due to regulation. Related hypothesis can be structured like this:
   H4: Firms, facing higher level of regulations relative to their home country are more likely to voluntarily de-list.
