5.7 Why Several U.S firms de-listed their shares from the Tokyo stock exchange? Is this beneficial to shareholders? Discuss.

    5) Many U.S. firms listed in Tokyo stock exchange to establish reputation and open up to new and attractive product markets for future investment and trade. However, when Japan lost this attractiveness, U.S. firms de-listed from Tokyo stock exchange. U.S. firms enjoyed first mover advantage, but after Tokyo stock market became easily accessible they choose to de-list.
    
    6) Capital Market Segmentation is a financial market imperfection, mainly caused by government restrictions, investor perception and institutional practices. Most important results of segmentation are high transaction costs, political risks, foreign exchange risk, lack of transparency, corporate governance and high regulatory barriers. Because of segmentation, U.S. stocks can be inaccessible for Japanese investors due to high transaction costs, information costs, and high government controls (Stulz, 1995 and Alexander et al, 1988). Capturing this demand in Japan can be a reason for U.S. firms to establish presence in Japanese stock markets. However, after the integration of Japanese stock markets into the world markets, Japanese investors can access to U.S. markets on their own. Therefore, the decreased demand for the U.S. stocks in Japan might result with U.S. firms? de-listings.
    7) The economic recession in Japan reduced the benefits of enlisting in Tokyo stock exchange.
    8) Currency sentiment may be another reason for delisting of U.S. firms. When Japanese economy was booming, many U.S. firms entered Japanese markets to build up wealth, reduce the cost of capital, and create value. However, after Japan started to experience slowing down which is followed by the booming in Chinese economy, currency sentiment chang ed from Japan to China.
De-listing from Japanese stock market is found to be motivated by value creation incentives rather than managerial incentives.    

11.Empirical evidence shows that US corporations realize abnormal gains from issuing Euro-debt. If this is true, why they issue only a small fraction relative to their domestic market, or why don't they raise more debt in the offshore markets? Discuss 
    1. The clientele hypothesis (Kim & Stulz, 1988) 
    a. states that Firms can increase shareholder wealth by exploiting their comparative advantage in providing securities that are high in demand by certain financial clientele (catering to a specific cliental group). 
    b. implies that For periods of time firms can borrow at lower costs in the Eurobond market than in the domestic market. 
    i. Take advantage of a lower interest rate
    ii. Europeans are the financial clientele
    iii. The hypothesis is about increasing shareholder wealth. The US investors are willing to pay a premium to issue debt ? issuing cost is high
    2. In a fully integrated market, no differential cost of borrowing should exist in different markets. However, in imperfect worldwide markets, advantages of the Eurobond market create higher demand for its securities:
a. bearer bonds that are not registered ? investor privacy (Investor anonymity [People are more willing to pay more for the anonymity])
    b. not subjected to withholding tax ? tax evasion
    c. investor risk diversification ? less risk
    d. only accessible to good-credited companies ? company reputation
    3. Therefore, U.S firms can benefit by raising capital offshore (reducing cost of capital and increasing value). In other words, bargains survive because when demand for the Eurobonds form foreign investors is high, foreign investors compete for them and hence bid up their prices
Why Eurobond market is limited:
    4. Accessible only to good credited firms 
    a. Because of the loose control, only the firms that have high reputation can be able to issue successfully and attract investors in those markets.
    5. Excessive bond issuance will destroy firm reputation & value
    a. Excessive issuance will have the potential to decrease the creditworthiness. 
    i. Therefore, US firms Cannot issue too much because if they do so they are signaling to the rest of the market that they have trouble in the US market. 
    ii. If firms keep issuing overseas, investors might think that they are trying to avoid US regulations. 
    1. More precisely, higher percentage of debt from Eurobonds may signal the inability of firm to meet disclosure performance and reporting requirements in the US.
    6. Euro-bonds are costlier than issuing domestic bonds 
    a. contracting & enforcement costs are higher 
    b. issuing costs are higher by 1-2% 
Doukas mentioned that Clientele is a permanent effect; Catering is short term in nature. In fact, the clientele hypothesis should have been called Catering effect. 



7.2 Eurobonds
    According to the clientele hypothesis firms can increase shareholder wealth by exploiting their comparative advantage in providing securities that are high in demand by certain financial clienteles.
Normally, in a fully integrated market no differential cost of borrowing should exist in different market. However, in real life to some extend there are imperfections and these have two major advantages. First Eurobond market creates higher demand for its securities, which are withholding taxes and investor anonymity. Because of these reasons there is high demand for US bonds in foreign markets but since the supply is constant in short term, US firms can exploit high gains from these markets.  
    Kim & Stulz (1988) studied this issue. They found, on average, positive abnormal returns after 183 Eurobond issues over the period of 1975-1985 as compared to zero or negative reactions to domestic bond issues reported by earlier research.
However, such markets are available for firms with high reputation. Because of the loose control, only the firms that have high reputation can be able to issue successfully and attract investors in those markets.
Further, if the firm raises more capital from Eurobond markets then it does from domestic markets, lenders may think that the firm has some problems. More precisely, higher percentage of debt from Eurobonds may signal the inability of firm to meet disclosure, performance and reporting requirements in the US. This is why firms only issue small fraction relative to the domestic debt in offshore markets.  



KEY CITES:
Kim and Stulz (1988) found positive abnormal returns on euro bond issues, but did not find wreck support for the clientele affect. The authors found on average positive abnormal returns post the 183
Eurobond issues over the period of 1975-1985 as compared to zero or negative reactions to domestic bond issues reported by earlier research.

The clientele hypothesis states that firms can increase shareholder wealth by exploiting their comparative advantage in providing securities that are high in demand by certain financial clientele. The idea behind this is whenever opportunities arise, managers of reputable firms carter to clients (lenders). This hypothesis implies that for periods of time some firms can borrow at lower costs in the Eurobond market than in the domestic market. [Take advantage of a lower interest rate] [Europeans are the financial clientele][The hypothesis is about increasing shareholder wealth. The US investors are willing to pay a premium to issue debt]

In a fully integrated market, no differential cost of borrowing should exist in different markets, but some imperfections take place between US and European markets. There are two main advantages or imperfections of the Eurobond market which creates higher demand for its securities:
3. Tax benefits - are tax free:
4. Investor anonymity (unknown) [People are more willing to pay more for the anonymity]

Therefore, U.S firms can benefit by raising capital offshore (reducing cost of capital and increasing value). In other words, bargains survive because when demand for the Eurobonds from foreign investors is high, foreign investors compete for them and hence bid up their prices

Dr.  Doukas mentioned that Clientele is a permanent effect; Catering is short term in nature. In fact, the clientele hypothesis should have been called Catering effect. 

In perfect market, U.S. firms must be indifferent between borrowing dollar abroad or at home. If the supply of dollar-denominated bonds issued abroad is not perfectly elastic in the short run, however, an unexpected increase in the demand for these securities creates profitable financing opportunities. 
Kim & Stulz (1988) find that impediments to the adjustment of asset (Euro-bonds) supplies to new demand conditions are large enough to create profitable financing opportunities for US issuing firms. European investors compete for Euro-bonds because bearer bonds enable European holders to remain anonymous and thus escape detection by fiscal authorities and not subject to withholding tax as domestic bonds issued to foreigners. The privacy and tax advantages of Eurobonds make foreign investors willing to hold them even when they have a lower expected return than domestic bonds with comparable risk. 
Since foreign investors compete for Eurobonds, the borrowing cost for a U.S. firm in the Eurobond market depends on the elasticity of the supply of these Eurobonds. This implies that for periods of time some firms can borrow at lower costs in the Eurobond market than in the domestic market. And the evidence is, on average, firms that issued fixed-rate debt on the Eurobond market increased their shareholders' wealth during a portion of the sample period. Most of the positive abnormal stock returns, however, occur between 1979 and 1984, when the yield spread between domestic and offshore bonds was exceptionally large. Since the abnormal returns are positively related over time to the yield spread, Kim & Stulz (1988) attribute them to the ability of firms with a reputation for low default risk to exploit temporary financing opportunities.
Why Eurobond market is limited:
The Euro-bond market is only accessible to firms with a good reputation. For U.S. corporations, contracting and issuing costs are higher for Eurobonds than for domestic bonds. Differences in contracting and issuing costs between the Eurobond and the domestic bond markets make the supply of Eurobonds imperfectly elastic. Therefore, bargains are not instantly arbitraged away on the Eurobond market because the supply of securities on that market is not perfectly elastic in the short run, leading investors to accept a lower yield than would prevail with a perfectly elastic supply curve. 
Although U.S. firms can exploit differences in yields across markets, there are limits to acting quickly. A firm must have a reputation for presenting little risk of default to take advantage of these yield differences, then it can act only insofar as selling more debt does not damage that reputation. This places a natural limit on each firm's ability to benefit from Eurobond financing opportunities. The difference between the contracting and issuing costs of Eurobond and domestic bond issues is negatively related to the borrower's reputation. The reputation of the marginal borrower on the Eurobond market is determined by the yield spread between the two markets. Firms without such a reputation can only acquire it over time. Further, bargains are often small enough that only firms with the requisite reputational capital that have already planned to issue debt can profit.



Q4
QUESTIONS APPEAR IN MOST EXAMS
2015-While US firms have intensified their overseas expansion in recent years, in an attempt to enhance their market share and profitability, (Denis et al 2002) have recently show that US multinational firms trade at a discount. The evidence of this study is in sharp contrast with most of the previous empirical evidence that do not show that US multinationals lose shareholder value as a result of expanding abroad. Discuss (20 Points)

In their paper, (Denis et al 2002) argues that although internalization has benefits such as internalizing value added resources across national borders (internalization theory), attracting investors who wants to diversify their portfolio and increasing flexibility against country specific limits such as taxes and regulations; internationally diversified companies are traded at discount due to various managerial costs such as information asymmetry, coordination, monitoring and traditional agency costs. Similar finding is also found by (Jensen 1986)?s ?Free Cash Flow? Theory which argues that substantial cash flows leads managers to invest in negative NPV projects. But these finding is inconsistent with other studies and observations. If internationalization is destroying firm value, then why companies keep expanding their operations.

First of all (Denis et al 2002) has some methodological problems. They use excess value based on book value of debt. This creates a downward bias for MNEs due to their higher debt capacity compared to domestic companies. But more importantly (Denis et al 2002) neither discriminate expansions nor companies. However literature show that depending on firm and country characteristics, internationalization provides different result.

First firm characteristic is capital structure. (Doukas & Kan 2006) argues that (Denis et al 2002) is looking from only one perspective.  Based on contingent claims hypothesis, they argue that bondholders and shareholders have conflicting interests. Global diversification is acting like an insurance for bondholders where premium is paid by shareholders. In addition, low leveraged companies do not suffer from discount. This insurance argument is also confirmed by (Kuppuswamy & Villalonga 2010). In their study, (Kuppuswamy & Villalonga 2010) found that global crisis is more severe for focused domestic firms while advantageous for multinationals and conglomerates. At time of crises both ?more money effect? and ?smart money effect? are observed. Multinationals and conglomerates had more efficient internal capital markets and they can hold more debt	
Second firm characteristics is quality of management. Endogeneity problem could occur when it comes to relationship between firm value and internationalization. Just as well managed companies prefer to go overseas in order to enlarge their success, poor managed companies also prefer international expansion to escape from competition. (Doukas 1995) shows that better managed companies (high Tobin`s Q) have higher return when they expand (highest when they don?t have any pre-existing operations) and poorly managed companies (low Tobin`s Q) have lower value (lowest when they have existing operations) when they expand. 

And thirdly strength of intangible assets is an important firm characteristics. Based on internalization theory, (Morck & Yeung 1991) found that internationalization itself does not create value but only when it is interacting with intangible assets such as research and development and/or marketing. In other words, investors do not appreciate significantly traditional advantages such tax avoidance, indirect portfolio diversification, access to low cost inputs but utilization of company`s intangible assets in broader markets. 

Where firm expands is also important factor for internationalization results. Based on multinational network hypothesis (Doukas & Travlos 1988), found that although firms suffer from insignificant value losses when they expand into countries where they have already operations, they experience significant value increases when they expand into new markets (preferably emerging) and new industries. 

Tax policies of target country is also an important factor. As (Doukas 1995) shows, foreign acquisitions in low tax rate jurisdiction have positive impact on firm value. Moreover, higher tax rates in US encourages MNEs to keep their profits overseas and use these oversea profits in expansion of their operations. 

In summary, global diversification increases firm value however many firm characteristics (intangible assets, capital structure, previous activities, quality of managers) and country characteristics (level of development, existence of prior operations, tax rates) are heavily influencing the outcome.

2015-Describe the differences between the internalization theory and multinational network hypothesis? (10 Points)
Multinational network hypothesis argues that key driver for MNEs? expansion is building a network. By building a multinational network, firms can arbitrage national restrictions (taxes, regulations etc.), capture informational externalities, utilizing operational hedges, accessing low cost materials and enjoy economics of scale. From investor perspective, they can use MNEs as an instrument to diversify their portfolios thus benefit from systematic advantages of being a multinational company as argued by imperfect world capital model. 

In their study (Doukas & Travlos 1988) tests this model. If multinational network hypothesis is true, then with the announcement of foreign acquisition, company will attract investors and company will perform abnormal returns as predicted by both multinational network hypothesis and imperfect world capital model. What (Doukas & Travlos 1988) found is that not all international expansions are same. Most appreciated MNEs are better managed companies (High Tobin`s Q) those already have some kind of international operations but expanding into that country for the first time and preferably emerging markets.  

According to internalization theory, firm-specific intangible assets of MNEs are key drivers of international expansion. When market imperfections and other transaction costs prevent MNEs to utilize their assets, MNEs invest in other countries directly. Like any other public good, these intangible assets such as production or marketing skills enjoy economies of scale and their values are appreciated when MNEs internalize those markets. Internationalization could also occur in a reverse way. In these cases, firm does not possess intangible assets prior expansion but expand in order to obtain intangible assets such as skills, expertise and other knowledge-based resources in order to utilize them in other countries including home market. 

In their study (Morck & Yeung 1991) examined the internalization theory. They found that investors are not appreciating multinationality itself or other multinationality advantages such as using MNEs to diversify their portfolios, but investing more in companies those have high marketing and production assets (R&D spending and advertising are used as proxy). Therefore there must be other explanations for why companies with less intangible assets expand overseas such as reverse internalization, managerial incentives or they just want to escape from home market competition.

I think both theories are capturing some aspect of MNEs? expansion both looking from different perspectives. In 1970s where there was huge restrictions of capital market and goods markets, investors would use MNEs as a proxy for diversifying portfolios. However investors would also like to know whether international expansion is a result of success in home market or just a tool for escaping home market competition or related to agency incentives. Therefore companies which have high valued intangible assets would benefit most from international expansion. Moreover, if MNEs are utilizing their intangible assets by going overseas, then the marginal benefit would be higher if they are new to market. 

2015-When you investigate the impact of a decision (i.e. investment, capitals structure change, foreign acquisition, among others on corporate performance it is always advisable to test for endogeneity of that decision. Explain why it is important to do so and what econometric procedure would you adopt? (10 Points)
In a statistical model, a parameter or variable is said to be endogenous when there is a correlation between the parameter or variable and the error term. In other words loop causality between independent and dependent variables of a model leads to endogeneity. This is a problem since it violates one of the central assumptions of regression. Endogeneity could affect finance research in various ways. For example company`s strategy is dependent on firm-specific characteristics and industry conditions. Thus, if we argue that diversification leads to value discount we have to be sure that diversification leads to discount not low value firms choose to diversify. If we don?t take account to endogeneity we can find biased and erroneous results. 

There are several steps to address endogeneity problem. First step is determining which variables leads to endogeneity and why. There are three factors leading endogeneity. First one is omitted variables, variables should be included in the groups of independent variables to predict the dependent variables, but are not. Omitted variables can be correlated with any of the independent variables which results to correlation between composite error term and independent variables. For example, we could argue that good credit rating is associated with more leverage; however good credit rating is correlated with the size and size is positively correlated with the leverage. Second one is simultaneity resulting from ambiguous effects between independent variables. For example firms with high investment opportunities that can lead to an underinvestment problem have more incentive to hedge because hedging can alleviate it. On the other hand lower investment opportunities can lead to problem of larger free cash flow, which create more incentive to hedge. Third and final factor is measurement error. For example if we calculate leverage with book value of debt, we will have problem.

Second step is developing an instrumental variable that is correlated with independent variables but not with the dependent variable. We shall run a regression to determine the dependent variable and plug it back into the original equation. Most frequently used procedure is 2SLS (Two stage least square) regression; however 3SLS regressions are also used. There are also methods such as panel data with fixed effects and Hausman test.


2015-Discuss foreign exchange risk hedging in the eyes of corporate managers and shareholders (20 Points)
a) For shareholders
Foreign exchange risk exposure can be defined as sensitivity of firm value to exchange rate movements. All assets are exposed to exchange rate risk. It is highest for short term monetary assets denominated in foreign currencies and lowest in fixed real assets independent of their location. Although a firm may not have any foreign transaction, it could be effected indirectly from exchange rate movements. 

According to modern portfolio theory, foreign exchange risk is not appreciated by investors in a perfect world. Although hedging stabilizes earnings, it shall not change cost of capital and risk management is irrelevant to the firm`s shareholders who can do it on their own. However as (Doukas 1989) points out the world imperfect, thus although investors could diversify unsystematic risks, they cannot diversify systematic risk. Therefore they shall be ready to price the hedging. Hedging has various advantages. Currency hedging ensure the firm has sufficient internal funds in the presence of high underinvestment cost, reduces financial stress and cost of debt & capital. Its stabilizing effects aids in corporate planning.  

Impact of currency hedging and whether appreciation of investor is little bit controversial. According to (Jorion 1990, 1991), there is a positive relationship between degree of international involvement and hedging activities however hedging is not priced by shareholders. However (Allayannis & Weston 2001) finds that there is a positive relationship between use of foreign currency derivatives and firm value. (Guay & Kothari 2003) points out that use of derivatives and exchange risk is relatively small for overall cash flow and firm value. Thus unless currency exposure and return of hedging is large enough, it will not impact firm value. (Bartov & Bodnar 1994) looks from another perspective and find that there is no abnormal returns with contemporaneous changes of dollar, but changes in dollar impacts firm value in a lagged way. Similar finding is also found by (Chow et al 1997) which argues that stock returns are only sensitive to long-run changes. 

MNEs could also use operational hedging as an alternative to financial hedging. However (Allayannis et al 2001) finds that operational hedging itself doesn?t create firm value but only combined with financial hedging.

Hedging has also various disadvantages. First reason is hedging have costs. Secondly hedging does not increase the cash flow of firm only reduce instability. And third reason is that managers could hedge in the expense of shareholders. Unless a firm faces one of the following situations, high cost of financial distress, high underinvestment cost, or high currency risk exposure, the use of currency hedging is questionable. Even if one of these conditions exists, currency risk hedging may not add value to the firm. If investors do not have any preference regarding currency risk hedging then they will not pay a premium for such an activity. Further, if investors think that hedging risk is diversifiable then they may even discount hedging activities. Since low risk is favored by debt holders, currency risk hedging might transfer wealth from shareholders to bondholders (Guay & Kothari 2003). Hence, unless there is no serious risk, shareholders will not value hedging.

In summary whether firms shall hedge is depending on various factors. Although hedging provides some benefits, it is not worth doing it and appreciated by investors unless firm has significant foreign exchange exposure and international involvement. Moreover, impact of foreign exchange movements is observed in a lagged way since investors? are relatively insensitive to daily volatiles in currency rates and information is often unavailable until the time of disclosure. 
b) For managers
According to agency theory managers have self- oriented incentives (Jensen & Meckling 1976) and are usually more risk averse than the investors. Therefore, they care about their personal benefits more than stockholder wealth. Managers are usually better informed about structural imperfections as compared to individual investors, and they can take advantage of the disequilibrium conditions through selective hedging. In the presence of transaction costs, managers have cheaper access to hedging as compared to individual shareholders.

In their study (Knopf et al; 2002) finds a positive relationship between managers? stock option portfolio and hedging. Since managers are more risk averse compared to shareholders; as sensitivity of the CEO's total portfolio increases derivative usage increases. If the stock return volatility is high, they will be willing to take risks and not hedge. On the other hand the stock price volatility is low they won?t take risks and hedge, since this could lead to the value of their equity declining.


2015-Recent empirical evidence shows that stock trading in less developed and emerging markets is characterized by high levels of synchronicity and explain why such a phenomenon exists (10 points)
There are be several explanations for stock synchronicity. First explanation is level of development and other market fundamentals.  For example in early 20th century example synchronicity of US stock markets were comparable to emerging markets today, however synchronicity declined over time. Small and less developed economies with little diversification leads to stock synchronicity. However market fundamentals are not sufficient to explain whole picture as (Morck et al 2000) (Piotriski & Roulstone 2004) (Chan & Hameed 2006) show.

Firms and stock markets run differently in emerging markets. As (Morck et al 2000) points out emerging markets does not provide strong property rights which deters risk arbitrage and encourages noise trading. Thus, political events and rumors in those countries lead to market-wide stock price swings. Moreover, high insider trading makes firm specific information less useful and less contributing to firms? market capitalization

Third explanation is poor disclosure standards (Chan & Hameed 2006). In emerging markets, disclosure standards are low and in many cases companies do not disclose information more than the laws required. Moreover these companies are often family companies and analysts lack required network to gather firm-specific information. Under these conditions, analysts have to rely on market-wide information which are less discriminate within securities. Thus as number of analysts coverage increases, stock price synchronicity increases. 


Why foreign firms list their shares in advanced capital markets such as the NYSE and/or LSE? 
In addition, why during the last seven years most foreign firms have decided to list their stock shares outside the US capital market? (10 Points)
One of them refers to the benefits associated with reducing agency costs as a result of increased disclosure requirements and investor protection. Discuss (10 Points)
There are several drivers behind cross listing in advanced capital markets such as NYSE and LSE. According to market segmentation theory (Alexander et al; 1988), gains from cross listing would be zero if world capital markets are perfectly integrated and maximum if capital markets are completely disintegrated. Thus, by cross listing firms are overcoming barriers of capital flow. Cross listing allows firm diversify their risk and can gain advantage due to national differences (taxes, regulations and institutions)

However imperfect world capital markets is not the only motivation behind cross listing. Cross listing provides advantages to both existing and potential investors of firms and firms? management. Cross listing broadens investors? base, reduces cost of capital and provide growth opportunities. As (Stultz; 1995) showed in Nestle case, small investor base means higher expected rate of return for investors and lower stock price for the company. From mathematical perspective local CAPM overestimates cost of capital.

From investors? perspective their transaction cost is reduced since they do not have to go to abroad and deal with foreign currencies. Since advanced capital markets have more depth and liquidity, bid-ask spreads are lowered, orders are executed with a greater immediacy. Information cost is also reduced due to high disclosure requirements of advanced capital markets. Thus investors and analysts has gain access to more firm-specific information which makes markets more efficient. Cross listing also signals that firms have ability to satisfy these standards, thus boost firms? reputation and credibility (Doidge et al; 2004).

Cross listing also means lower agency cost and better corporate governance. Emerging markets MNEs are often family corporations where controlling shareholders generally manages the company with the expense of interests of minority shareholders. Since their home country does not provide strong legal and regularity framework for protecting public investors, insider trading is also popular. High disclosure requirements means bonding (?bond? to behave properly) and monitoring of the firm and its managers and protection for the investor, thus reduced agency cost for investors.  

We can also talk about managerial incentives behind cross listing.  With cross listing; firms become larger, more visible and more prestigious. Thus managers? compensations are likely to grow with size of the company so as their future employment opportunities.

If some firms are delisting from NYSE or LSE, this shows that their cost of listing in advanced capital markets outweighs their benefits. As (Doidge et al; 2004) point out controlling shareholders of emerging market MNEs are sacrificing their control over for growth opportunities. Therefore this delisting decision shall be result of lack of growth, increasing costs regarding cross listing or macro factors about NYSE and LSE. So we have to reverse the logic why they cross listed in the first place. For example imperfect world capital market argument is much weaker compared to previous decades. This is a double edge sword. While other financial markets gain depth due to this integration but also began to be effected by shocks. Another factor could be that other financial markets are trying to adopt disclosure requirements of advanced capital markets. Thirdly trust toward regulatory institutions such as SEC could be weakened in recent years due to scandals. From firm-specific point of view if growth opportunities are not worth for losing their control over the company and legal costs of cross listing then firms will delist. In their study (Sarkissian & Schill 2009) found that in most cases there are no significant permanent valuation gains in cross-listing
Define the uncovered interest rate parity condition and use it to derive the expected future spot rate 12 months from now if the spot exchange rate is $2.04/£ and the interest rate in US and UK are R$=4.50% and R£ =6.25% (10 Points)
According to covered interest rate parity, in equilibrium condition, premium or discount on a forward rate is offset by interest rate differential (Frenkel & Lewin; 1975).
(Forward Rate-Spot Rate)/(Spot Rate)=(Domestic Interest Rate-Foreign Interest Rate)/(1+Foreign Interest Rate)
In other words if equilibrium exists, then there is no opportunity for arbitrage. Previous research argues that covered condition does not hold in many cases thus firms are not exploiting profit opportunities. However (Frenkel & Lewin; 1975) argues that interest rate parity is not a line but a zone due to transaction costs (ask-bid spread, brokerage fees, time lags and inelasticity of supply and demand).

In uncovered interest rate parity, investors do not use a forward contract to lock the exchange rate, thus they are not ?covered? from exchange risk. Therefore in uncovered interest rate parity instead of forward rate, expected future spot rate is used. 
       (Expected Future Spot Rate)/(Spot Rate)=((1+Domestic Interest Rate))/((1+Foreign Interest Rate))

       Expected Future Spot Rate=$2.04£?((1+0.0450))/((1+0.0625))=$2.0064£
       
Frenkel & Levich, 1975: 
    Previous studies of covered interest arbitrage suggest that the parity condition is not always satisfied and thus implying unexploited profit opportunities.
       
    * Asset-market theory of exchange rate: 
    o The exchange rate is the relative price of two national assets rather than the price of different national outputs, determined by the demand for the supply of various national currencies
    o This is consistent with the asset market theory of exchange rate that exchange rates immediately adjust to reflect new information
    o The response of the forward market appears to be weaker thus supporting the overshooting hypothesis
       



State a testable hypothesis (a theoretical prediction) in the context of international finance. Then describe your testing procedure, consisting of 3-4 stages/steps that would allow you to assess the merits of your hypothesis. (10 Points)
a) He generally does not mention the topic but in 2007 he specifically talked about foreign firms delisting from NYSE in 2007
Theoretical Background
If companies are decided to delist from NYSE, this means that they did not experience expected net benefits from cross listing in NYSE. On one hand they are limiting effects of their segment home market and gaining access to more liquid and developed financial market, thus reducing risk premium and provides growth opportunities. However these developed markets also have higher disclosure standards, bonding & monitoring is taken more seriously which is expensive and can be against interests of controlling shareholders. In summary controlling shareholder of emerging market companies are sacrificing their expropriation of minority shareholders for growth opportunities (Doidge et al; 2004) 
Hypotheses
For the growth opportunities not only they underperformed in NYSE but they are also expecting not to underperform significantly after delisting. Thus following hypotheses are proposed
Hypothesis 1a: If NYSE cross-listed firms are subjected to lower abnormal return, lower turnover and lower firm value, they are likely to delist from NYSE
Hypothesis 1b: Delisted companies are not subjected to lower abnormal return, lower turnover and lower firm values
Related data could be gathered from various sources such as COMPUSTAT, CRSP, ThomsonOne WorldScope and firms? financial reports.

As said, cross listing in NYSE creates problems for controlling shareholders and costs for the company. Thus following hypothesis is proposed 
Hypothesis 2: If NYSE cross-listed firms are from emerging markets that have insufficient legal framework & property rights, they are more likely to delist from NYSE

Data
Related data could be gathered from income statements of the firms. The more problem they have, their legal expenditures section on their income statement will be bigger. Of course interviews with top managers of a few company could be beneficial and enrich the research as primary data. 
Analysis
Logistic regression shall be preferred since dependent variable is binary 1 for delist, 0 for listing.


QUESTIONS APPEAR IN SOME EXAMS
2015-Managerial Decisions are influenced by envy. While several explanations have been given about international corporate expansion, it`s quite likely that CEO envy might have been played an important goal behind foreign investment (merges and acquisitions) activity. Postulate/formulate the basis of a CEO envy testable hypothesis, on the basis of this view and design a testing procedure that consists of a 3-4 stages to draw inferences about the CEO envy effect (i.e. the link between CEO envy and foreign mergers and acquisitions (20 Points)
Theoretical Background
Agency related theories argues for two managerial related reasons for international expansion. According to stewardship model, CEOs (and top management teams) expand firms? operations in overseas when expansion benefits shareholders (Donaldson & Davis 1989). However agency theory also argues that CEOs can also expand company for their personal interest, prestige and ?empire building? motives (Jensen & Meckling 1976). CEO`s envy is about perceived utility differences between CEO and the person (most probably payment differences with other CEOs in equivalent companies in terms of size and industry) he/she envies. If CEOs envy each other based on relative compensation and CEOs of bigger firms get paid more, then a merger in the industry that increases firm size for one CEO will cause other envious CEOs to be tempted to undertake value-dissipating but size-enhancing acquisitions, thereby starting a merger wave.
Hypotheses
In order to investigate impact of envy on merger waves, following model is proposed. 

Envy induces a correlation in merger activities by making other CEOs in this cohort envious of the larger firm size and compensation now linked with the CEO of the firm that acquired first. Consequently, even if their own synergies do not warrant acquisitions, these CEOs acquire in order to diminish the utility-sapping impact of their envy. Moreover, as more firms merge, the effects of envy is likely to be stronger for the CEOs who have not joined the fray. Thus, the model predicts that the envy-induced cross-sectional correlation in mergers is generated by the sequential decisions of firms, which then leads to results about how the gains from mergers vary depending on the timing of the merger within the wave.
Hypothesis 1: There is a sequential equilibrium for a firm to acquire on a particular date only if the CEO`s post-acquisition wage exceeds a threshold wage that is a function of the history of past mergers. The threshold wages are decreasing in the envy parameter and each threshold wage solution for date D is associated with a unique equilibrium. 

Envy generates a positive cross-sectional correlation in mergers because a firm is more likely to acquire when another firm in its cohort has acquired. The intuition is that the CEO of an acquiring firm experiences an increase in firm size and in his wage. This causes the CEO of a non-acquiring firm to become envious and this envy manifests itself in the CEO experiencing an elevated marginal utility of a wage increase. Consequently, this CEO may achieve a wage increase by acquiring a firm that he otherwise would not have because the marginal utility associated with the accompanying wage increase would have been considered too low compared to the effort involved in the acquisition. Hence, acquisitions are undertaken that would have been eschewed in the absence of previous acquisitions. This is the mechanism by which envy engenders a positive cross-sectional correlation in merger events, even when shocks to value gains from acquisitions are pairwise uncorrelated
Hypothesis 2: There is a positive correlation between merger events of different firms when CEOs envy each other. 

Mergers in a wave occur in decreasing order of the wage increases to the acquirer CEOs. The intuition is that a firm that acquires a target early in a wave is motivated to do so primarily because the value gain from the acquisition results in a sufficiently large increase in the CEO`s compensation to make it worthwhile for the CEO to acquire without envy playing a role. Firms with lower value gains opt not to acquire early. However, once other firms have acquired, even the CEOs of firms that abstained earlier may acquire because their envy of other acquiring CEOs leads to an increase in the marginal utility they experience from the merger-related elevation in their wages. Since the post-acquisition wages of CEOs are increasing in the value gains from mergers, the empirical implication that follows is that later mergers in a wave will have lower bidder (announcement) returns than earlier mergers.
Hypothesis 3: The merger-induced increase in the wage of an acquiring firm`s CEO is higher in an earlier merger than in a later merger. The value gain to an acquirer is higher in an earlier merger than in a later merger, controlling for target size.

It`s also possible that some systematic triggers can start merger waves. Thus, the model shall be extended to interpret firm size in market value terms, so that CEO envy is defined over market capitalization. Due to the empirical regularity that firms? returns become less highly correlated during stock market upturns and more highly correlated during market downturns, it`s likely to be observed that a large positive shock to market returns will increase the size (market capitalization) disparity among firms in a cohort, and a large negative shock to market returns will have the opposite effect. This shall increase size disparity makes merger waves caused by envy to become more likely, so a merger wave is more probable in a bull market. And because the bull-market mergers are likely to be motivated by envy, they are of lower quality than bear-market mergers. Moreover, it is excepted that the bull-versus-bear-market effect on merger activity largely disappears if we control for the dispersion in market values. 
Hypothesis 4: Acquisitions undertaken during bull markets have lower bidder returns than those undertaken during bear markets

The intuition is that since the wage of a CEO making an acquisition is an increasing function of the value gain from the acquisition, the utility gain to the CEO from an acquisition is increasing in the acquirer`s value gain from the acquisition but decreasing in the size of the target because of the additional disutility of post-acquisition integration imposed by larger targets. Thus, an acquiring firm`s CEO prefers a smaller target and a target with greater value gain to the acquirer. Targets with high value gains and small size are therefore acquired earlier in a merger wave. Larger targets that are not initially acquired because of the greater personal cost to the CEO of implementing the acquisition may be acquired later in the merger wave when a CEO`s envy of the CEOs who acquired targets previously increases the marginal utility of his expected wage gain from an acquisition sufficiently to overcome the higher utility loss associated with integrating a larger target.
Hypothesis 5: The target size is smaller in an earlier acquisition than in a later acquisition, controlling for the value gain to an acquirer.

Data
Gathered from M&A section from SDC.
Analysis
Cross-sectional regression
2015-Brand Value/Recognition is considered a major corporate achievement. How you would go about testing its impact on firm value? Provide a detailed methodological procedure, key measures to be considered and data to be employed in testing the impact of brand value on firm value (10 Points)
Theoretical Background
Brand recognition is extent to which a consumer can correctly identify a particular product or service just by viewing the product or service's logo, tag line, packaging or advertising campaign, while brand value is about net future cash flows derived from brand. In other words brand is an intangible asset, firms with stronger brands (high brand recognition, high values associated with brand) can generate larger earnings and cash flow compared to firms with weaker brands. Furthermore, as the magnitude of the capitalized future earnings and cash flows of a firm's successful and established brand-name products increases, relative to their unbranded product counterparts, so should the magnitude of the Market-to-Book Ratios of those firms that market these brand names, which, in turn, should increase shareholder value. Therefore, firms with larger accumulated brand values should have higher Market-to-Book Ratios than firms with smaller accumulated brand values.

Hypotheses
Given the efficient capital market model is true and capitalized future earnings and cash flows of successful and established brand-name products are greater than the capitalized earnings and cash flows of comparable unbranded generic or commodity products, firms marketing these brand names should evidence higher Market-to-Book Ratios, resulting in greater shareholder value, than firms marketing their unbranded (generic, commodity) counterparts. 

Hypothesis 1: There`s an exponential positive relationship between brand value and market-to-book ratio

Data
Brand value can be gathered through various secondary sources, many consulting companies provide brand valuation lists and methods. Market to Book Ratio is market value of a firm (share price x number of shares) divided by firm`s book value which is also publicly available. To estimate financial value for specific brand we can divide brand sales by total firm`s sale.

Analysis
Non-linear regression
M/B Ratio = a + b (Brand Value)a + error
Why the fixed exchange rate system is considered problematic relative to the floating exchange rate system? Provide an example to support your view (10 Points) 

In floating exchange rate system, exchange rates are allowed to be ?float? freely and determined by demand and supply. Main advantage of floating exchange rates is exchange is updated constantly based on trade and capital flows and central banks does not intervene exchange rates with the exceptions such as days with high volatility. In fixed change rates, the central bank ?fixed? value of its currency against a hard currency (usually US Dollar) and constantly adjust monetary supply, buying/selling its own currency in order to maintain those exchange rate. In other words, in floating exchange rate system rate is determined by market forces, while in fixed exchange rate system it is determined by government/central bank.
 
Although fixed exchange system has advantages such as creating stability, boosting exports or reducing inflation depending on country, holding peg against market forces are destined to be short term. In order to keep exchange rates fixed, central bank either depletes its reserves or prints so much local currency which may lead to inflation later. From theoretical perspective, it leads to overshooting when peg is broken.

Two example could be given. First one is Switzerland which is an export dominated economy (70% of GDP are exports). In 2011 Swiss Central Bank peg Swiss Franc to Euro and for 3 years exchange rate was 1.20 Swiss Francs to Euro in order to keep its competitive advantage. Since Switzerland is considered to be safe haven, investors are constantly buying Swiss Francs which forces Swiss Central Bank to print Swiss Francs and buy foreign currencies in order to prevent Swiss Francs appreciation. However within 3 years, FX reserves/GDP ratio reached to 70%. Swiss fear that this highly increased monetary supply will ultimately lead to other problems such as inflation. So in 2014 Swiss leads to abandon the peg which leads to even higher valued Swiss Franc against Euro, probably higher if currency was never mean to be pegged.

Another example could be current Venezuela where government arbitrary sets 1 USD to 6.2 Bolivar. Currently, Venezuela has significant FX shortages, many imported goods are not available and USD/Bolivar exchange rate is 250+ and inflation is roaring. If Venezuela had never implemented a fixed exchange rate system, bolivar would not be devalued that much. Recent examples could be Argentina and Turkey in late 90s, where both countries implanted fixed (in Turkey it was pegged) exchange rates which leads to major economic crisis when reserves are depleted.
What is a carry trade transaction? (2011) (5 Points)
Carry trade is a strategy in which an investor sells a certain currency with a relatively low interest rate and uses the funds to purchase a different currency yielding a higher interest rate. A trader using this strategy attempts to capture the difference between the rates, which can often be substantial, depending on the amount of leverage used. Similar behavior is also observed in corporate finance. As (McBrady et al; 2010) show, firms are also behaving opportunistically, borrowing in low interest currencies and lending in high interest currencies.

The market must be in disequilibrium in order to carry trade occur. Investors can ?cover? themselves from exchange rate fluctuations by buying a forward contract or keep them uncovered. And the profits shall exceed ask-bid spreads, brokerage fees, costs regarding inelasticity of supply and demand and other related transaction costs (Frenkel & Lewin; 1975)
Raising debt in foreign capital markets (i.e. outside of the firm's home capital market) could be beneficial in terms of reducing the firm's cost of capital. While this seems to be in contradiction with conventional finance theory, which suggests that the method of financing is not value enhancing, empirical studies have shown the opposite. Discuss. (2009) (10 Points)
Eurobond is a type of international bond, denominated in a currency not native to the country where it is issued. In general it is understood as dollar denominated bonds issued by US companies outside US. Eurobonds provide some advantages to investors in taxation and privacy since Eurobonds are not subjected to withholding tax and often does not require registration. It also allows investors to diversify their portfolios.
	
From company perspective, according to clientele hypothesis, firm value can be increased by catering to investor demands (by offering securities in high demand by a financial clientele). In their study, (Kim & Slutz; 1988) show that for a short period of time, firms can borrow at a lower cost in the Eurobond market than in the domestic market since demand for dollar securities from foreign investor is high which makes prices of Eurobond higher.

But this situation makes us to ask the obvious question. If Eurobonds are providing so much advantage then why companies are still borrowing in US and why all companies are not trying to issue Eurobonds. First reason is that issuing costs of Eurobonds are higher compared to domestic bonds. Second reason is that excessive Eurobonds will signal the market that the firms are experiencing problems about satisfying US disclosure requirements. If firms keep issuing overseas, investors might think that they are trying to avoid US regulations. Empirical studies (Doukas 1989) also show that advantages of Eurobonds are only limited to high creditworthy companies. Moreover, based on contagion theory, (Doukas 1989) show that companies are affected not only from events in their country but also neighboring countries. 
The US dollar has been devalued dramatically against the Euro and the UK pound while it has remained fairly stable (or fixed) against Chinese currency. Should China and the US be concerned with the current states of exchange rates? Discuss (2007 & 2008) (5 Points)
a) Discuss the consequences of this depreciation from the perspective of US capital markets.
According to asset-market approach, exchange rate is determined by supply and demand of national currencies, rather than national outputs. Three main factors could be attributed to depreciation of dollar in recent years. Major reason is monetary policies of Federal Reserve. By increasing monetary supply to boost the economy, FED is creating inflationary expectations about the dollar. As shown by (Doukas & Melhem; 1986), markets are immediately processing changes in monetary supply especially unexpected component. 

From demand perspective, as predicted by interest rate parity models, low interest rates reduces investors? demand on dollar denominated assets and leads to capital outflow since other currencies especially emerging markets offer better rates. Second reason is other currencies such as Euro is getting more market share in international transactions, thus reducing demand for dollar. Third reason is current account and budget deficit of US economy. Running twin deficits signals investors that US may experience trouble in debt servicing in future.
b) Discuss the consequences of this appreciation from the perspective of US capital markets.
According to asset-market approach, exchange rate is determined by supply and demand of national currencies, rather than national outputs. Three main factors could be attributed to dollar appreciation. Major reason is expected monetary policies of Federal Reserve. After global crisis, FED started quantitative easing. By lowering interest rates to zero and buying financial assets, FED increased monetary base in order to stimulate economy. However this loose monetary policy did not cause high inflation and moderately successful in boosting economy. This situation could be described as moderate liquidity trap. Since aggregate demand was insufficient, this excess money mostly flushed into stock markets. In other words, new money which is aimed to boost real economy is often used by corporate managers to buy their stocks back. 

As US economy bounce back and unemployment and inflation figures are getting closer to FED`s targets, FED reduce and finished quantitative easing but still holds interest rates at almost zero. Although investors are roughly estimating when FED will increase interest rates, they are not sure when exactly it will happen. Thus as shown by (Doukas & Melhem; 1986), markets are immediately processing changes in monetary supply especially unexpected component.

From demand perspective, as predicted by interest rate parity models, high interest rate will increase investors? demand on dollar denominated assets and lead to capital inflow especially from emerging markets. Thirdly US economy is performing better than other major economies, namely European Union and Japan, and reduced its budget deficit and current account deficit significantly. These factors empowers credibility of US in terms of debt servicing in future.
Has the US capital market lose its competitive advantage? Discuss (2009) (10 Points)
Competitive advantage, in general, advantage of an entity over its competitors preferable unique, rare and inimitable. Competitive advantage of US capital market could be analyzed from two different perspectives. 

First one is exchange rate perspective. According to asset-market approach, exchange rate is determined by supply and demand of national currencies, rather than national outputs. In order to maximize their gains, investors prefer assets denominated in appreciating currencies. If we look at from this approach, expected monetary policies of FED makes a positive impact on US capital markets. As argued by interest rate parity models, higher interest rates will increase demand to dollar denominated assets and investors are transferring their money from emerging markets to US. Moreover US economy is performing better than other major economies, namely European Union and Japan, and reduced its budget deficit and current account deficit significantly. Therefore US is safest place to go.

Second perspective lies in cross listing literature. There are several motivations why firms are listing in US. According to market segmentation theory (Alexander et al; 1988), gains from cross listing would be zero if world capital markets are perfectly integrated and maximum if capital markets are completely disintegrated. Thus by cross listing firms are overcoming barriers of capital flow. Cross listing allows firm diversify their risk and can gain advantage due to national differences (taxes, regulations and institutions). However imperfect world capital markets is not the only motivation behind cross listing. Cross listing provides advantages to both existing and potential investors of firms and firms? management. Cross listing broadens investors? base, reduces cost of capital and provide growth opportunities. As (Stultz; 1995) showed in Nestle case, small investor base means higher expected rate of return for investors and lower stock price for the company. From mathematical perspective local CAPM overestimates cost of capital. From investors? perspective their transaction cost is reduced since they do not have to go to abroad and deal with foreign currencies. Since advanced capital markets have more depth and liquidity, bid-ask spreads are lowered, orders are executed with a greater immediacy. Information cost is also reduced due to high disclosure requirements of advanced capital markets. Thus investors and analysts has gain access to more firm-specific information which makes markets more efficient. Cross listing also signals that firms have ability to satisfy these standards, thus boost firms? reputation and credibility (Doidge et al; 2004). Cross listing also means lower agency cost and better corporate governance. Emerging markets MNEs are often family corporations where controlling shareholders often manages the company with the expense of interests of minority shareholders. Since their home country does not provide strong legal and regularity framework for protecting public investors, insider trading is also popular. High disclosure requirements means bonding (?bond? to behave properly) and monitoring of the firm and its managers and protection for the investor, thus reduced agency cost for investors. We can also talk about managerial incentives behind cross listing.  With cross listing; firms become larger, more visible and more prestigious. Thus managers? compensations are likely to grow with size of the company so as their future employment opportunities.

It will be too bold to claim that US capital markets lost all their competitive advantage. If that were the case, emerging market giants such as AliBaba would not come to US. However we can argue that all these advantages are weakening, all that is solid melts into air. If we look at from historical perspective, we are going from perfectly disintegrated markets to perfectly integrated markets. Emerging market stock exchanges are trying to adopt disclosure and regulatory standards of advanced capital markets such as NYSE and LSE. increasing integration of world capital markets means depthness and liquidity benefits associated to advanced capital markets are disappearing.
