STREAM #1, EXCHANGE RATES

Covered Interest Exchange Rate
Frenkel & Levich, 1975

* RQ: Does empirical data satisfy interest parity theory or allow for arbitrage opportunities? ? the role of transaction costs
* Theory #1: Interest parity theory: In equilibrium, the premium on a forward contract for foreign exchange is related to the interest rate differential: (F-S) / S = (i-i*)/(1+i*) where ?F? and ?S? are forward and spot exchange rates, ?i? is the domestic interest rate, and ?i*? is the foreign interest rate.
o When the equation above is satisfied, there will be no profit opportunities.
* Theory #2: Transaction cost theory: Transaction costs (ask-bid spread and the brokerage fee) in securities and foreign exchange markets imply the existence of a neutral band around the interest parity line, within which no additional arbitrage is profitable. There are 4 transaction cost in the covered outflow of the arbitrage funds from domestic --> foreign market:
o Sales of domestic securities
o Purchase of foreign currency
o Purchase of foreign securities
o Sale of foreign currency (forward)
* Method: assumed that the arbitrage between US dollar and British Sterling is through the German mark. ($/£)t= ($/DM)t(DM/£)t (In reality, the upper limit of discrepancy between the 2 sides is the TC).
* Findings:
o Transaction costs (together with the elasticities) create a neutral band that accounts for all apparent profit opportunities
o A time lag between observing and executing a profit opportunity reduces the profit opportunities
o Covered interest arbitrage does not entail unexploited profit opportunities
* Implication/Contribution:
o Prior studies suggest that the parity condition is not always satisfied thus implying unexploited profit opportunities. This paper demonstrated that transaction costs, elasticities, and the timing of trades account for any possible profit opportunities.

New Information & Exchange Rates
Doukas & Melhem, 1986
* RQ: What`s the impact of new information/ weekly money supply announcements to exchange rates (forward, spot)?
* Theory: Asset-Market Approach: Exchange rate is relative to price of 2 national currencies, rather than price of different national outputs, determined by the demand and supply of various national currencies
o Exchange rates move randomly in response to new information that alters the expectations of the market about exchange rates
o When market is dominated by news that modifies expectations, exchange rates are likely to be more volatile than other economic variables 
o When market`s efficient, information`s rapidly incorporated in stock price
o The speed of adjustments is different between assets & goods market
* Method: SF ? STH = b0 + b1Ma + b2Mu + ut: SF= spot on Friday; STH= spot on Thursday; Ma= anticipated money supply; Mu= unexpected money supply announcement; market is efficient when b0 = 0 
* Findings:
o B2 is significant ? the unexpected component of the US money supply has a greater effect than any of the other variables
* Consistent with asset-market approach that exchange rates immediately adjust to reflect new information
o The response of the forward market appears to be weaker thus supporting the overshooting hypothesis
* The jump in the spot price (volatility) right after the money supply announcement exceeds the jump in forward prices
o Market participants use information other than past money supply changes in forming expectations
* Doukas: the time being measured in this study is deficient because other announcements could be occurring during the time period ? a better study could look at transactional data

Exchange Rate Exposure & Firm (U.S. MNE) Value
Jorion, 1990
* RQ: How does exchange rate risk affect the value of US MNEs (exposure)
o First empirical study to test the belief that exchange rate affect firm value
* Theory: Exposure: the sensitivity of firm value to exchange rate randomness can be measured by the regression coefficient of the change in the value of the firm on the change in the exchange rate
o Foreign exchange rate exposure is decomposed into: 1>, monetary assets; 2>, fixed real assets
o Translation exposure: short-term foreign monetary assets are fully exposed to exchange rate risk & domestic monetary assets are not (both real assets are)
* Methods: Rit= ai + bitRst + eit; Rit = rate of return on ith company`s common stock; Rst = rate of change in a trade weighted exchange rate; bit= yi + yFit + eit; F= foreign sales/ total sales (exchange rate exposure is related to foreign involvement) 
* Findings: 
o Overall, there`s no association between foreign exchange rate exposure and stock returns except for the 14 foreign firms (one subgroup of the sample)
o Big flaw is that Jorion didn?t find support for the foreign exchange risk for U.S. MNEs, yet investigated possible determinants of exchange rate exposure (foreign involvement)

Exchange Rate Exposure & Firm Value
Bartov & Bodnar, 1994
* RQ: What`s the relationship between changes in exchange rates and value of the firm?	
o Consistent with previous literature they failed to find a correlation
o  possible drawbacks of the non-finding: 1>, Limitations in sample selection; 2>, mispricing by investors
* To address 1>, selected firms with similar exchange-rate exposures, i.e., correlations between changes in firm value and the value of the U.S. dollar of the same sign. 
* To address 2>, examined the relation between lagged changes(in spite of simultaneous changes) in the U.S. dollar & firm value
* Theory: Exposure (same as Jorion 1990): Exchange rate movement should affect firm value, that U.S. firms with foreign exposure should see increase/decrease in firm value (stock price) with an unexpected depreciation/appreciation of the dollar (as foreign currency cash flows translate into larger/smaller dollar cash flows)
* Findings:
o Problems with sample selection techniques do not completely explain the non-findings
o Contemporaneous changes in the dollar have little power in explaining firms? abnormal stock returns; however, a lagged change in the dollar is negatively associated with abnormal stock returns, based on corrected sample selection procedures. 
* Due to the complexity of relation between currency changes & firm value, a complete market response to the impact of past changes in the dollar on firm value is delayed till information regarding the past performance/ assets/ liabilities is disseminated
o Structure break: 2 sub period test showed relationship between lagged changes in dollar & current abnormal performance is more pronounced in the sub-period before 1984 than the one after it ? showing that the extent of mispricing weakens over time. 

STREAM #2, HEDGING

Managerial Risk Preference & Hedging 
Knopf, et al., 2002
* RQ: What is the relationship between manager`s risk preferences and hedging activities considering both stock return volatility and stock price?
* Theory: 
o Agency theory: managers are risk-averse because they have investment in stock and human capital in the firm ? they mitigate the risk of their stock options
o Managerial Incentive theory of Hedging: managers will induce the firm to hedge more as the sensitivity of the managers? wealth to stock price increases, and will hedge less as the sensitivity of managers? wealth to stock volatility increases
* Findings:
o Sensitivity to stock price: sensitivity of total portfolio (stock & stock option) to stock price increases, firms hedge more
* Managerial wealth tied to stock price --> incentive to reduce risk --> hedge more
o Sensitivity to stock return volatility: sensitivity of stock option portfolio to stock return volatility increases, firms hedge less
* Value of stock option increases with volatility of stock returns --> incentive to increase risk --> hedge less
* Implications: both types of incentives should be used separately in adjunction to one another when examining how managerial options influence firm hedging. Second, while the firm hedges more as the sensitivity of the total portfolio to stock price increases, it hedges less when the portfolio becomes more sensitive to stock return volatility.

The Overall Value of Hedging
Guay & Kothari, 2003
* RQ: How much do firms hedge with derivatives?
o Firm derivatives use are unlikely to cover their entire risk exposure (exchange rate/ interest rate exposure, ect) 
* Knopf et al., (2002) found support to managerial incentives theory that managers will hedge more as the sensitivity of the manager`s wealth to stock price increases. However, the assumption was that firms? derivatives use does affect stock price volatility. 
o This paper however, founds firms? derivatives portfolios do not affect stock return volatility, thus questioning the findings in Knopf et al., 2002.
* Corporate risk-management theory:  Market imperfections --> volatility costly --> Hedging can:
o Reduce the volatility of taxable income &lower expected taxes 
o Reduce risk premium required by managers in their compensation when managers are risk averse and under-diversified
o Reduces the costs of financial distress
* In sum: hedging with derivatives is a small component of firms? overall risk-management profile
* Doukas: there is no underlying theory in this paper? hedging doesn?t add value to the firm.  Firms prosper by operating efficiencies, & hedging is there simply to protect you. 
o Derivatives can: ??fine-tune?? the overall risk-management profile
o Speculation (reasons other than traditional risk-management theory

Exchange Rate Exposure & Hedging Value
Jorion, 1991
* RQ: The pricing the exchange-rate exposure in the financial market? 
* Arbitrage pricing theory: if the economy is described by a small number of pervasive factors, then these factors may be priced in the sense that investors will be willing to pay a premium to avoid these sources of risk.
o hedging policies can affect the cost of capital if the exchange rate is one of those factors
* Modern portfolio theory:  investors are not willing to pay a premium for firms with active hedging policies if foreign exchange risk can be diversified away
* Findings: 
o Overall, the results of 2 models confirm that the exposure of U.S. common stock to residual exchange rate risk is not systematically related to expected returns. 
o US investors do not seem to price foreign exchange risk. This does not mean that foreign exchange risk is not present, it simply means it is not priced by investors
* As such, active hedging policies by managers cannot affect the cost of capital & other reasons must explain why firms decide to hedge

STREAM #3, EUROBOND & EUROCREDIT

Eurobond ? Cliental Hypothesis
Kim & Stulz, 1988
* RQ: The Euro-bound market ? the cliental hypothesis
* Euro Bounds (bounds issued by U.S. firms in USD to foreign investors):
o bearer bonds that are not registered ? privacy
o not subjected to withholding tax ? tax evasion
o investor risk diversification ? less risk
o only accessible to good-credited companies ? company reputation
o contracting & enforcement costs are higher ? cost
o issuing costs are higher by 1-2% - cost 
* The clientele hypothesis: firm value can be increased by catering to investor demands (by offering securities in high demand by a financial clientele), specifically because:
o For a short period of time, firms can borrow at a lower cost in the Eurobond market than in the domestic market.  
o Bargains survive because when the demand for dollar securities from foreign investors is high, they bid the Eurobonds prices higher.  
* Findings: 
o positive average abnormal stock return associated with the offering announcement
o Abnormal returns are positively related over time to the yield spread (to domestic bonds)
o The abnormal return is attributed to firms with a reputation for low default risk to exploit temporary financing opportunities
* Doukas: the paper does not support the clientele hypothesis, it only shows firms timed a hot market

Euro-Credit Market & Contagion Effects
Doukas, 1989
* RQ: Euro-credit market efficiency & contagion effects
* Contagion theory:  even if markets are information efficient, lenders immediately process information and adjust expectations about borrowers? creditworthiness, contagion effects could occur which adds systematic, or non-diversifiable risk to international lending
o Individual Latin American country spreads are influenced not only by the creditworthiness of the borrowing country but also by the creditworthiness of other borrowing countries in this region
* Efficient market theory related to sovereign lending
* Sovereign risk:  the risk a lender faces that the borrower government may forfeit its foreign obligations 
* Findings: 
o DXIRe (lender`s perceived probability of default) is significant: lenders process information efficiciently
o DXIRu is significant: changes in spread margin when unanticipated information is received by market participants ? sovereign risk has been incorporated in the process of Euro-syndicated credit pricing
o Euro-credit pricing process is information efficient however non-country-specific risk factors systematically influence country-specific spreads
* Syndicated Euro-credit market operates efficiently in the sense that "news" associated with the changing state of borrowers' creditworthiness has an immediate effect on Euro-spreads.
* Non-country-specific risk factors are embedded into the default risk perceptions of lenders.
* Managerial implication: International lenders are exposed to considerable systematic risk when they do not spread their lending exposure across different geographical regions

STREAM #4, CROSS-LISTING

Global CAMP
Stulz, 1995 
* RQ: Instead of using a local CAPM to evaluate a company`s risk in local markets, a global CAPM views a company as part of the global portfolio of stocks
* Benefits & Costs of global diversification: 
o Benefits: Globalization --> investors spread risk with globally diversified portfolios --> lower risk premium, required returns, & cost of capital --> higher stock price & firm value
o Costs: Globalization --> synchronization among international capital markets --> increased sensitivity of firm stock price to global events --> higher investors risk -->higher risk premium & cost of capital --> offsetting the benefits of global diversification 
* Theory: CAPM
o Local CAPM:  RS = RF + ?(RM-RF) 
o Global CAPM: RS = RF + ?(RG-RF) 
* views a company as part of the global portfolio of stocks
* RS = Required; RF = Risk free rate; ? = stock`s beta in local/global market; RM = market return 
* Findings:
o Cost of capital for firms in small countries should be estimated using the global CAPM rather than local CAPM
o Nestlé`s cost of capital is overstated/higher using the local CAPM
* local investors will demand a higher expected return to compensate them for bearing more risk in their portfolios than if they could diversify their holdings across international markets
* Globalization of financial markets increases the values of companies by reducing the cost of capital (which in turn stimulates further economic growth).

Cross-Listing Value for Segmented Markets
Alexander, et al., 1988
* RQ: What effects do international Listings have on Stock Returns
* Theory: Market Segmentation: the degree to which investors of a country cannot invest in the securities of another country and vice versa. 
o Integration:  the degree to which investors of one country are free to invest in the securities of another and vice versa.  
* International listing: Once a stock is listed in a foreign market, foreign investors can trade stock in their own currency this: 
o Circumvent foreign exchange regulations
o Reduce information costs & transaction costs in dealing with foreign currency & market
* Findings: 
o If capital market before dual listing was partially or completely segmented, international listing --> higher stock price/ lower expected return (lower risk --> lower risk premium)
o Decline in expected returns is more for firms from more segmented markets than less segmented markets 
* I.E.: Canadian market is less segmented than non-Canadian markets 

Doidge et al., 2004
* The benefits of cross-listings 
o access to lower cost of capital, risk premium reduction with global risk-sharing
o increased shareholder base, liquidity, visibility, exposure, & prestige
o information disclosure (U.S. market requires) ? increase firm credibility & value 
o enhanced governance, monitoring & protection of minority shareholders -> reduced agency cost
* The costs of cross listings
o SEC reporting, compliance, and disclosure; legal and banking costs

U.S Cross-Listing Value 
Doidge, et al., 2004 
* RQ: Why are foreign firms listed in the US worth more?
* External finance only occurs when market thinks controlling shareholders do not expropriate --> reduced agency cost when controlling shareholders takes action to limit their expropriation of minority shareholders --> only happens when there`s a need to access external market for capital --> therefore cross listing is valuable because: 
1. firms take growth opportunities
2. reduced agency cost by limiting controlling shareholders
* Although benefits seem to outweigh cost, few firms cross-list, because:
o Firms are controlled by large shareholders, who will cross-list the US only if firm`s growth opportunities exceed the benefit from expropriating the firm`s resources.
* Findings:
o Target country: firms list in the US have higher Tobin`s Q valuations than those that do not list in the US
* U.S. listing achieves lower cost of capital at the expense of reduced controlling shareholder expropriation
o Country of origin: listed firms from a weaker investor-protection country are expected to have better growth opportunities than from stronger investor protection country
* Greater increase in investor protection through US listing, higher cross-listing premium
* Higher Tobin`s Q after listing in the U.S., 
o Emerging market firms (with weaker investor protection) experience higher growth opportunity 

STREAM #5, DIVERSIFICATION

International Acquisition of U.S Firms
Doukas & Travlos, 1988
* RQ: How does international acquisition affect stock prices of the U.S. firms? 
* Corporate Multinationalism Theory/ Network theory:  
o Firm`s market value will increase from the expansion of its existing multinational network.
o The global expansion fulfills both investors? international-diversification objectives & the acquiring firm`s advantages in a multinational network (branching tree) 
* Findings: 
o Main effect: U.S. acquirers will see positive abnormal returns from foreign acquisition announcements
o Network effect: If no existing branch in foreign country --> acquisition announcements by U.S. MNEs lead to positive abnormal returns;  
* However, if acquirer already operates in target`s nation, there`s negative (insignificant) abnormal return with announcement
* Firms diversify internationally for the 1st time anywhere experience positive (insignificant) abnormal returns
* Acquirers benefit more when expanding to new industry or less developed market (emerging markets)
* Supports the theory of corporate multinationalism/ network theory ? that increases in market value could come from expansion of existing multinational network. 

Diversification Using Intangibles
Morck & Yeung, 2002
* RQ: foreign investment occurs when a firm can increase its value (Market evaluation: Tobin`s Q) by internalizing markets for some of its intangible assets
* Internalization theory: FDI occurs when a firm can increase its value by internalizing markets for certain intangible assets (Buckley & Casson, 1976)
o That internationalization enhances the scope for firms using its intangible assets
* Managerial Objectives Theory (Weakly supported): Multinational firms are more complex --> hard for shareholder to monitor management`s decision -->managers are more likely to act in their self-interest
* Findings:
o Positive impact of R&D and advertising on market value of the firm is enhanced by firms? multinationalility scale
o However, multinationality per se does not have any significant impact on Q

o Do NOT support the Imperfect World Capital Markets theory that investors are prevented from global stock diversification due to barriers and information asymmetries, but MNEs help them diversify ? therefore value firm higher
o NO tax advantages or low-cost factors affecting Q ? contradicts Doukas (1995)
o Weak support that multinationality without intangibles reduce firm value (managerial objective theory) ? self-centered managers --> agency cost --> complex organizational structure creates difficulties to monitor
* Firms should not go international without intangibles

Diversification & Overinvestment
Doukas, 1995
* RQ: What`s the effect of Q (indicating good or poor managing/ overinvestment) on the stock return of U.S. firms? foreign acquisition
* Multinational network theory: foreign investment decisions enhance the expanding firm`s ability to benefit from the systematic advantages inherent in a multinational network
* Free cash flow theory: firms with substantial amounts of free cash flow will overinvest even if the investment/expansion is not profitable
* Overinvestment hypothesis: average bidder returns in response to announcements of foreign acquisition is smaller for overinvesting (Low Q) firms than for value-maximizing (High Q) firms ? Supported 
o Because managers feel their compensation, power, prestige and job satisfaction are enhanced by managing a growing company. However, these objectives are of little interest to investors (spending cash instead of making a dividends to investors)? divergence of interest cause agency cost and lower the return 
* Findings: 
o The separation of firms of value Maximizers vs. Over-investors is based on Q ? good contribution by Doukas 
o Difference in stock return in reaction to foreign acquisition announcement:
* Maximizers (high Q) --> high return; Over-investors (low Q)--> low return
* Maximizers (high Q) --> cash irrelevant with return; Over-investors (low Q) --> cash negatively related to return
* Support overinvestment hypothesis that: firms with access cash tend to overinvest by accepting marginal projects with negative NPVs 
o Maximizers (high Q) gain even higher return when investing in countries without existing operations, & are less developed ? same as Doukas & Travlos, 1988
o Over-investors gain even lower return when investing in countries with existing operations ? same as Doukas & Travlos, 1988
o Acquirer/bidder gain is higher when target is in lower tax regions 

Denis, et al., 2002
Diversification is good for:
* Internalizing value added resources across national boundaries
* Increasing firm flexibility to respond to changes in the tax codes, prices, and institutions (I.E. if tax increase in one country, an MNE that diversifies to different countries can shift focus to another lower tax country)
* Firms can diversify with lower costs than individuals --> providing investors portfolio diversification --> willing to pay premium 
Diversification is bad for:
* Complex organization --> Information asymmetry between HQs and Subs--> higher internal coordination & monitoring costs
* Inefficiency by subsidizing less-effective subs
* Managerial personal incentives (better pay, more-diversified portfolio, & prestige) 

Diversification Hurts Firm Value
Denis, et al., 2002
* RQ: How does global & industrial diversification impact firm value?
* Internationalization: internationalization is beneficial because firms possess informational based assets (intangibles) that have synergetic effects when used across the globe
o Good & bad sides (see above)
* Findings:
o Global & industry diversification are associated with reduced value 
* Cost outweighs benefits, compared to domestic single industry firms
o Global diversification complement industrial diversification
* The trend of increased globalization does not substitute for decreased industrial diversification
* Firms increase/decrease global diversification are likely to increase/decrease industrial diversification
o Discount with global diversification is stable overtime while the discount with industry diversification decreases
* Reasons:
o Negative excess values of globally diversified firms is not due to poorly performing firms choosing to diversify
* Result is not due to endogenous firm choices ? there seem to be a causal link between diversification & value
o Diversified firms invest inefficiently: on one segment but not another --> reallocation of resources among segments is inefficient

Diversification Doesn?t Hurt Firm Value
Doukas & Kan, 2006
* RQ: Global diversification doesn?t destroy firm value
o Denis et al., 2002 showed that global diversification results in 18% shareholder loss. Doukas & Kan claim the finding is erroneous because it ignored the bondholder`s perspective. 
* Contingent Claims hypothesis: global diversification discount increases with leverage, indicating that firms with globally diversified operations are subjected to lower risk
* Findings: 
* Consistent with risk-reduction effects of global diversification:
o Bidders with prior global operations trade at a discount 
o The shareholder value loss to foreign acquisition is associated to high leverage 
* All-equity bidders trade at a premium
* Only bidders that are considerably levered trade at a discount
* The use of book value debt creates a downward bias in globally diversified firms
o EV (excess value) measure of firm value based on the book value of debt, undervalues globally diversified firms 
* Consistent with the Contingent Claims View, foreign involvement increases bondholder value while decreasing shareholder value 
* Overall, results indicate that global diversification does not destroy firm value

STREAM #6, SYNCHRONICITY

Synchronicity in Emerging Markets
Morck, et al., 2000
* RQ: Why do emerging economies have synchronous stock price movements?
o Stock prices in economies with high per capita GDP move in unsynchronized manner while stock prices in low per capita GDP economies move up or down together. 
* In emerging economies:
o This is not a result of market fundamentals, such as market size, country size, and economy diversification.
o Less respect for private property by government --> more synchronous stock price movements. 
o Poor property rights protection deters risk arbitrage and creates opportunities for noise traders.  Impedes the capitalization of firm-specific information into stock prices
* In developed economies:
o Shareholders have stronger legal protection against corporate insiders --> greater firm-specific returns variation --> lower synchronicity
o Economies that protect public investors' property rights discourage inter-corporate income-shifting by controlling shareholders. --> firm-specific risk-arbitrage more attractive
* Stock markets in emerging economies may be less useful as processors of economic information than stock markets in advanced economies. 
o Because if movements are mainly due to either politically driven shifts in property rights or noise trading, stock markets may allocate capital poorly, thereby retarding economic growth.

Synchronicity & Analysts 
Chan & Hameed, 2006, 
* RQ: What`s the role of analysts in Stock Price Synchronicity in Emerging Markets
o Morck et al (2000) has shown that stock prices move more together in emerging markets than in developed markets, due to:
* low disclosure requirements
* low degree of corporate transparency
* prevalence of family and group controlled firms
* Stock price synchronicity is associated to analyst activities:
o If analysts generate mainly market-wide information, stock prices would move in a synchronous manner, vice versa for firm specific data. 
o Emerging markets? security analysts mainly product macro information. 
o Analysts follow each other: Returns of high analyst following portfolios lead to returns of low analyst following portfolio
* When forecast dispersion`s high, the effect of analyst coverage on stock price synchronicity is reduced
* Reason:
o Poor information disclosure and lack of transparency increases cost of collecting firm-specific information --> analysts generate earnings forecasts based on macro information
* Because of this, information provided analysts are not valuable in emerging markets





1a. prior empirical evidence has shown that international diversification is beneficial to firm value and performance. Some empirical studies have raised concerns about the diversification benefits of international diversification. Do shareholders of globally diversification firms enjoy a premium?
Denis et al. (2002) investigated the diversification and firm value and found that globally diversified firms trade at a discount due to agency and transaction costs such as information asymmetry, coordination and monitoring costs compared to domestic firms operating in the same industries. However, their findings are in contrast with other studies that suggest that diversification can create value for firms. The reason for inconsistent result in Denis at et al study is believed to be due to methodological deficiencies. Doukas and Kan (2006) criticized the measurement used by Denis et al and argued that they only considered changes in shareholder value and ignored bondholders' value. To elaborate, Denis et al. used excess values (EV) to measure the firm value.  Ev measure the firm's actual value relative to its imputed value based on book value debt which can be problematic and biased because it only measures change in shareholder value. Therefore, they ignored bondholders? value. Furthermore, the definition of international diversification based on foreign sales may not capture the full extent of diversification, which result in a misrepresentation of their effects on firm value. Furthermore, Campa & Kedia (2002) argue that the reason for the inconsistent finding is that they did not address the endogeneity. They control for firm-fixed effects and self-selection of the decision to diversify; they find the diversification discount drops and sometimes turns into a premium. Also, they neither discriminate location of expansions nor management quality of the companies.

1b. Does corporate managers? compensation structure affect corporate hedging?
Yes, managing hedges can result in conflicts of interest between managers and shareholders, as managers may make decisions based on their own interests rather than the interests of shareholders (Jensen & Meckling, 1976). Hedging decisions can be influenced by managers' own wealth and compensation incentives, resulting in agency costs (Smith & Stulz, 1985).

1c. Does currency hedging increase firm value as it has been argued in the previous literature? Does hedging really increase firm value and if yes how?
Currency risk exposure is a firm`s sensitivity to fluctuations in exchange rates. Hedging is a form of risk management through which an opposite position is taken in a related security or asset (derivatives) which consequently can reduce the risk of adverse price movements in the other asset. There is disagreement about whether the manager should hedge the currency risk and whether hedging increases shareholder value. 
On the one hand, proponents of currency hedging argue that companies with high exposure to currency risk should hedge to mitigate the financial exchange exposure. For example, Jorion (1990) finds that firms with a higher level of foreign sales have a higher exchange-rate exposure. Hence, hedging the currency risk is necessary to protect the company's financial performance and shareholders? wealth. Barkov and Bodnar (1994) criticize previous inconsistent findings regarding the relationships between currency hedging and firm value. They argue that the reason for the inconsistencies can be due to sample biases, only using US dollar currency, and the failure to consider time lags for investors to use information. Their study suggests that lagged models and using a broader range of currency pairs can provide more accurate results, and they support the view that internationalization can still benefit over risk. Additionally, Doukas (1995) points out that investors are faced two kinds of risks including systematic risk and nonsystematic risk and hedging can mitigate systematic risk, as it provides a solution to protect against economic shocks and stabilizes cash flow. Allayanis et al (2001) also find a significant relationship between financial hedging (short term hedges) and firm`s value due to decreasing risk exposure, but no relationship between operational hedging and firm`s value. It means operational hedging alone does not add firm value. Yet, when operational hedging is used with financial hedging, results indicate that firm value is increased.
On the other hand, opponents argue that hedging is costly and may not necessarily increase the cash flow of the firm. For example, Guay and Kothari (2003) find that derivatives usage in firms' risk management strategies is not significant compared to overall firm size and cash flows. While hedging can increase cash flows, it comes with costs such as transaction and translation costs. Thus, value is added only if increased return/value is sufficiently large to compensate for cost of hedging.Also, Holberg & Moon (2017) find that financial hedging is not always effective as it does not protect against demand shocks (quantity risk), therefore firms may instead opt for operational hedging activities, such as purchasing input from the same nation that they sell output to. 

2a. it has been argued that high delta CEOs are less likely to engage in risky corporate decision but high vega CEOs are most likely to persue riskier corporate decisions. Discuss how and in what direction their decision would affected.
Delta is the measurement of an option`s sensitivity to changes in the price of the underlying asset, while Vega is the measurement of an option`s sensitivity to changes in the volatility of the underlying asset. Managers with a high Delta, are likely to hedge more as they can reduce the risks associated with these changes and protect their positions by hedging (given that hedging is a form of risk management). In contrast, managers with a higher Vega are less likely to hedge as volatility may be more attractive because it can increase profits on their options positions. Knopf et al (2002) find that as the sensitivity of managers? stock option portfolios to stock price increases, firms tend to hedge more. However, as the sensitivity of managers? stock option portfolios to stock return volatility increases, firms tend to hedge less. 

2b. Empirical studies have shown that stock trading in less developed countries is subject to high levels of synchronicity. What are empirical implications of capital markets stock price synchronicity from investors point of view.
Stock price synchronicity refers to the movement of stock prices in the same direction and it is more common in less developed and emerging markets. Previous studies found some explanations for this phenomenon. Morck et al (2000) found that correlated market fundamentals, such as market size, country size, and economic diversification can lead to increased synchronicity as investors rely more on market-wide information rather than firm-specific information due to the lack of trust in individual firms. Another factor which can lead to synchronicity is poor intellectual property rights protection in low-income economies. In such economies, insufficient protections for intellectual property rights discourage informed risk arbitrage which creates opportunities for noise traders, this in turn, impedes the capitalization of firm information into stock price which lead to increased synchronicity. Mock et al (2000) also found that a lack of regulatory enforcement and low levels of voluntary disclosure increase synchronicity. To elaborate, there are many family or group-owned firms in these markets which make it difficult to obtain detailed information from. This difficulty in gaining information, limits the availability of firm-specific data. Consequently, analysts must rely on stock market information which results in a higher degree of synchronicity in stock prices. Furthermore, Chan & Hameed (2006) examines the relationship between analysts? coverage and stock price synchronicity in emerging markets. Emerging markets? security analysts mainly produce market-wide information, which increases synchronicity. They find securities which are covered by more analysts, incorporate greater (lesser) market-wide (firm-specific) information. The idea is that when the market-wide information is high and firm-specific information is low for a particular security, investors cannot differentiate between the securities, so the prices of these securities will be more likely to move in the same direction. 

2c. The effect of synchronicity on countries and economic prospects of firms.
According to the previous question answer and factors which lead to synchronicity, the high synchronicity of stock prices suggests that emerging markets may be less effective in processing economic information compared to advanced economies which effectively process information and guide capital toward the best capital use. This inefficiency can hinder the optimal allocation of capital, and potentially impede economic growth (Morck et al., 2000).


3. Is endogeneity a major concern in empirical studies? And if yes, describe econometric methods and alternatives procedure available to address endogeneity bias.
Yes, endogeneity is one of the major concerns in empirical studies in finance, strategic management, psychology etc. researches which need to be aware of that.  When in cause and effects relationships, one of the independent variables (explanatory variables) is correlated with error term (all the stuff we did not include in our model) we can have endogeneity concern and it can break one of the key assumptions of OLS regression model that can lead to biased estimation and invalid findings.  For example, when we study the effect of exercise on losing weight without considering the effect of motivation in this relationship we are causing the endogeneity. Here just the exercise is not responsible for losing weight because we know that when we are motivated, we can do more exercises and also keep the diet better and consequently lose weight. 
Fortunately, we can solve the endogeneity issue by some econometric solutions that appropriately address this problem.
At first step, we need to look at the sources which can lead to endogeneity and recognize them in our study. Omitted variables, reverse casualty and measurement errors are sources which are responsible for endogeneity. For instance, in our example, motivation can be such as omitted variable which should be included in the group of explanatory variables to predict the losing weight as dependent variable, but it is omitted. If the omitted variable is correlated with any of the explanatory variables, then the error term is correlated with the explanatory variables, resulting in endogeneity problem. The second source of endogeneity is reverse casualty, which occurs when the direction of causality between variables is ambiguous. In other words, we do not know whether x causes y or vice versa, for instance, in one study we want to look at the factors which increase the venture capital firm performance and we found that quality of investment can explain whether the performance is satisfying, but here also we can investigate that if venture capital firms show the acceptable performance consequently due to this good performance and reputation, more better investments may approach to the them, so here we are facing with reverse causality which can initiate the endogeneity.  Finally, the third source is measurement error in both dependent and independent variables. If we do not measure our variables perfectly, it will become part of the regression error and derive to invalid findings. Specifically, they can be problematic in studies that heavily rely on proxies to represent theoretical constructs. 
For the second step, after finding the sources of endogeneity, we need to choose the right solution to address it. There are several econometrics procedures that can help mitigate this problem. The simplest solution is to include relevant control variables. Another way for mitigating endogeneity problem is to use instrumental variable method. We can find a variable that is correlated with the independent variable but not with the DV. The instrumental variable can impact the dependent variable indirectly and just through its impact on the independent variable. We can run a regression to determine the predicted variable and plug it back into the original equation. The instrumental variable must satisfy two conditions: the instrument must be correlated with the endogenous variable, and the instrument variable must be uncorrelated with outcome variable. Additionally, there are other methods that can help address the endogeneity problems such as Heckman`s correction model, Panel Data Methods with Fixed-firm effect, Difference-in-Differences (DD), and finally avoiding Proxies or using Proxies correctly.

Foreign M&As are among the most visible initiatives a CEO can take as they can expand the size of the company in foreign markets and feed his or her need to be seen as bold and daring. Accordingly, we can argue that the relationship mentioned above is more positive when the CEO is narcistic:
H2: The positive relationship between CEO envy and the frequency of a company's foreign M&A activities is stronger when the CEO exhibits higher levels of narcissism.

6. Discuss the key differences between multinational network and the internalization hypothesis. And to what extend the benefits of firms offshoring activities might be subject to a missing factor regardless of both hypotheses.

Multinational network and internalization theory are different approaches to study why firms decide to expand internationally and improve their competitive advantages according to internalization theory, firms decide to expand and operate in foreign countries to leverage and exploit their resources which are valuable, rare, non-imitable and non-substitutable (Barney, 1995). In other words, they internalize markets for resources and intangible assets to reduce the market imperfections and other transaction costs such as monitoring cost and appropriation opportunities which prevent MNEs to utilize their assets through the markets (Buckley & Casson, 1976, Morck & Yeung, 1991).  So it seems that only firms that have intangible assets with the VRIO characteristics should be expected to benefit from international expansion. 
On the other hand, according to multinational network hypothesis, the primary advantage of the multinational firm lies in the flexibility to transfer resources across borders through a globally maximizing network (Bartlett & Ghoshal, 1990; Kogut, 1983). This view implies that subsidiaries and headquarters not only interact with each other, but also with other external entities such as suppliers, customers, regulators etc. MNEs? interactions with others within and across network can impact the resource configuration which is an important attribute of MNEs. Through this network, MNEs can circumvent institutional restrictions (such as tax in their home countries), capture informational externalities (learning cost externalities), achieve cost reductions, production economies of scale. Also, investors can use MNEs to diversify their portfolios. Thus, MNEs benefit from systematic advantages of being a multinational company. As evidence we can refer to Doukas & Travlos, (1988) study which found MNEs create value by expanding globally and utilizing their network of operation. They also found that abnormal returns are larger when firms expand into new industry and geographic markets, especially those less developed than the US





7.describe the main reasons firms choose to cross list their stocks in foreign markets 
International listings can be defined as the listing of stocks in a foreign exchange in addition to a domestic exchange (Alexander et al., 1988). According to market segmentation hypothesis, since there are barriers to complete integration of capital markets, markets to some extents are segmented, and firms try to mitigate the negative aspects of capital market segmentation, such as high-risk premium, information costs, regulatory constraints, and governmental policies by adopting specific financial policies such as international listing which help them raise funds at a lower cost than at home. Furthermore, based on bonding hypothesis, firms cross-list in more regulated markets to bond themselves to higher regulation, information disclosure, and monitoring standards which consequently help them mitigate potential agency conflicts and transaction costs. Applying this perspective, Doukas & Wang (2014) find that Chinese firms with foreign listings gain higher valuations compared to firms without foreign listings. Additionally, based on the liquidity hypothesis, by cross-listing, firms can have access to more liquid markets which in turn improve their stock liquidity (Doidge et al, 2004). 
So, a firm outside the US might list its shares on US markets, in addition to domestic listing, for several possible reasons such as, lower cost of capital, increased ability to raise equity, increased shareholder base, and enhanced visibility and prestige.

The reason why developed countries have higher tax levels compared to their host counterparts can be attributed to several reasons. These countries provide different services such as high-quality education, high quality public safety, and high investor? protection. High-level taxes can provide funding and maintaining these services. These services in turn impact the quality of life of their residents. Taxes can also be used to maintain market stability and institutional environment (legal and regulatory systems) which are essential for sustaining long-term economic growth. Indeed, developed countries rely on the combination of these factors to maintain their competitive advantage. As mentioned by the bonding hypothesis, many firms cross-list in more regulated markets such as the US market to bond themselves to higher corporate governance standards such as regulation, information disclosure, and monitoring standards which can help them mitigate potential agency costs and transaction costs. Doukas and Wang (2014) also tested this hypothesis and find that Chinese firms with foreign listings gain higher valuations compared to firms without foreign listings. Therefore, developed countries may prefer maintaining the tax level high to be able to provide a safe place for foreign firms to invest or enter their markets due to their strict regulation (which is costly) that can be beneficial for foreign firms in the long term. Finally, as mentioned in the literature, shareholders can choose between expropriating less-developed countries with low taxes and poor investment protection or committing to greater investor protection and enjoying firms? growth opportunities.

Question 1: Endogeneity Problem
The endogeneity problem is one of the most pervasive methodological challenges in empirical finance and international business research. At its core, endogeneity arises when an explanatory variable in a regression model is correlated with the error term, violating a fundamental assumption of ordinary least squares (OLS) estimation and rendering the resulting coefficient estimates biased and inconsistent.
In international finance research, the typical empirical framework takes the form: Performance Measure = f(Strategy Choice Variable, Controls) + error. A significant coefficient on the strategy variable is interpreted as evidence that the strategy is superior. The problem, however, is that firms do not randomly select their strategies. Instead, companies choose strategies — such as whether to engage in foreign direct investment, hedge currency risk, cross-list on foreign exchanges, or diversify internationally — based on their own observable and unobservable attributes and industry conditions. As Shaver (1998) and Reeb, Sakakibara, and Mahmood (JIBS) explain, if companies choose strategies based on their attributes and those of their industry, then empirical models that do not account for this choice process are potentially misspecified, and the normative inferences drawn from them may be incorrect.
There are three principal sources of endogeneity: (1) Omitted variable bias occurs when variables that jointly determine both the dependent and independent variables are excluded from the model. For example, firm quality or managerial talent might drive both the decision to go multinational and the subsequent performance improvement, making it appear that multinationality causes performance gains when in fact a third variable drives both. (2) Reverse causality (simultaneity) occurs when causation runs both ways between the dependent and independent variables. In studies of whether cross-listing improves firm value, for instance, better-governed, higher-quality firms self-select into cross-listing, so observed value premiums may reflect pre-existing firm quality rather than a causal effect of listing. As documented by Doukas and Wang (2014) in their study of Chinese cross-listed firms, this is explicitly acknowledged and addressed using difference-in-differences analysis. (3) Measurement error in the independent variables can also induce correlation between regressors and the error term.
Several econometric solutions are available. The most common is instrumental variables (IV) or two-stage least squares (2SLS), wherein an instrument is found that is correlated with the endogenous regressor but uncorrelated with the error term. Doukas and Kan (2006), examining whether global diversification destroys firm value, use a two-stage procedure wherein a probit model first predicts the decision to diversify globally, and the predicted values are then used in the second-stage OLS regression. This addresses the concern that firms self-select into diversification based on pre-existing characteristics. Panel data models also partially mitigate omitted variable bias by controlling for unobserved firm fixed effects. The difference-in-differences (DiD) estimator, matching methods, and regression discontinuity designs are additional tools.
The endogeneity problem has profound implications for the interpretation of research findings. A failure to account for self-selection can lead to dramatically overstated or understated policy conclusions. For example, the observed "diversification discount" documented by Denis, Denis, and Yost (2002) — the finding that globally diversified U.S. firms trade at lower valuations — may in part reflect the fact that firms with pre-existing problems self-select into diversification, rather than diversification itself causing value destruction. Recognizing and correcting for endogeneity is therefore not merely a technical refinement but a prerequisite for drawing valid causal inferences in empirical finance research.
Question 2: Hedging Currency Risk
Currency risk, also known as exchange rate risk or foreign exchange (FX) risk, arises when a firm has assets, liabilities, revenues, or costs denominated in a foreign currency whose value relative to the domestic currency can fluctuate unpredictably. For multinational corporations (MNCs), exporters, and firms with foreign debt, currency movements can materially alter cash flows, earnings, and balance sheet values. Hedging currency risk refers to the use of financial instruments or operational strategies to reduce or eliminate this exposure.
There are three primary forms of currency exposure. Transaction exposure refers to the risk that exchange rate changes will affect the value of specific contractual cash flows — for example, a U.S. exporter that invoices in euros faces uncertainty about the dollar value it will receive when the invoice is settled. Translation exposure arises when a firm consolidates foreign subsidiaries' financial statements into its home currency; changes in exchange rates affect reported earnings and balance sheet values even without any underlying cash flow impact. Economic exposure (or operating exposure) is the broadest category and refers to the impact of exchange rate movements on the present value of the firm's future operating cash flows. Jorion (1990) finds that the exchange-rate exposure of U.S. multinationals is positively correlated with the degree of foreign involvement, and Bartov and Bodnar (1994) document that lagged changes in the dollar are negatively associated with abnormal stock returns for exchange-rate-exposed firms.
The most common instruments used to hedge currency risk are currency forward contracts, currency futures, currency options, and currency swaps. Forward contracts lock in an exchange rate for a future date and are widely used to hedge transaction exposure. Currency options provide the right but not the obligation to exchange at a specified rate, allowing firms to benefit from favorable rate movements while limiting downside risk. Cross-currency swaps exchange principal and interest payments in different currencies and are used to hedge long-term economic or financing exposures. Allayannis and Ofek (2001) and Geczy, Minton, and Schrand (1997) document that U.S. firms with greater foreign sales are significantly more likely to use currency derivatives.
Operational hedging represents an alternative or complement to financial hedging. Strategies include matching revenues and costs in the same currency (natural hedging), diversifying production and sourcing internationally, invoicing in the home currency, and adjusting pricing strategies in response to exchange rate movements. McBrady, Mortal, and Schill (2010) provide evidence that firms opportunistically denominate their bond issuances in low-yield foreign currencies, a form of currency-related financial strategy that is consistent with firms actively managing their currency cost of capital.
The corporate rationale for hedging, as formalized by Smith and Stulz (1985), rests on market imperfections. In a Modigliani-Miller world of perfect capital markets, hedging would be irrelevant at the corporate level because shareholders can replicate any hedging strategy themselves at no cost. However, when markets are imperfect — due to financial distress costs, tax convexity, costly external financing (Froot, Scharfstein, and Stein, 1993), and managerial risk aversion — corporate hedging can increase firm value by reducing the volatility of cash flows and earnings. The empirical evidence broadly supports the value-relevance of currency hedging for firms with material foreign exposures, making it a core tool in international financial management.
Question 3: Why Do Some Corporate Managers Tend to Hedge More Than Others? Discuss.
The decision to hedge is ultimately a managerial one, and the empirical literature has established that cross-sectional variation in hedging intensity is substantially driven by the incentive structure embedded in executive compensation packages rather than simply by firm-level characteristics. The seminal framework of Smith and Stulz (1985) identifies managerial risk aversion as a key determinant: when managers have a large portion of their personal wealth tied to the firm, they have an incentive to reduce firm risk through hedging, even if shareholders with diversified portfolios would prefer the firm to remain exposed.
The critical insight developed by Knopf, Nam, and Thornton (2002) is that stock options create two opposing effects on managerial risk preferences. The first is sensitivity to stock price, measured by delta (Δ) — the change in the value of the manager's stock and option portfolio for a 1% change in the stock price. A high-delta manager has strong incentives to hedge, because losses from adverse price movements directly reduce their personal wealth. Knopf et al. find that hedging activities are positively related to the sensitivity of a manager's stock and option portfolio to stock price (LPRICESEN), consistent with theory. The second effect is sensitivity to stock return volatility, captured by vega (ν) — the change in the option portfolio's value for a 1% increase in return volatility. Because option values increase with volatility through the convex payoff structure, a high-vega manager benefits personally when the firm takes on more risk, and therefore has an incentive to hedge less. Knopf et al. find hedging is negatively related to vega (LVOLSEN), though this relationship is not statistically significant in their broad sample.
Bodnar, Giambona, Graham, and Harvey (in their "A View Inside Corporate Risk Management") directly survey managers who make hedging decisions and conclude that personal risk aversion, in combination with other executive traits, plays a key role in whether a company hedges. This finding underscores an important deficiency in firm-level theories of risk management that ignore the individual manager.
Firm characteristics also explain variation in hedging. Firms with greater financial leverage are more likely to hedge because hedging reduces the probability of financial distress and the associated costs. Firms with significant investment opportunities and potential underinvestment problems (Myers, 1977) are more likely to hedge to ensure internal funds are available to finance positive-NPV projects (Froot, Scharfstein, and Stein, 1993). Firms with tax convexity — where the effective tax rate is a convex function of taxable income — can reduce expected taxes by smoothing income through hedging. Firms with greater growth opportunities (higher Tobin's Q), more R&D spending, and higher debt ratios are empirically found to be more active hedgers (Graham and Rogers, 2002).
Ownership structure and governance matter too. Firms with higher managerial stock ownership (raising delta) tend to hedge more. Firms with large outside blockholders who monitor management may impose hedging discipline. Industry also plays a role: commodity producers and firms in industries with natural price exposures systematically use more derivatives. Tufano's (1996) study of gold mining firms documents that hedging is more prevalent when managers hold more stock (high delta) and less when they hold more options (high vega). In sum, the decision to hedge more or less is not merely a firm-level policy choice; it is deeply personal and incentive-driven.
Question 4: If Firms Hedge Against Currency Risk Using Currency Derivatives, Should Investors Pay a Premium for the Equity Shares of These Firms?
This question goes to the heart of the Modigliani-Miller (MM) theorem as applied to risk management. In a world of frictionless, perfectly efficient capital markets, the answer is no: investors should not pay a premium for shares of firms that hedge currency risk, because shareholders can replicate the hedging strategy at the same cost themselves by taking offsetting positions in currency derivatives. Under MM, corporate financial policy — including the hedging decision — is irrelevant to firm value.
However, we do not live in a world of perfect capital markets. The corporate finance literature has identified several market imperfections that make corporate-level currency hedging potentially value-enhancing, and therefore potentially worthy of a valuation premium. First, financial distress costs: when currency movements can push a leveraged firm toward financial distress, hedging reduces this probability and the associated deadweight costs (legal fees, operational disruption, loss of key employees and customers). Since distress costs are borne disproportionately by shareholders and debtholders, hedging can genuinely increase the total value of the firm (Smith and Stulz, 1985). Second, tax convexity: if the firm's effective tax rate is a convex function of pre-tax income due to tax loss carryforwards or progressive tax structures, reducing income volatility through hedging reduces expected taxes and increases after-tax cash flows. Third, costly external financing: as Froot, Scharfstein, and Stein (1993) argue, if raising external capital is more expensive than using internal funds, hedging helps ensure that internally generated cash is available to fund positive-NPV projects, avoiding the underinvestment problem. This is particularly relevant for high-growth firms with significant investment opportunities.
Jorion (1990) documents that exchange-rate exposure is priced in U.S. equity markets — that is, the degree of foreign exposure is correlated with stock returns. This implies that investors do take note of currency risk and may discount shares of firms with higher unhedged exposure. Bartov and Bodnar (1994) find that lagged dollar changes are negatively related to the abnormal returns of firms with foreign currency impacts on their financials, consistent with exchange rate exposure being a risk factor. If currency exposure is systematic and priced, then eliminating it through hedging could reduce the required return on equity and increase share value.
The empirical evidence provides some support for a hedging premium. Allayannis and Weston (2001) find that U.S. multinationals that use currency derivatives trade at higher Tobin's Q ratios than non-hedgers, with the premium estimated at approximately 4.9%. This suggests investors do reward hedging firms with higher valuations.
However, counterarguments exist. If the currency risk being hedged is purely idiosyncratic (firm-specific and diversifiable), standard asset pricing theory implies it should not command a risk premium in equilibrium, and hedging it adds no value for a diversified investor. Moreover, hedging involves transaction costs, counterparty risk, and the risk of speculative losses if derivatives are used inappropriately. Geczy, Minton, and Schrand (1997) and McBrady, Mortal, and Schill (2010) also document that some firms use currency denomination strategically to exploit interest rate differentials, which may introduce new risks. On balance, when market imperfections are material and when hedging genuinely reduces costly volatility, a modest premium is theoretically justified and empirically observable.
Question 5: What Is Stock Price Synchronicity, and Why Is It More Prevalent in Less Developed Countries?
Stock price synchronicity refers to the tendency of individual stock prices within a market to move together — that is, the degree to which individual stock returns co-move with the overall market return. Morck, Yeung, and Yu (2000), whose work forms the basis of the readings in this course, measure synchronicity using the R² from a regression of individual stock returns on market and industry returns. A high R² implies that a large fraction of an individual stock's price variation is explained by market-wide movements, while a low R² implies that stock prices contain substantial firm-specific information. In practical terms, a highly synchronous market is one in which most stocks rise and fall together, while a market with low synchronicity features stocks with diverse, idiosyncratic price movements that reflect firm-specific information.
The foundational empirical finding is striking: emerging and developing economies exhibit dramatically higher stock price synchronicity than developed economies. For example, in China, Malaysia, and Poland, over 80% of stocks frequently move in the same direction in a given week. In contrast, developed markets like the United States, Denmark, and Ireland rarely see more than 57% of stocks moving together. The R² measure corroborates this: the five lowest R²s are for the U.S., Ireland, Canada, the UK, and Australia, while the five highest are for Poland, China, Malaysia, Taiwan, and Turkey.
Morck et al. consider three explanations for this pattern. The first is that fundamentals in less developed economies are more correlated — that is, if these economies are small and undiversified, with many firms operating in similar industries or depending on a few large companies, then firm-level earnings may naturally co-move. However, the authors find that while fundamentals co-movement matters, controlling for it does not eliminate the synchronicity-income relationship, so it cannot be the full explanation.
The second explanation involves poor property rights protection. In countries with weak rule of law, political events, rumors, and government policy changes can cause market-wide swings unrelated to fundamentals. Poor property rights also make informed risk arbitrage unattractive — sophisticated investors are unwilling to invest resources in gathering firm-specific information if they cannot reliably profit from it without expropriation risk. As a result, stock prices fail to impound firm-specific information, and market-wide noise trading dominates, increasing synchronicity.
The third explanation involves the protection of public investors from corporate insiders. In countries where controlling shareholders can engage in intercorporate income shifting — tunneling value out of listed companies to related parties — firm-specific accounting information becomes less useful or reliable for outside investors. Rational arbitrageurs therefore do not trade on it, reducing the degree to which firm-specific information is capitalized into stock prices, and increasing synchronicity. Morck et al. find that in developed economies, stronger legal protection of public shareholders from corporate insiders is associated with more firm-specific information in stock prices.
The key conclusion is that high stock price synchronicity in less developed markets is not merely a reflection of genuinely correlated firm fundamentals, but reflects weak institutions, poor property rights, and inadequate investor protection that prevent firm-specific information from being impounded into prices — leaving stock markets informationally inefficient.
Question 6: Does Stock Price Synchronicity in Foreign Countries Motivate Firms to Cross-List in Advanced Capital Markets? If Yes, Why?
Yes, stock price synchronicity in the home country is a significant motivation for firms from less developed markets to cross-list their shares in advanced capital markets such as the NYSE, LSE, or NASDAQ. The mechanism is straightforward but profound: when a firm's home-country stock market is highly synchronous — meaning individual stock prices fail to impound firm-specific information and instead move in lockstep with the broader market — the firm's shares are effectively mispriced, failing to reflect the firm's unique fundamentals, growth opportunities, and managerial quality. Cross-listing in an advanced market with a richer information environment can break this synchronicity and allow the firm's stock price to become more informative.
The theoretical underpinning connects directly to the work of Morck, Yeung, and Yu (2000), who show that low institutional quality, poor property rights protection, and weak investor protection — precisely the conditions that generate high synchronicity — make it difficult for firm-specific information to be capitalized into prices. A firm that cross-lists in the U.S. subjects itself to the SEC's rigorous disclosure requirements, U.S. GAAP accounting standards (or reconciliation thereto), and intense scrutiny from sophisticated analysts and institutional investors who specialize in uncovering and trading on firm-specific information. This enriches the information environment around the firm.
Chan and Hameed (2006), studying analyst coverage in emerging markets, find that in these settings — characterized by high synchronicity — analyst coverage actually increases market-wide information incorporation rather than firm-specific information, counter to the usual assumption. Cross-listing in developed markets attracts Western analysts who specialize in firm-level due diligence, increasing the firm-specific component of the stock price.
Doukas and Wang (2014), studying Chinese firms that cross-list via B-share listings, explicitly hypothesize and find that firms with foreign listings have less synchronous (more informative) stock prices than purely domestically listed firms. Cross-listing creates a bonding mechanism: by subjecting themselves to the regulatory and disclosure requirements of the host market, controlling shareholders commit to limiting expropriation of minority shareholders through tunneling. This reduces the noise in financial reports and makes firm-specific information more credible and useful to investors. As a result, informed arbitrageurs are more willing to gather and trade on firm-specific information, increasing its capitalization into prices and lowering synchronicity.
The broader bonding hypothesis (Coffee, 1999, 2002; Stulz, 1999) provides additional rationale. Firms cross-list in advanced markets to voluntarily bond themselves to higher governance standards, thereby resolving agency conflicts between controlling and minority shareholders. To the extent that high synchronicity in the home market reflects poor governance and limited information disclosure, cross-listing attacks the root cause of synchronicity rather than merely its symptom.
The practical implication is that firms from high-synchronicity markets can enjoy a double benefit from cross-listing: a direct valuation premium from improved governance and lower cost of capital, and an indirect gain from more accurate stock pricing that better reflects firm fundamentals. Doidge, Karolyi, and Stulz (2004) document that foreign firms cross-listed in the U.S. trade at substantially higher Tobin's Q ratios than comparable non-cross-listed firms, with the premium larger for firms from countries with weaker investor protection — precisely the conditions that generate high synchronicity.
Question 7: CEO Envy and International Mergers & Acquisitions: Hypotheses and Research Design
CEO envy, as a behavioral motivation for corporate acquisitions, refers to the tendency of executives to pursue mergers and acquisitions partly in response to the compensation packages, firm size, or prestige of peers and competitors, rather than purely to maximize shareholder value. While the formal term "CEO envy" may not appear as prominently in the classic M&A literature as hubris (Roll, 1986), the underlying idea is closely related to managerial incentives theories of corporate expansion and connects naturally to research on relative performance evaluation and social comparison in executive behavior.
The hypothesis rooted in envy or social comparison would posit that CEOs whose compensation, firm size, or market prestige falls below that of their industry peers are more likely to pursue acquisitions — including international ones — to close this gap. A related variant is that CEOs of high-compensation firms may serve as "models" whose compensation and empire size are emulated by peers, generating acquisition waves. This is distinct from value-maximizing motives because the acquisitions are driven by managerial utility maximization (ego, status, compensation tied to firm size) rather than synergy creation.
To formalize the envy hypothesis in the context of international M&A, one could posit: H1 — CEOs whose total compensation or firm size ranks below the industry median are more likely to engage in cross-border acquisitions than their higher-ranked peers; H2 — the probability of an international acquisition announcement increases with the relative compensation gap between a CEO and a defined peer group; H3 — acquisitions motivated by CEO envy generate negative abnormal returns for acquirers, reflecting value destruction.
The research design would require several steps. First, construct a measure of CEO "envy" or social comparison. One approach is to calculate the ratio of CEO compensation relative to industry peer compensation (Bizjak, Lemmon, and Naveen, 2008, document that firms benchmark CEO pay against peers). A CEO whose pay or firm size is below the peer median would be scored as relatively "envious." Second, use an event-study methodology to measure cumulative abnormal returns (CARs) around the announcement of international acquisitions for subgroups of high-envy vs. low-envy CEOs. Third, run multivariate regressions controlling for standard deal and firm characteristics (firm size, Tobin's Q, leverage, prior performance, target country, industry) to isolate the incremental effect of relative CEO standing on both the decision to acquire internationally and on acquirer returns.
One could also exploit compensation committee decisions and peer group construction (firms are required to disclose peer groups in proxy statements) as a natural instrument. Doukas (1995) documents that bidder returns are significantly and inversely related to free cash flow for low-Q bidders, consistent with empire-building motives. This framework can be extended to test whether relative CEO standing explains cross-sectional variation in acquirer returns beyond Q and cash flow. A DiD approach around exogenous shocks to industry peer compensation (e.g., a major competitor's CEO receiving a large raise) could further identify causal effects.
Question 8: CEO Hubris and International Mergers & Acquisitions: Hypotheses and Research Design
CEO hubris, formalized by Roll (1986), refers to the tendency of overconfident CEOs to overestimate their own ability to manage acquired firms and to overestimate the synergies available from a merger. Roll's hubris hypothesis predicts that acquirers pay too much for targets — the acquirer's stock price declines at announcement while the target's rises, and on net, combined value is not created. In the context of international M&A, hubris is particularly relevant because cross-border acquisitions involve greater complexity, information asymmetry, and cultural distance, giving overconfident managers more room to miscalibrate value.
The hubris hypothesis applied to international acquisitions generates several testable predictions. H1 — Acquirers in cross-border deals experience significantly negative abnormal returns at announcement, consistent with overbidding driven by managerial overconfidence; H2 — CEOs with a history of prior successful acquisitions (who may attribute success to skill rather than luck) are more prone to hubris and overpay in subsequent deals; H3 — Hubris-motivated acquisitions targeting firms in distant countries (greater geographic, cultural, or institutional distance) are associated with worse acquirer returns, as overconfidence amplifies the misjudgment of integration costs.
Measuring hubris empirically requires a proxy for CEO overconfidence. The literature has used several: (1) Option moneyness — Malmendier and Tate (2005, 2008) use the decision of CEOs to hold deep in-the-money options past their rational expiration as a measure of overconfidence, since a rational, risk-averse executive would exercise early. (2) Press characterization — counting the frequency of terms like "confident" or "optimistic" vs. "cautious" in newspaper profiles of the CEO. (3) Insider trading — CEOs who systematically buy shares before announcements but do not sell signal overconfidence about future prospects. (4) Forecasting accuracy — comparing CEO earnings forecasts to actual outcomes.
The research design for testing the hubris hypothesis in international M&A would proceed as follows. First, collect a sample of cross-border acquisition announcements (e.g., from the SDC Platinum database) and measure short-window CARs (e.g., −1 to +1 around announcement). Second, classify acquirers by hubris proxy (e.g., option-holding behavior from ExecuComp data). Third, estimate OLS and 2SLS regressions of acquirer CARs on the hubris measure, controlling for deal type (related vs. unrelated, target country governance quality), firm characteristics (Tobin's Q, leverage, size), and the CEO's prior track record. Doukas and Travlos (1988) show that shareholders of MNCs not already operating in the target country earn significant positive abnormal returns, while those expanding into markets where they already operate earn insignificant or negative returns — the latter consistent with hubris-driven empire expansion. Doukas (1995) further shows that bidder returns are inversely related to Tobin's Q for low-Q bidders, consistent with agency-driven overinvestment rather than value-maximizing behavior. A natural extension would be to show that overconfident CEOs (by proxy) systematically generate worse outcomes in cross-border acquisitions, and that their boards fail to discipline this behavior due to weak governance structures.
Question 9: Hypothesis Development: The Link Between Brand Value/Recognition and Firm Value
Brand value and brand recognition represent intangible assets that are difficult to replicate, legally protected through trademarks, and capable of generating sustained pricing power, customer loyalty, and barriers to entry. The link between brand value and firm value is grounded in theories of intangible capital and the internalization theory of multinational firms, and generating testable hypotheses requires careful articulation of the mechanism, direction, and boundary conditions of this relationship.
The primary hypothesis is: H1 — Firms with higher brand value, as measured by commercially recognized brand valuation services (Interbrand, Kantar BrandZ, or Brand Finance) or advertising expenditure intensity as a proxy, exhibit higher firm valuations as measured by Tobin's Q, even after controlling for financial leverage, firm size, profitability, and industry effects. The theoretical mechanism follows from the internalization theory developed by Caves (1971), Buckley and Casson (1976), and formally tested by Morck and Yeung (1991). Intangible assets like brand recognition behave like public goods within the firm: their value increases as the firm expands geographically and market-wise, because the same brand can be deployed at near-zero marginal cost across multiple markets. This public-good property justifies both a valuation premium for brand-intensive firms and explains why such firms are more likely to expand internationally.
A second hypothesis addresses international expansion: H2 — The positive relationship between brand value and firm value is stronger for firms with greater geographic diversification (measured by foreign sales as a fraction of total sales or the number of countries of operation). Morck and Yeung (1991), using Tobin's Q as the dependent variable and measures of multinationality interacted with R&D and advertising intensity, find that the impact of advertising spending on q is enhanced by multinationality. This supports the idea that brand assets generate more value when deployed across more markets.
H3 — The brand-value premium is more pronounced in industries with higher advertising intensity and lower physical capital requirements (e.g., consumer goods, luxury products, technology), where intangible capital constitutes a larger fraction of total firm value, compared to capital-intensive industries.
H4 — Increases in brand value rankings (measured as year-over-year improvements in Interbrand scores) are associated with contemporaneous positive abnormal stock returns, suggesting that brand-value improvements are incrementally informative to markets beyond what is captured in traditional financial metrics.
The research design would involve an event-study and panel regression approach.
